Insanity and Raj'a: Words vs Actions, and Disliked Acts
Rulings on insanity and disliked acts in raj'a

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the preferred Hanafi view on raj'a by an insane husband and identify actions that are disliked during the 'iddah.

Core Idea
An insane husband's raj'a is valid by action but not by words, and kissing or seeing her uncovered without desire is disliked.

The passage tackles two more subsidiary issues: the effect of insanity on the validity of raj'a, and etiquette during the waiting period. On insanity, there is a difference of opinion, but the preferred view (al-Bazzazi) distinguishes between actions and words. On etiquette, Abu Yusuf warns against intimate acts without desire, as they may inadvertently trigger a full raj'a.

Guided Path
1
Understand the scenario of insanity
A man divorces his wife revocably and then becomes insane before the 'iddah ends.
Now he cannot give valid consent.
2
Identify the three views
Some say both word and action invalid; some say both valid; some say action valid but word invalid.
The text mentions these without naming specific scholars.
3
Note the preferred view
Al-Bazzazi adopted the view that action is valid, word is not.
Reason: an insane person is accountable for actions but not for his utterances.
4
Extract the disliked acts
Abu Yusuf rules it is makruh to kiss or touch without desire and to see her uncovered.
To avoid involuntary desire leading to raj'a.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطلاق باب الرجعة براجعتك أو راجعت امرأتي وبما يوجب حرمة المصاهرة والإشهاد مندوب عليها ولو قال بعد العدة راجعتك فيها فصدقته تصح وإلا لا كراجعتك فقالت مجيبة مضت عدتي أبو حنيفة سوى بينهما في الثبوت. وفي شرح الطحاوي : لو قال أبطلت رجعتي أو لا رجعة لي عليك لا تبطل الرجعة انتهى. وفي القنية : أجاز مراجعة الفضولي صح ويصير مراجعاً بوقوع بصره على فرجها بشهوة من غير قصد المراجعة انتهى واختلف فيما إذا طلق رجعياً ثم جن ثم راجع بقول أو فعل فقيل لا يصح بهما، وقيل يصح بهما، وقيل تصح بالفعل دون القول كما في القنية من غير ترجيح، واقتصر البزازي على الأخير ولعله الراجح لما عرف أنه مؤاخذ بأفعاله دون أقواله وعلله في الصيرفية بأنه استدامة النكاح والرضا ليس بشرط ولهذا لو أكره على الرجعة بالفعل يصح انتهى. وفي الحاوي القدسي : وإذا راجعها بقبلة أو لمس فالأفضل أن يراجعها بالإشهاد ثانياً ا هـ . وفي المحيط : قال أبو يوسف: ويكره التقبيل واللمس بغير شهوة إذا لم يرد الرجعة ويكره أن يراها متجردة لأنه لا يأمن من أن يشتهي فيصير به مراجعاً ثم يحتاج إلى الطلاق فيؤدي إلى تطويل العدة انتهى.
Chapter of Divorce, Section on Revocable Divorce. By 'I take you back' or 'I take back my wife' and by what necessitates the prohibition of affinity, and witnessing is recommended upon it. And if he says after the waiting period: 'I took you back during it' and she confirms him, it is valid; otherwise not. Like 'I take you back' and she responds: 'My waiting period has passed.' Abu Hanifa equated between them in affirmation. And in Sharh al-Tahawi: If he says: 'I annulled my revocable return' or 'I have no revocable return over you,' the revocable return is not annulled. End. And in al-Qunyah: He permitted the revocable return by a disinterested third party, valid, and he becomes a retractor by his sight falling on her private part with desire without intending the revocable return. End. And there is disagreement about when he divorces revocably, then becomes insane, then performs revocable return by word or action. Some said it is not valid by either; some said valid by both; some said valid by action not by word, as in al-Qunyah without preference. al-Bazzazi restricted to the latter, and perhaps it is the preferred view because it is known that he is held accountable for his actions, not his words. And he explained in al-Sayrafiyyah that it is continuation of the marriage, and consent is not a condition; hence if he is coerced into revocable return by action, it is valid. End. And in al-Hawi al-Qudsi: If he takes her back by a kiss or touch, it is better that he takes her back with witnessing a second time. End. And in al-Muhit: Abu Yusuf said: It is disliked to kiss and touch without desire if he does not intend the revocable return, and it is disliked to see her uncovered because he is not safe from desiring and thus becoming a retractor, then needing divorce, leading to lengthening of the waiting period. End.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage provides several subsidiary rulings (فروع) related to revocable divorce. First, it reiterates the verbal expressions that constitute raj'a. Then it discusses the case where a man claims to have performed raj'a during the 'iddah but after its expiry: his claim is accepted only if the woman confirms it, otherwise her denial is taken (without oath). This applies to both 'I take you back' and her claim 'my 'iddah has passed' — Abu Hanifa treats them equally. Next, it covers the ruling of annulling raj'a: merely saying 'I annul my raj'a' has no effect. Then, it permits a third party (فضولي) to perform the raj'a on behalf of the husband, and even a glance with desire can constitute raj'a without explicit intent. The passage then discusses a case where a man becomes insane after divorce but before performing raj'a: there is a difference of opinion on whether his subsequent raj'a by word or action is valid. The preferred view (as chosen by al-Bazzazi) is that action is valid but words are not, because actions follow intent and he is accountable for actions even if insane for words. Coercion into action is also valid. Finally, Abu Yusuf states it is disliked to kiss or touch without desire if not intending raj'a, and to see her uncovered to avoid involuntary desire.

Key Terms
الجنون (al-junun): Insanity or loss of mental faculties that negates legal responsibility for certain acts
المكروه (al-makruh): Disliked; an act that is blameworthy but not sinful enough to be prohibited

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Bazzazi's preferred view, what is the ruling on raj'a by an insane husband?
A) Valid only by words
B) Valid only by action
C) Valid by both
D) Valid by neither

Key Takeaway
An insane husband's raj'a is valid by action but not by words, and intimate acts without desire are disliked to avoid accidental raj'a.

Recommendation of Witnessing (Ishhad) in Revocable Divorce
Explanation of the ruling on witnessing in revocable divorce, clarifying it is recommended not obligatory

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on witnessing in revocable divorce, explain the proof from Quran 65:2, and distinguish between sunni and bid'i raj'a.

Core Idea
Witnessing in revocable divorce is recommended (mandub), not obligatory, based on the structure of Quran 65:2.

In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi ruling on whether calling witnesses is required when a husband takes back his wife during the waiting period (iddah). The passage clarifies that ishhad is only recommended, and the proof lies in the very wording of the Quranic verse that commands it. Understanding this distinction prevents misunderstanding the legal effect of omitting witnesses and highlights the Hanafi usul principle that a single imperative cannot carry two different legal weights in the same context.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act under discussion? 'Ishhad' in the context of revocable divorce (raj'a). What is the hukm? The text states it is 'mandub' (recommended).
2
Locate the dalil
Find the Quranic proof: Surah al-Talaq 65:2, which commands witnessing. Note that the verse also commands holding (imsak) and separation (tafriq).
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Analyze how the imperative 'ashidu' is interpreted. The key is that it follows two commands; if it were obligatory in one and recommended in the other, the same word would have two meanings in one verse, which is impermissible in Hanafi usul.
This is the core of the proof: consistency demands that the imperative be interpreted as recommendation in both contexts.
4
Distinguish sunni from bid'i raj'a
Identify the two types: sunni (commendable) requires verbal statement, witnessing, and informing the wife; bid'i (innovation) omits one or both of these. The passage notes that omission violates the sunna but does not invalidate the raj'a.
5
Note the practical consequence
If witnesses are not called, the raj'a remains valid (sahih) but the husband has acted contrary to the sunna. This is the thamarah al-khilaf of the ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (والإشهاد مندوب عليها أي على الرجعة وفاقاً لمالك والشافعي على الأظهر خروجاً من خلاف عند الشافعي ومالك وإن كان ضعيفاً وعملاً بقوله تعالى واشهدوا ذوي عدل منكم [الطلاق: ٢] بناء على أنه للندب بدليل أنه أمر بالإشهاد بعد الأمر بشيئين : الامساك والمفارقة : فلو كان الإشهاد واجباً في الرجعة مندوباً في المفارقة للزم استعمال اللفظ الواحد في حقيقته ومجازه وهو ممنوع عندنا واحترازاً عن التجاحد وعن الوقوف في مواضع التهم. وأشار المصنف رحمه الله إلى أن الرجعة على ضربين : سني وبدعي. فالسني أن يراجعها بالقول ويشهد على رجعتها ويعلمها ولو راجعها بالقول ولم يشهد أو أشهد ولم يعلمها كان مخالفاً للسنة كما في شرح الطحاوي
His statement: 'And witnessing is recommended upon it,' i.e., upon the revocable divorce, in agreement with Malik and al-Shafi'i according to the most apparent view, so as to exit from disagreement with al-Shafi'i and Malik even though it is weak, and acting on the Almighty's statement: 'And call to witness two just persons among you' (65:2) based on it being for recommendation because He commanded witnessing after commanding two things: holding (the wife) and separation. If witnessing were obligatory in revocable divorce and recommended in separation, it would necessitate using one word in its true and metaphorical sense, which is prohibited according to us. And as a precaution against denial and against falling into places of suspicion. And the author, may Allah have mercy on him, indicated that revocable divorce types: sunni and bid'i. The sunni is that he takes her back verbally, witnesses his revocable return, and informs her. If he takes her back verbally but does not witness or witnesses but does not inform her, he has violated the sunna, as in Sharh al-Tahawi.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage is a gloss (قوله) on the matn's statement 'والإشهاد مندوب عليها' (witnessing is recommended upon it). It explains that the Hanafi school considers witnessing (إشهاد) in revocable divorce as recommended (مندوب), not obligatory (واجب). This is in agreement with the most apparent view of Malik and al-Shafi'i, to avoid conflict with them, though the disagreement is weak. The proof is from the Quranic verse (65:2): 'And call to witness two just persons among you.' The reasoning: the verse commands witnessing after commanding holding (إمساك) and separation (مفارقة). If witnessing were obligatory for raj'a but recommended for separation, the same word (shahada) would have to be used in its real and metaphorical sense in the same verse, which is prohibited in Hanafi usul. Therefore, it must be for recommendation in both. Additionally, witnessing serves to prevent denial and suspicion. The passage then distinguishes between sunni (commendable) and bid'i (innovation) raj'a. The sunni way is to verbally take her back, have witnesses, and inform her; omitting any of these is against the sunna.

Key Terms
الإشهاد (al-ishhad): The act of calling two just Muslim witnesses to observe the revocable divorce
سني وبدعي (sunni wa bid'i): Two types of revocable divorce: sunni (following prophetic practice with witnessing and notification) and bid'i (innovation, e.g., without witnesses or notification).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school consider witnessing (ishhad) in revocable divorce as recommended rather than obligatory?
A) Because the command 'ashidu' is absolute and not specific to raj'a
B) Because the verse commands it after both 'imsak' and 'tafriq', so using the same word for obligation in one and recommendation in another would violate the rule against using a word in both real and metaphorical senses
C) Because later scholars decided it is not required
D) Because witnesses are difficult to find

Key Takeaway
Witnessing in revocable divorce is recommended to avoid denial and suspicion, but its omission does not invalidate the marriage resumption.

Claiming Revocable Divorce After Expiry of 'Iddah
Ruling on claim of raj'a after 'iddah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a husband's claim of raj'a after the 'iddah expires and apply the qiyas to the agent's sale analogy.

Core Idea
A past act the husband cannot now initiate is not accepted over the wife's denial.

In Hanafi fiqh, the ability to initiate an act matters when evaluating claims about that act. When a husband claims he performed raj'a during the 'iddah after the 'iddah has expired, he is reporting a past event he cannot recreate. The wife's denial is therefore given weight without oath. This principle is clarified through a parallel with a sales agent whose authority has been revoked.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario
The husband claims after the 'iddah ends that he pronounced raj'a during the 'iddah. The wife either confirms or denies this.
Raj'a must be done within the 'iddah; after expiry, it is no longer possible.
2
State the Ruling
If the wife confirms, the raj'a is valid (the marriage revives). If she denies, the raj'a is invalid and her word is taken without oath.
The wife's denial is accepted 'without oath' as in the six matters.
3
Understand the Reasoning
The husband is reporting an event he cannot now initiate. The one who denies (wife) is believed because she is in possession of the fact (the condition of the marriage).
This parallels the rule for 'six matters' where a woman's word is taken.
4
Apply the Qiyas
Analogous to a sales agent: if he claims to have sold goods before being dismissed, he is believed; if he claims after dismissal, he is not believed. The inability to initiate now is the common effective cause.
The qiyas reinforces that authority to act at the time of the claim is key.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ولو قال بعد العدة راجعتك فيها فصدقته تصح وإلا لا أي وإن لم تصدقه لا تصح الرجعة لأنه أخبر عن شيء لا يملك إنشاءه في الحال وهي تنكره فكان القول لها من غير يمين لما عرف في الأشياء الستة، وإن صدقته صحت لأن النكاح يثبت بتصادقهما فالرجعة أولى ونظيره الوكيل بالبيع، إذا قال قبل العزل كنت بعته من فلان صدق بخلاف ما لو قاله بعد العزل كذا في الكافي. وفي تلخيص راجعتك بكذا
His statement: And if he says after the waiting period: 'I took you back during it' and she confirms him, it is valid; otherwise not, i.e., if she does not confirm him, the revocable return is not valid because he informed about something he cannot initiate now, and she denies it; so the word is given to her without oath, as known in the six matters. And if she confirms him, it is valid because marriage is established by their mutual confirmation; even more so for revocable return. And its analogy is the agent in sale: if he says before being dismissed: 'I sold it to so-and-so,' he is believed, contrary to if he says it after being dismissed. So in al-Kafi. And in Talkhis: 'I take you back with such.'
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling when a husband claims after the expiry of the waiting period that he performed the raj'a during the 'iddah. According to the Hanafi school, his claim is accepted only if the wife confirms it. If she denies, her word is taken without requiring her to take an oath, because he is reporting an event that cannot be initiated now, and she is denying it. This is similar to six other matters where the woman's word is accepted (the 'six matters' are listed in a later gloss). The analogy given is that of a sales agent: if he claims before being dismissed that he sold the item, he is believed; if he claims after dismissal, he is not believed. The passage concludes with a reference to Talkhis (a work by al-Kafi).

Key Terms
الأشياء الستة (al-ashya' al-sittah): Six matters where the woman's word is accepted without oath; specified in later gloss as raj'a, nikah, fay', istilad, raqiq, nasab, wala', hadd, li'an (actually nine).
قياس (qiyas): Analogical reasoning; here the analogy between husband's claim of raj'a and agent's claim of sale.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi ruling on a husband's claim of raj'a after the 'iddah expires, when is his claim valid?
A) Always, because he is the husband
B) Only if he has witnesses
C) Only if the wife confirms
D) Only if he takes an oath

Key Takeaway
A husband's claim of an act he can no longer perform is not accepted against the wife's denial, analogized to a dismissed agent's claim.

Clarifying the Dislike in Raj'a by Action: Tanzih vs. Tahrim
Clarification that the dislike (karahah) for performing raj'a by action is only tanzihi (reprehensible but not prohibited)

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between karahah tanzihiyyah and tahrimiyyah in the context of raj'a by action and apply the correct Hanafi ruling.

Core Idea
The dislike for raj'a by action is tanzihi, not tahrimi, because revocable divorce does not prohibit intercourse.

In this passage, al-Ramli refines the earlier statement that raj'a by action is 'disliked'. He clarifies that the dislike is only tanzihi, meaning it is better to avoid but not sinful. This refinement is supported by the legal fact that revocable divorce does not prohibit intercourse, which would make intercourse permissible. Thus, the dislike cannot be at the level of prohibition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its initial ruling
The act is performing raj'a (taking back a revocably divorced wife) by action (e.g., intercourse). It is initially described as 'disliked'.
2
Determine the type of karahah
Al-Ramli states that the dislike is tanzihi, as indicated by the later statement that revocable divorce does not prohibit intercourse. If intercourse is permissible, the dislike cannot be tahrimi.
Tanzihi = better to avoid but not sinful; Tahrimi = prohibited and sinful.
3
Understand the supporting evidence
The passage cites two proofs: (1) the author will later say revocable divorce does not prohibit intercourse, implying action is permissible; (2) the text in al-Fath recommends verbal raj'a, implying action is permissible but not best.
The permissibility of intercourse is the key evidence.
4
Note the recommended practice
It is recommended (mustahab) to perform raj'a verbally, not by action. This reinforces that action is not prohibited but is not the preferred method.
The ruling is not absolute; there is a better alternative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (فإنها مكروهة بالفعل قال الرملي : الظاهر أن الكراهية هنا تنزيهية كما يشير إليه كلام هذا الشارح الآتي في شرح قوله والطلاق الرجعي لا يحرم الوطء ا هـ . قلت : ويدل عليه قوله في الفتح والمستحب أن يراجعها بالقول .
His statement: 'For it is disliked through action.' al-Ramli said: The apparent meaning is that the dislike here is tanzihi (prohibitive disregard), as indicated by the words of this commentator later in explaining his statement: 'Revocable divorce does not prohibit intercourse.' End. I say: It is supported by his statement in al-Fath: 'And it is recommended that he take her back verbally.'
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This short gloss refines the earlier statement that performing raj'a by action is 'disliked' (مكروه). The commentator al-Ramli clarifies that this dislike is only tanzihi (i.e., it is better to avoid it but it is not forbidden). The evidence he gives is that later the author will state that revocable divorce does not prohibit intercourse, implying that intercourse (an action) is permissible, so the dislike cannot be tahrimi. The commenter then adds that in al-Fath it says it is recommended to do raj'a verbally, which implies the action is permissible but not the best practice.

Key Terms
كراهة تنزيهية (karahah tanzihiyyah): A disliked act that is better to avoid but does not incur sin; it is not prohibited.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What type of dislike (karahah) is attributed to performing raj'a by action according to al-Ramli?
A) Tahrimiyyah (prohibitive dislike)
B) Tanzihiyyah (mere reprehensible)
C) It is not disliked at all
D) It is forbidden (haram)

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, performing raj'a by action is makruh tanzih, meaning it is valid and not sinful but better avoided in favor of verbal declaration.

The Six (or Nine) Matters Where the Woman's Word is Accepted
List of nine matters where the woman's word is accepted, with intra-madhhab refinement on oath requirement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the nine family-law matters where the Hanafi school accepts a woman's statement, and state which matters require an oath according to the operative fatwa.

Core Idea
The woman is believed in nine specific matters, but the later fatwa requires her oath in the first seven.

This passage presents a distinctive Hanafi list of cases where a woman's word is taken as true without external evidence. Originally enumerated as six matters, the list is here expanded to nine. However, the later fatwa from Abu Yusuf and Muhammad restricts automatic acceptance by requiring the woman to take an oath in the first seven matters. Understanding this list and its intra-madhhab development is essential for correctly applying the school's procedural rules in family disputes.

Guided Path
1
Recite the nine matters in order
Memorize the list: raj'a, nikah, fay', istilad, riqq, nasab, wala', hadd, li'an. These are the domains where the woman's statement is accepted due to her exclusive knowledge.
The list is expanded from six to nine; note which three were added.
2
Distinguish between the first seven and last two
The fatwa (mufta bihi) requires the woman to take an oath only for the first seven matters. The last two (hadd and li'an) remain without an oath requirement.
The earlier view (without oath) is marjuh; the later fatwa of the two is relied upon.
3
Identify the intra-Hanafi difference
The difference is between the earlier transmission (no oath needed) and the view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (oath required for first seven). The fatwa adopts the latter.
This shows how later authorities (mutakhkhirin) can settle differences.
4
Apply the rule to a scenario
Given a dispute, determine which matter it falls under, then decide if an oath is required. For example, a claim of revocable divorce (raj'a) falls in first seven → oath needed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (لما عرف في الأشياء الستة بل التسعة وهي الرجعة والنكاح والفيء والاستيلاد والرق والنسب والولاء والحد واللعان. لكن الفتوى على التحليف في السبعة الأولى وهو قولهما كما سيأتي
His statement: 'As known in the six matters' or rather nine, which are: revocable return, marriage, return (fay'), attribution of parentage (istilad), slavery, lineage, patronage, hudud punishment, and imprecation (li'an). But the fatwa is upon requiring an oath in the first seven, and that is the view of the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) as will come.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This gloss identifies the 'six matters' (often referred to in earlier text) as actually nine matters where the woman's word is accepted without oath (originally six, but here expanded to nine). The list includes: raj'a, nikah, fay' (return of a slave or wife?), istilad (attribution of parentage), slavery (whether she is free or slave), nasab (lineage), wala' (patronage), hadd (penal punishment), and li'an (imprecation). However, the fatwa (operative ruling) is that an oath is required in the first seven of these, according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). This overrides the earlier statement that no oath is required. The passage promises further details.

Key Terms
الأشياء الستة (al-ashya' al-sittah): A set of matters where the woman's word is accepted; originally six, but here extended to nine.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the fatwa (operative opinion) mentioned, how many of the nine matters require the woman to take an oath?
A) All nine
B) The first seven
C) Only raj'a and nikah
D) None, she is always believed without oath

Key Takeaway
The woman's word is accepted in nine specific matters, but the operative fatwa requires her to take an oath in the first seven to ensure credibility.

The Recommendation of Witnessing in Revocation (Raj'ah)
Commentary on the ruling that witnessing is recommended, not obligatory

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on ishhad during raj'ah and explain the proof from the Quranic verse and Hanafi usul.

Core Idea
Calling witnesses upon revocation is recommended, not obligatory, based on interpreting the Quranic imperative as a recommendation.

In this passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq, the author addresses whether a husband must call two just witnesses when revoking a revocable divorce. The Hanafi school holds that witnessing is recommended (mandub) but not required for the validity of the revocation. The proof draws on the Quranic verse (al-Talaq 65:2) and a usul principle that the same word cannot be used in two contradictory senses. The passage also distinguishes between sunni (proper) and bid'i (improper) forms of revocation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
Read the passage to determine the legal status (hukm) of ishhad upon raj'ah. The text says 'al-ishhadu mandub' — it is recommended, not obligatory.
Mandub is equivalent to sunnah muakkadah in Hanafi terminology.
2
Locate the dalil
Find the primary evidence used: the Quranic verse 'w-ashhidu dhawyi 'adlin minkum' (al-Talaq 65:2). Observe that it is a command (amr) in the imperative form.
The command is qat'i in transmission but zanni in indication.
3
Analyse the wajh al-dalalah
Understand why the command is interpreted as recommendation rather than obligation. The passage argues that the command to witness follows two prior commands (retention and separation) which themselves are not obligatory. If witnessing were obligatory in one context and recommended in another, the same word would be used in two different senses — a violation of Hanafi usul.
This is a classic application of the principle that al-amr yufidu al-wujub unless there is a qarinah (indication) to the contrary.
4
Note cross-madhhab agreement
The passage states that this view is in agreement with the most apparent opinion of Malik and al-Shafi'i, thereby avoiding unnecessary conflict.
The text mentions 'khurujan min khilaf' — exiting from disagreement.
5
Distinguish sunni and bid'i raj'ah
The author indicates that raj'ah can be sunni (proper) or bid'i (improper). Sunni requires three elements: uttering the revocation, calling two witnesses, and informing the wife. Omitting any of these makes it bid'i but does not invalidate the revocation itself.
This distinction impacts the reward and ideal practice, not the validity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And witnessing is recommended upon it. His statement: (And witnessing is recommended upon it, i.e., upon the revocation, in agreement with Malik and al-Shafi'i according to the most apparent view, to exit from the difference with al-Shafi'i and Malik even if it is weak, and acting upon His statement, the Exalted: 'And call to witness two just persons among you' [al-Talaq: 2] based on it being for recommendation, evidenced by the fact that He commanded witnessing after commanding two things: retention and separation. For if witnessing were obligatory in revocation and recommended in separation, that would necessitate using a single word in its reality and metaphor, which is prohibited according to us, and as a precaution against denial and against falling into situations of suspicion. And the author, may Allah have mercy on him, indicated that revocation is of two types: sunni and bid'i. The sunni is that he revokes her by statement and calls witnesses to his revocation and informs her. If he revoked by statement and did not call witnesses, or called witnesses and did not inform her, he has violated the sunnah, as in Sharh al-Tahawi.

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof on the legal status of witnessing (ishhad) upon revocation (raj'ah). The author establishes that ishhad is mandub (recommended), not wajib. He provides proof from the Quranic verse (al-Talaq 65:2) which commands witnessing, but argues that the context shows it is a recommendation because it comes after the command for retention or separation. If it were wajib in raj'ah and mandub in separation, the same word would be used in two different senses, which is impermissible in Hanafi usul. He also mentions that the revocation is either sunni (proper, with statement, witnessing, and informing the wife) or bid'i (improper if any of these is missing). The passage cites agreement with Malik and al-Shafi'i on the recommendation, though their own schools may differ internally.

Key Terms
إشهاد (ishhad): Calling two just witnesses to attest to the revocation
رجعة (raj'ah): The husband's revoking a revocable divorce (talaq raj'i) during the waiting period
مندوب (mandub): Recommended act, rewarded but not sinful to omit

Post-'Iddah Revocation: When the Husband's Claim Is Accepted
Ruling on the validity of a claimed revocation after the 'iddah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a husband claims revocation after the 'iddah and the wife confirms or denies, and apply the analogy of the agent to explain the underlying principle.

Core Idea
A post-'iddah claim of revocation is valid only if the wife confirms it.

This passage addresses a nuanced scenario: a husband says he revoked his wife during the 'iddah but only announces it after the 'iddah has ended. The ruling hinges on whether the wife confirms the claim. The Hanafi school uses a key analogy to the agent's authority to sell and a broader principle about six (or nine) matters where the word of the one denying a past event is accepted. Understanding this teaches us when a person is believed about a past act.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The husband says 'I revoked you during the 'iddah' but makes this statement after the 'iddah has ended. The wife either confirms or denies.
The key fact: the statement is made after the period when revocation could actually be effected.
2
State the ruling
If the wife confirms the claim, the revocation is valid. If she denies, the revocation is invalid.
The text says 'fasaddaqatuhu tasihhu wa-illa la' (if she confirms him, it is valid; otherwise not).
3
Understand the reasoning
When the wife denies, the husband is reporting an event he cannot create at the present time (since the 'iddah is over). Her denial is therefore accepted without an oath, based on the principle of the six matters.
The 'six matters' are cases where the word of the one denying a past event is given precedence.
4
Examine the analogy
The passage compares the husband to an agent for sale. If the agent says 'I sold it' before being dismissed, he is believed. But if he says so after dismissal, he is not. Similarly, the husband is believed only if his claim pertains to a time when he had the power to revoke.
The common illa (effective cause) is that the person no longer has the authority to create the act at the time of the statement.
5
Recognize the broader principle
The passage also mentions that the fatwa is to administer an oath in the first seven of the nine matters, according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn). This is an intra-Hanafi difference.
The six matters are expanded to nine, including fixed punishment and imprecation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

His statement: (And if he says after the waiting period: 'I revoked you during it' and she confirms him, it is valid; otherwise (no, i.e., if she does not confirm him, the revocation is not valid, because he is informing about something he cannot create at that moment and she denies it, so the say is hers without an oath, according to what is known in the six matters. And if she confirms him, it is valid because marriage is established by their mutual confirmation, so revocation is more appropriate. And in Talkhis: 'Your revocation of her by such' his statement: (For it is disliked by action. I say: It is evidenced by his statement in al-Fath: 'It is recommended that he revoke her by statement.' Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof addressing a scenario where a husband claims he revoked his wife during the 'iddah but makes the statement only after the 'iddah has ended. The ruling is: if the wife confirms his claim, the revocation is valid; if she denies, it is invalid. The reason given is that the husband is reporting an event he cannot create at the present time (since the 'iddah is over), so her denial takes precedence without her needing to take an oath. This is based on a general principle regarding six matters (later expanded to nine) where the word of one who denies is accepted. The proof includes an analogy to the case of an agent: if the agent says 'I sold the item' before being dismissed, he is believed; after dismissal, he is not. The passage also notes that the mere act of intercourse during the 'iddah is disliked (not haram) as a form of revocation, and it is better to revoke by statement. Finally, it clarifies that the fatwa is to have the wife swear an oath in the first seven of these matters, according to the Sahibayn.

Key Terms
عدة (iddah): The waiting period after divorce, during which revocation is possible
الأشياء الستة (al-ashya' al-sittah): Six matters where the word of one denying a past event is accepted: revocation, marriage, return (fay'), istilad, slavery, and lineage. Later expanded to nine including patronage, fixed punishment, and imprecation.
وكيل بالبيع (wakil bil-bay'): An agent authorized to sell

A husband's claim of revocation after the 'iddah is only valid if the wife confirms it, because he no longer has the power to create the revocation at that moment.

Raj'ah Dispute: When Wife Claims 'Iddah Ended
Intra-Hanafi dispute on raj'ah timing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding raj'ah when the wife claims her 'iddah has ended, and explain the role of inshā' vs ikhbār in the dispute.

Core Idea
Raj'ah is a creative act; its validity depends on the 'iddah being ongoing at the moment of utterance.

This lesson delves into an intra-Hanafi disagreement about the timing of revocation (raj'ah). When a husband declares raj'ah and the wife immediately responds that her 'iddah has already ended, does the raj'ah take effect? Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, offer conflicting answers. Understanding their reasoning — especially the distinction between inshā' (creative speech) and ikhbār (reportive speech) — is essential for grasping how Hanafi jurists approach the intersection of verbal acts and temporal facts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the dispute
The husband says 'I revoke you' (rāja'tuk) and the wife immediately responds 'My waiting period has ended.' The key question: Is the raj'ah valid?
Note that the wife's response is immediate, without any silence.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and proof
Abu Hanifa rules that the raj'ah is invalid because it coincides with the end of the 'iddah. His proof hinges on the nature of the statements: the husband's utterance is inshā' (creative, bringing about a new state), which does not require a prior event. The wife's utterance is ikhbār (report), which presupposes that the 'iddah ended before her speech. Since they occur simultaneously, the raj'ah is ineffective.
Inshā' establishes something new; ikhbār reports a past fact.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and proof
The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) rule that the raj'ah is valid. Their proof: The 'iddah is presumed ongoing until the wife reports its end. The husband's statement precedes her report, so it occurs while the 'iddah is still apparently in effect. They analogize to divorce: if a husband says 'I divorce you' and the wife responds that her 'iddah ended, the divorce occurs. Similarly, a principal dismissing an agent does not negate a sale already made by the agent.
The Sahibayn base their argument on the apparent continuity of the 'iddah and analogy (qiyas).
4
Identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
If the raj'ah is invalid (Abu Hanifa), the wife is made to swear an oath that her 'iddah has indeed ended. If the raj'ah is valid (Sahibayn), no oath is needed because the marriage is already reinstated.
The oath is based on nukūl (refusal) as an implied admission.
5
Evaluate the tarjih and authority weight
The text from al-Bahr al-Raiq notes that later works (al-Badā'i', Ghāyat al-Bayān, etc.) mention the oath only according to Abu Hanifa. A claim of consensus on the oath is explicitly rejected as a mistake. The Sahibayn's view on the validity of raj'ah appears strong in later fatwa, but no definitive tarjih is given in this passage.
The authority weight is nawadir; the mufta bihi is not clearly stated here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كراجعتك فقالت مجيبة مضت عدتي. قوله : (كراجعتك فقالت مجيبة مضت عدتي يعني لو قال لها راجعتك فأجابته بقولها مضت عدتي لا تصح الرجعة عند أبي حنيفة لأنها صادفت حال انقضاء العدة فلا تصح . وقالا : تصح والقول له لأنها صادفت العدة لبقائها ظاهراً ما لم تخبر بالانقضاء وقد سبقت الرجعة خبرها بالانقضاء كما لو قال طلقتك فقالت مجيبة انقضت عدتي فإنه يقع الطلاق، وكالموكل إذا قال للوكيل عزلتك فقال الوكيل مجيباً له بعت لا يصح كذا في المحيط. وله أن قوله راجعتك إنشاء وهو إثبات أمر لم يكن فلا يستدعي سبق الرجعة. وقولها انقضت عدتي إخبار وهو إظهار أمر قد كان فيقتضي سبق الانقضاء ضرورة ومسألة الطلاق قيل على الخلاف فلا يقع عنده كما لو قال أنت طالق مع انقضاء عدتك والأصح أنه يقع لإقرار الزوج بالوقوع كما لو قال بعد انقضاء العدة كنت طلقتها في العدة كان مصدقاً في ذلك بخلاف الرجعة. قيد بكونها إجابته من غير سكوت لأنها لو سكتت ساعة تصح الرجعة اتفاقاً. وأشار بكون الزوج بدأها إلى أنها لو بدأت فقالت انقضت عدتي فقال الزوج مجيباً لها موصولاً بكلامها راجعتك لا يصح بالأولى ولهذا لم يذكر الأسبيجابي فيها خلافاً. وإذا لم تصح الرجعة في مسألة الكتاب تستحلف عنده والفرق بينها وبين الأولى أن اليمين فائدتها النكول وهو بذل عنده، وفي المسألة الأولى تحليفها على الرجعة وبذلها لا يجوز، وفي الثانية تحليفها على مضي عدتها وهو الامتناع عن التزوج والاحتباس في منزل الزوج وبذله جائز. وأما مذهبهما في المسألة الثانية فقد عرفت أنه صحة الرجعة فلا يتصور أن يقال تستحلف المرأة بالإجماع كما ذكره الشارح وقلده في فتح القدير وشرح المجمع وقد اقتصر على أنها تستحلف عند أبي حنيفة في البدائع وغاية البيان والأقطع والخلاصة والولوالجية فكان نقل الإجماع سهواً
Like 'I revoke you,' and she responds, 'My waiting period has ended.' His statement: (Like 'I revoke you,' and she responds, 'My waiting period has ended,' meaning that if he says to her 'I revoke you' and she answers with her statement 'My waiting period has ended,' the revocation is not valid according to Abu Hanifa because it coincides with the state of the waiting period having ended, so it is not valid. And the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: It is valid, and the say is his, because it coincided with the waiting period due to its apparent continuation as long as she has not reported the expiration, and the revocation preceded her report of expiration, as in the case if he said 'I divorced you' and she responded 'My waiting period has ended' then the divorce occurs. And like the principal if he says to the agent 'I dismiss you' and the agent responds 'I have sold,' it is not valid. So it is in al-Muhit. And for him (Abu Hanifa) that his statement 'I revoke you' is a creation (insha'), which is establishing something that was not, so it does not require prior revocation. And her statement 'My waiting period has ended' is a report, which is revealing something that was, so it necessarily requires prior expiration. And the issue of divorce: it is said that it is on a difference; it does not occur according to him, just as if he says 'You are divorced along with the expiration of your waiting period,' and the correct view is that it occurs because the husband admits to its occurrence, just as if he says after the expiration of the waiting period 'I divorced her during the waiting period,' he is believed in that, unlike the revocation. It is conditioned that her response be without silence, because if she remained silent for an hour, the revocation is valid by consensus. And his indication that the husband began: that if she began and said 'My waiting period has ended,' then the husband responding to her in continuation of her speech 'I revoke you' is not valid a fortiori, and for this reason al-Asbijabi did not mention a difference in it. And if the revocation is not valid in the issue of the book, she is made to swear an oath according to him, and the difference between it and the first is that the benefit of the oath is the refusal (nukul) which is giving something according to him. And in the first issue, making her swear concerning the revocation and its giving is not permissible; and in the second, making her swear concerning the passing of her waiting period—which is abstaining from marriage and staying in the husband's house—its giving is permissible. As for the madhhab of the two (the Sahibayn) in the second issue, you have known that it is the validity of the revocation, so it cannot be said that the woman is made to swear by consensus, as the commentator mentioned and followed in Fath al-Qadir and Sharh al-Majma', but it is restricted to that she is made to swear according to Abu Hanifa in al-Bada'i', Ghayat al-Bayan, al-Aqta', al-Khulasa, and al-Walwaljiyya, so the transmission of consensus was a mistake.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) regarding a scenario: the husband says 'I revoke you' (rāja'tuk) and the wife immediately responds, 'My waiting period has ended.' According to Abu Hanifa, the revocation is not valid because it coincides with the end of the 'iddah. According to the Sahibayn, it is valid because the 'iddah is considered ongoing unless the wife reports its end, and the husband's statement preceded her report. The Sahibayn draw an analogy with divorce: if a husband says 'I divorce you' and the wife responds that her 'iddah has ended, the divorce occurs. They also analogize to a principal dismissing an agent, where the agent's claim of sale after dismissal is not effective. Abu Hanifa distinguishes: the husband's statement is an enaction (inshā') establishing something new, not requiring prior occurrence, while the wife's statement is a report of a past event, which requires that the event actually occurred. The divergence leads to different rulings on whether the wife must swear an oath. The passage clarifies that the wife is made to swear only according to Abu Hanifa in this case, not by consensus; the Sahibayn's position makes the revocation valid so no oath is needed.

Key Terms
إنشاء (inshā'): A speech act that brings about a new legal state (e.g., raj'ah), as opposed to reporting a past event.
إخبار (ikhbār): A report about a past event, which presupposes the reported fact.
نكول (nukūl): Refusal to take an oath, which leads to a ruling against the refusing party.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn, why is the raj'ah valid when the wife immediately responds that her 'iddah has ended?
A) Because the wife's report is not accepted in court.
B) Because the 'iddah is considered ongoing until she reports its end, and the husband's declaration precedes the report.
C) Because the husband's statement is a creative act that overrides any report.
D) Because the wife's silence for an hour would have made it valid anyway.

Key Takeaway
The validity of raj'ah when the wife claims her 'iddah has ended depends on whether one prioritizes the apparent continuity of the 'iddah (Sahibayn) or the logical implication of the wife's report (Abu Hanifa).

Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Whose Word is Accepted in Revocation of a Slave Wife
Intra-Hanafi dispute on whose word is accepted in a revocation dispute involving a slave wife.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding whose word is accepted when a slave wife and her master dispute over revocation after 'iddah, and identify the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
In a slave wife's revocation dispute, Abu Hanifa trusts her knowledge of her own 'iddah; the Sahibayn prioritize the master's right over her.

This lesson explores an intra-Hanafi disagreement over a specific scenario: a slave woman's husband claims to have revoked her during her 'iddah, but the slave woman denies it, and the master confirms the husband's claim. Whose word should a qadi accept? Abu Hanifa argues that the slave woman's word is accepted because she knows her 'iddah best. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) argue that the master's word is accepted because he owns the right to her sexual availability (bud'), analogous to his acknowledgment of her marriage. The passage also notes an exception and distinguishes this case from the case of a free woman.

Guided Path
1
State the contested scenario
Identify the exact situation: the husband says he revoked during the 'iddah, the master confirms, the wife denies. Alternatively, the wife says 'iddah ended, both deny. Both versions involve a dispute over whose claim is believed.
The scenario involves a slave wife (ammah), not a free woman.
2
Identify the two positions
Abu Hanifa holds that the wife's word is accepted in both instances. The Sahibayn hold that the master's word is accepted.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
3
Analyze each dalil
Abu Hanifa: revocation depends on 'iddah, and she knows her 'iddah. Sahibayn: the master has the right to her sexual availability (bud'), so his word is like his acknowledgment of the marriage.
Note the linguistic tie: 'bud' is a property right.
4
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
If the wife's word is accepted, she can prevent the revocation. If the master's word is accepted, the revocation stands if he confirms.
This practical difference affects the outcome of the case.
5
Note the exception and distinction
If the master denies the husband and the wife confirms him, the master's word is accepted (correct view). Also, the passage distinguishes from the case of a free woman (who is always believed without oath).
This shows the limits of the dispute.

ولو قال الأمة زوج بعد العدة راجعت فيها فصدقه سيدها وكذبته أو قالت مضت عدتي وأنكرا فالقول لها. قوله : (ولو قال الأمة زوج بعد العدة راجعت فيها فصدق سيدها وكذبته أو قالت مضت عدتي وأنكرا فالقول لها أي أنكر الزوج والمولى وقبول قولها في الأولى قول أبي حنيفة لأن الرجعة تبتني على قيام العدة والقول فيها قولها وقالا : القول للمولى لأن البضع حقه كإقراره عليها بالنكاح. قيد بتصديق السيد لأن المولى لو كذبه وصدقته الأمة فالقول قول المولى على الصحيح في كتاب الدعوى قوله : والفرق بينها وبين (الأولى) المراد بالأولى المذكورة في المتن وهي ما إذا قال بعد العدة راجعتك فيها ولم تصدقه فإن القول لها من غير يمين
And if the husband of a slave woman says after the waiting period 'I revoked her during it,' and her master confirms him while she denies him, or she says 'My waiting period has ended' and both deny her, then the say is hers. His statement: (And if the husband of a slave woman says after the waiting period 'I revoked her during it,' and her master confirms him while she denies him, or she says 'My waiting period has ended' and both deny, then the say is hers, i.e., the husband and the master deny, and accepting her word in the first is the view of Abu Hanifa, because revocation is based on the existence of the waiting period, and the word concerning it is hers. And the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: The word is the master's, because the right to sexual intercourse (al-bud') belongs to him, like his acknowledgment of her marriage. It is conditioned on the master's confirmation, because if the master denies him and the slave woman confirms him, then the word is the master's according to the correct view in the Book of Claims. His statement: 'And the difference between it and the first (al-ula)': by 'the first' is meant that mentioned in the matn, which is if he said after the waiting period 'I revoked you during it' and she did not confirm him, then the word is hers without an oath.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a specific scenario involving a slave woman (أمة) who is married. The husband says after the 'iddah, 'I revoked her during it.' The master (owner) confirms the husband's claim, but the slave wife denies it. Alternatively, the wife says her 'iddah has ended, and both the husband and master deny her. Abu Hanifa's position: the word of the slave woman is accepted (القول لها) in both instances, because revocation depends on the existence of the 'iddah, and she is the one who would know its status. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say: the word of the master is accepted, because the right to the woman's sexual availability (bud') belongs to the master; it is analogous to the master's acknowledgment of her marriage. The commentary also notes an exception: if the master denies the husband's claim and the wife confirms it, then the word of the master is accepted (according to the correct view). The passage concludes by distinguishing this case from the earlier 'first' case (al-ula) in the matn, where a free wife denies the husband's claim of revocation after the 'iddah—there, her word is accepted without an oath.

Key Terms
الأمة (al-ammah): A female slave who is married
السيد (al-sayyid): The master/owner of the slave woman
البضع (al-bud'): The right to sexual intercourse with the woman, owned by the master in case of a slave

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The husband's word
B) The master's word
C) The slave woman's word
D) Both the master and the slave woman's word are equal

Key Takeaway
The acceptance of the slave wife's or the master's word in revocation disputes depends on whether priority is given to personal knowledge of the 'iddah (Abu Hanifa) or to the master's property right to the woman's sexual availability (Sahibayn).

Ruling on Slave Woman's Claim of iddah in Revocation Dispute
Ruling on slave woman's claim of iddah end in revocation dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a slave woman's claim regarding iddah and revocation, and apply the principle 'Whoever possesses the right to initiate possesses the right to inform'.

Core Idea
After iddah, the woman's word prevails; evidence can establish revocation like direct sight.

In Hanafi fiqh, when a husband claims he revoked a divorce after the iddah period, and a dispute arises with his slave wife and her master, who is believed? This lesson unpacks two scenarios from Kanz al-Daqa'iq and its commentary al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, revealing a nuanced interplay between legal authority, evidence, and the rights of the parties involved.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first dispute
The husband claims revocation after iddah; the master confirms, but the woman denies. Determine whose statement is given weight.
The text states 'فالقول لها' – the statement is hers.
2
Understand the legal maxim
The commentary introduces the principle: 'Whoever possesses the right to initiate possesses the right to inform.' This means that if someone can create a legal act, they can also report that act.
Examples: executor, master, revocator, agent with sale authority.
3
Analyze the second scenario
After iddah, if the husband produces evidence (bayyinah) that he said during iddah 'I revoked her' or 'I had intercourse', it is valid. Why? Because evidence is treated like direct sight.
This is an 'astonishing issue' because it establishes a confession via evidence that would not be accepted if confessed directly.
4
Connect to the underlying qiyas
The rule 'al-thabit bi al-bayyinah ka al-thabit bi al-mu'ayanah' is a qiyas jaliy: the original (asl) is direct sight; the branch (far') is evidence; the effective cause (illa) is certainty.
This principle is widely applied in Islamic evidence law.
5
Apply the restrictions
Note the qualification: 'after the iddah'. If the claim is made during iddah, the husband's word is accepted because he still possesses the right to initiate.
This highlights the temporal dimension of the ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قال الأمة زوج بعد العدة راجعت فيها فصدقه سيدها وكذبته أو قالت مضت عدتي وأنكرا فالقول لها الجامع للصدر : من ملك الإنشاء ملك الإخبار كالوصي والمولى والمراجع والوكيل بالبيع ومن له الخيار انتهى. ولو أقام بينة بعد العدة أنه قال في عدتها قد راجعتها أو أنه قال قد جامعتها كان رجعة لأن الثابت بالبينة كالثابت بالمعاينة وهذا من أعجب المسائل فإنه يثبت إقرار نفسه بالبينة بما لو أقر به في الحال لم يكن مقبولاً كذا في المبسوط قيد بقوله «بعد العدة» لأنه لو قال في العدة كنت راجعتك أمس ثبتت وإن كذبته لملكه الإنشاء في الحال.
And if he says about the slave woman 'a husband after the iddah has revoked her' and her master confirms him but she denies, or she says 'my iddah has ended' and both deny, then the statement is hers. The comprehensive principle from the breast: Whoever possesses the right to initiate possesses the right to inform, like the executor, the master, the revocator, the agent for sale, and the one who has an option. End. And if he establishes evidence after the iddah that he said during her iddah 'I have revoked her' or that he said 'I have had intercourse with her', it is a valid revocation because what is established by evidence is like what is established by direct observation. This is one of the most astonishing issues: a person's own confession is established by evidence even though if he were to confess immediately it would not be accepted. As stated in al-Mabsut. The restriction 'after the iddah' is because if he said during the iddah 'I revoked you yesterday', it is established even if she denies, because he possesses the right to initiate at that moment.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq with commentary from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq. It deals with a scenario involving a slave woman (أمة) whose husband claims he revoked the divorce (راجعت) after the iddah had ended. The husband makes this claim, and two parties respond: her master (سيدها) confirms the husband's claim, but the slave woman herself denies it. Alternatively, the slave woman claims her iddah has ended (مضت عدتي), but both the husband and master deny this. The ruling states that the statement (القول) is hers—meaning the woman's word is accepted. The commentary explains the underlying principle: 'Whoever possesses the right to initiate possesses the right to inform' (من ملك الإنشاء ملك الإخبار). This means that if someone has the authority to perform an act (like revoking a divorce), they also have authority to declare that they did so, and their declaration carries weight. The commentary then discusses a second scenario: if after the iddah, the husband produces witnesses that he said during the iddah 'I revoked her' or 'I had intercourse with her', this establishes the revocation as valid—even though if he simply claimed it without witnesses, it would not be accepted. This is because evidence (بينة) is treated like direct sight. The commentary notes this is an 'astonishing issue' because the husband's own confession (إقرار) is established by evidence, whereas if he confessed directly without evidence, it would not be accepted.

Key Terms
الأمة (al-ummah): A female slave owned by a master, who is married to a husband.
الجامع للصدر (al-jami' li al-sadr): A comprehensive principle cited from earlier scholars, meaning a general rule applied at the beginning of a discussion.
رجعة (ruj'ah): Revocation of a revocable divorce (talaq raj'i) during the iddah period.
بينة (bayyinah): Legal evidence, typically two male witnesses or one male and two females.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The statement of the slave woman is accepted and revocation is rejected.
C) The husband's statement is accepted because he has the right to revoke.
D) The master's statement is accepted because he owns the slave.

Key Takeaway
After iddah, the woman's denial of revocation is believed; the husband must produce evidence to prove his claim.

Conditions for the End of Iddah and Cutting Off Revocation
Ruling on when iddah ends: ghusl or passage of prayer time required

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which the iddah ends for a divorced woman in the Hanafi school, and apply the rule that if purity from the last menstruation lasts less than ten days, ghusl or the passage of a full prayer time is required for the iddah to end.

Core Idea
If purity from the last menses lasts ten days, iddah ends even without ghusl; if less, ghusl or prayer time is needed.

In the Hanafi school, the iddah of a divorced woman ends upon the completion of three menstruations (or two for a slave). However, the precise moment when the iddah terminates and the husband's right to revoke the divorce is cut off depends on whether the final purity period reaches the maximum of ten days. This lesson unpacks the rule: if the bleeding stops and remains stopped for ten days, the iddah ends immediately; if it stops in less than ten days, additional actions are required to remove uncertainty about possible return of blood.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios of purity after last menstruation
Distinguish between purity reaching ten days (maximum) and purity being less than ten days. The ruling differs based on this threshold.
The ten-day threshold is the maximum duration of menstruation in the Hanafi school (at most ten days).
2
State the consequence for the right of revocation
If purity lasts ten days, revocation is cut off even without ghusl. If less than ten days, revocation continues until ghusl or passage of a prayer time.
The husband's right to take back his wife ends only when the iddah is definitively over.
3
Understand the reasoning for the ghusl requirement
When purity is less than ten days, there is a possibility that the blood may return (since it's within the habit). Ghusl or the time for prayer (making prayer owed) provides certainty that iddah is complete.
This is a precaution to avoid uncertainty (shakk) about the end of iddah.
4
Recognize the exception for women not obligated to pray
The requirement of ghusl applies to Muslim women who are addressed with prayer. A kitabiyya (Jewish/Christian) woman, or an insane woman, is not required to perform ghusl; her iddah ends upon mere cessation of blood for less than ten days.
This is because the obligation of ghusl is tied to prayer, which they are not obligated for.
5
Evaluate the internal objection based on the Quranic verse
The text raises an objection: the Quran (2:228) says 'three quru' without mentioning ghusl. The Hanafi response is that the additional condition (ghusl) is necessary to remove the possibility of blood return, which is not addressed by the verse.
This shows that istihsan (juristic preference) can override a plain reading when certainty is at stake.

And the meaning of purity here is not the cessation (of bleeding), because by the passage of ten days she has left menstruation even if the bleeding has not stopped. And he indicated by the passage of time that its exit is necessary for the prayer to become a debt in her liability. If it occurs at the beginning, this is not established until the entire time passes because the prayer does not become a debt except by that. As for a kitabiyya (Jewish/Christian woman), by mere cessation for less than ten days her revocation is cut off because she is not addressed. And likewise the insane and mentally deficient. And one could say: The condition of ghusl after cessation for the completion of the custom before ten days is rejected by the evidence: His saying (Qur'an) 'three quru' (قرء)' which is free from this condition. If it is answered that certainty of cessation is lacking because it is not the maximum menstruation and the possibility of blood returning, it is refuted by saying that this additional ghusl does not help in cutting this possibility neither in reality nor in Shari'ah because if she performed ghusl and then blood returned and did not exceed ten days, he has the right of revocation after we said the revocation is cut off. So the matter is contingent on the non-return after ghusl, just as it was before it. And if he revoked her after this ghusl which we said cuts off the revocation, and then the blood returned and did not exceed ten, his revocation is valid. Similarly for tayammum. So the answer to the issue is really restricted as follows: if the bleeding stopped for less than ten days and did not return, or it returned but did not exceed ten, the cutting off of the revocation appears from the time of cessation because the iddah ends at that point, to the extent that if she had married before the ghusl, it would appear valid. If the blood returned and did not exceed, then the aforementioned rulings are reversed. As in Fath al-Qadir. Al-Bahr said: This, meaning the validity of revocation and marriage when blood returns within less than ten days, is as Fath al-Qadir researched. And although it contradicts the apparent texts, the meaning supports it. End. And you have known that the discussion is only about the condition of ghusl.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof (ruling_with_proof) regarding the conditions under which the right of revocation (رجعة) is cut off due to the completion of the iddah. The main point: If a woman becomes pure (i.e., her menstruation ends) after the last menstruation that marks the completion of iddah, and she has reached a full ten days (the maximum duration of menstrual impurity), then the revocation is cut off even if she has not performed ghusl. However, if the purity occurs in less than ten days, the revocation is not cut off until she performs ghusl or a full prayer time passes. The commentary discusses the reasoning: the husband's right to revoke ceases only when the iddah is definitely over. For a maximum of ten days, the iddah ends by the mere passage of time. For less than ten days, there is a possibility that the bleeding might resume, so ghusl or prayer time is required to confirm the end. The text then compares the case of a woman who claims iddah ended by childbirth or miscarriage: such claims require evidence, unlike the claim of iddah ending by menstruation. The passage also addresses the issue of oaths and the distinction between free and slave women. The later part of the commentary cites Al-Nahr and Fath al-Qadir, discussing the view that the condition of ghusl is not required by the Quranic verse 'three quru', and that the discussion amounts to a research on the requirement of ghusl.

Key Terms
القروء (al-quru'): The menstrual periods (or purity periods) that constitute the iddah for divorced women.
الاغتسال (al-ightisal): Ritual full-body purification from menstruation (ghusl).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The iddah ends with certainty only when the last menstruation is followed by ten full days of purity, or if less than ten days, by performing ghusl or waiting a full prayer time.

When Does the Right of Return (Raj'ah) Cut Off? The Ruling on Completion of 'Iddah
Ruling with proof: explains when raj'ah ends based on completion of 'iddah with conditions for certainty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on when the right of return (raj'ah) is cut off when the wife claims her 'iddah has ended, and explain the condition of ghusl for less than ten days.

Core Idea
The right of return is cut off only when the 'iddah is certain; for less than ten days, ghusl or prayer time is required to eliminate doubt.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a practical scenario: a divorcing wife claims her 'iddah has ended, but the husband denies. The Hanafi school accepts the wife's statement but imposes conditions to ensure legal certainty. You will learn the precise moment when the husband's right to revoke the divorce (raj'ah) is cut off, and why the school distinguishes between ten days and less than ten days.

Guided Path
1
State the general rule
Identify that when the wife claims her 'iddah has ended and the husband denies, the wife's statement is accepted (al-qawl laha). This is because she knows her own cycles best.
The rule applies only to claims of 'iddah completion by menstrual cycles, not by childbirth or miscarriage, which require evidence.
2
Distinguish the two cases
If the third menstrual blood stops for a full ten days, the right of return is cut off immediately, even if she has not performed ghusl. If it stops for less than ten days, the right is not cut off until she performs ghusl or a full prayer time passes (or tayammum and prayer if no water).
Ten days is the maximum duration of menstruation in the Hanafi school.
3
Understand the reasoning
The condition for less than ten days is precautionary: blood may return within the maximum menstrual period. Ghusl or the passing of a prayer time serves as a confirmatory indication that the blood has truly stopped.
This is based on ihtiyat (precaution) to ensure certainty that the 'iddah has ended.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Hanifa (according to some transmissions) held that mere stoppage is sufficient even for less than ten days, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad require ghusl or prayer time. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) follows the Sahibayn.
The external opposing school (Shafi'i) also considers mere stoppage sufficient, citing Surah al-Baqara 2:228.
5
Apply to special cases
For a kitabiyya (Scriptuary wife), the condition of ghusl is waived because she is not obligated to pray. Similarly, the insane or mentally deficient are not required to perform ghusl for the cut-off.
The passage also mentions that if blood returns after ghusl within ten days, the cut-off is reversed, meaning the husband may still revoke.
