وأنكرا فالقول لها وتنقطع الرجعة إن طهرت من الحيض الأخير لعشرة وإن لم تغتسل ولأقل لا حتى تغتسل أو بمضي وقت صلاة أو تتيمم وتصلي ولو اغتسلت ونسيت لأن ملكه قد ظهر للحال بخلاف الأول لاعترافه ببقاء العدة ولا يظهر ملكه معها. فالحاصل أنه لا فرق في الحكم بين المسألتين وهو عدم صحة الرجعة وإن اختلف التصوير. وقيد بكونها قالت مضت عدتي لأنها لو قالت ولدت يعني انقضت عدتي بالولادة لا يقبل إلا ببينة، وكذا لو قالت أسقطت سقطاً مستبين الخلق وللزوج أن يطلب يمينها على أنها أسقطت بهذه الصفة بالاتفاق، ولا فرق في هذا بين الحرة والأمة كذا في فتح القدير. وفي شرح النقاية : لو قالت انقضت عدتي ثم قالت لم تنقض كان له الرجعة لأنها أخبرت بكذبها في حق عليها انتهى . قوله : (وتنقطع إن طهرت من الحيض الأخير لعشرة وإن لم تغتسل ولأقل لا حتى تغتسل أو يمضي وقت صلاة أي وتنقطع الرجعة إن حكم بخروجها من الحيضة الثالثة إن كانت حرة أو الثانية إن كانت أمة لتمام عشرة أيام مطلقاً وليس المراد من الطهارة هنا الانقطاع لأنها بمضي العشرة خرجت من الحيض وإن لم ينقطع. وأشار بمضي الوقت إلى أنه لا بد من خروجه لتصير الصلاة ديناً في ذمتها فإن كان الطهر في آخر الوقت فهو ذلك الزمن اليسير الذي تقدر فيه على الاغتسال والتحريمة لا ما دونه، وإن كان في أوله لم يثبت هذا حتى يخرج جميعه لأن الصلاة لا تصير ديناً إلا بذلك. وعلى هذا لو طهرت في وقت مهمل قوله : (وظاهره أن القاطع للرجعة الانقطاع الخ) قال في النهر : ودل كلامه أي المصنف أن هذا فيمن تخاطب بالغسل والصلاة، أما الكتابية فبمجرد الانقطاع لما دون العشرة تنقطع رجعتها لعدم خطابها، وينبغي أن تكون المجنونة والمعتوهة كذلك ولقائل أن يقول اشتراط الغسل بعد الانقطاع لتمام العادة قبل العشرة يرده الدليل وهو قوله تعالى ثلاثة قروء ﴾ [البقرة: ٢٢٨] لخلوه عن اشتراطه. فإن أجيب بأن تيقن الانقطاع منتف لغرض أنه ليس أكثر الحيض واحتمال عود الدم دفع بأن هذا الاغتسال الزائد لا يجدي قطع هذا الاحتمال لا في الواقع ولا شرعاً لأنها لو اغتسلت ثم عاد الدم ولم يجاوز العشرة كان له الرجعة بعد أن قلنا انقطعت الرجعة فكان الحال موقوفاً على عدم العود بعد الغسل كما هو كذلك قبله ولو راجعها بعد هذا الغسل الذي قلنا إن به تنقطع الرجعة ثم عاودها ولم يجاوز العشرة صحت رجعته وكذا الكلام في التيمم فليس جواب المسألة في الحقيقة إلا مقيداً هكذا إذا انقطع لأقل من عشرة ولم يعاودها أو عاودها ولم يتجاوزها ظهر انقطاع الرجعة من وقت الانقطاع لانقضاء العدة إذ ذاك حتى لو كانت تزوجت قبل الغسل ظهر صحته، وإن عاودها الدم ولم يجاوز فالأحكام المذكورة بالعكس كذا في فتح القدير. قال في البحر: وهذا أعني صحة الرجعة والنكاح فيما إذا عاودها الدم فيما دون العشرة ؛ كذا أفاده في فتح القدير بحثاً وهو وإن خالفه ظاهر المتون لكن المعنى يساعده ا هـ . وأنت قد علمت بأن البحث ليس إلا في اشتراط الغسل كبعد الشروق لا تنقطع الرجعة إلى دخول وقت العصر وأطلق الاغتسال فشمل ما إذا اغتسلت بسؤر الحمار ولو مع وجود الماء المطلق فإنه تنقطع الرجعة لاحتمال طهارته وإن كانت لا تصلي به لاحتمال النجاسة ولذا لا يقربها الزوج ولا تتزوج بآخر احتياطاً كما في التتارخانية. وإنما شرط في الأقل أحد الشيئين لأنه لما احتمل عود الدم لبقاء المدة فلا بد من أن يتقوى الانقطاع بحقيقة الاغتسال أو يلزم شيء من أحكام الطاهرات فخرجت الكتابية لأنه لا يتوقع في حقها أمارة زائدة فاكتفى بالانقطاع ، كذا ذكره الشارحون وظاهره أن القاطع للرجعة الانقطاع لكن لما كان غير محقق اشترط معه ما يحققه فأفاد أنها لو اغتسلت ثم عاد الدم ولم يجاوز العشرة كان له الرجعة وتبين أن الرجعة لم تنقطع بالغسل، ولو تزوجت بعد الانقطاع للأقل قبل الغسل ومضي الوقت تبين صحة النكاح ؛ هكذا أفاد في فتح القدير بحثاً وهو وإن خالف ظاهر المتون لكن المعنى يساعده والقواعد لا تأباه قوله : (أو تتيمم وتصلي) أي لا تنقطع الرجعة عند فقد الماء حتى تتيمم وتصلي به فرضاً كان أو غيره، ولا يكفي مجرد التيمم عندهما لأنها طهارة ضرورية لم تشرع إلا عند العجز عن الماء فلا بد لها من مؤكد فلا ينافيه قولهما في باب الإمامة أنها طهارة مطلقة حتى جوز اقتداء المتوضيء بالمتيمم لأن موجود فقط، ولا نسلم المخالفة لظاهر المتون لأنه لو عاودها تبين عدم انقطاعه والله تعالى الموفق ا هـ. ولا يخفى عليك أن البحث في اشتراط الغسل يؤدي إلى صحة النكاح بعد الانقطاع للأقل قبل الغسل، ذلك وكذا يؤدي إلى صحة الرجعة وعدم صحة النكاح لو اغتسلت ثم عاودها ولم يجاوز بل في كلام الفتح فما معنى الرد على المؤلف في النقل؟ ثم إن قول المتن ولأقل لا حتى تغتسل» يفيد أنها لو لم تغتسل لا تنقطع الرجعة وإن لم يعاودها الدم، وكذا يفيد عدم صحة تزوجها قبل الغسل. وبحث صاحب الفتح بخلاف هذا كما لا يخفى. وقوله قال في البحر وهذا أعني صحة الرجعة والنكاح الخ» الظاهر أن فيه سقطاً والأصل وعدم صحته النكاح تأمل. بقي أن ظاهر كلام المتن هنا أن الاغتسال فيما لو انقطع لأقل من العشرة يقطع الرجعة ولو كان لدون العادة، وظاهر صدر عبارة الفتح السابقة تخصيصه بالعادة، وذكر المؤلف في باب الحيض ما نصه وفي الخلاصة : إذا انقطع دم المرأة دون عادتها المعروفة في حيض أو نفاس اغتسلت حتى تخاف فوت الصلاة وصلت واجتنب زوجها قربانها احتياطاً حتى تأتي على عادتها لكن تصوم رمضان احتياطاً، ولو كانت هذه الحيضة هي من العدة انقطعت الرجعة احتياطاً ولا تتزوج بزوج آخر احتياطاً، فإن تزوجها رجل إن لم يعاودها الدم جاز، وإن عاودها إن كان في العشرة ولم يزد على العشرة فسد نكاح الثاني، وكذا صاحب الاستبراء يجتنيها احتياطاً ا قال في فتح القدير : ومفهوم التقييد أنه إذا زاد لا يفسد ومراده إذا كان العود بعد انقضاء العادة، أما قبلها فيفسد وإن زاد لأن الزيادة توجب الرد إلى العادة والفرض أنه عاودها فيها فظهر أن النكاح قبل انقضاء الحيضة ا هـ . كلام المؤلف هناك. قوله: (لأن الثالثة اهـ.
And if he denies, then the statement is hers, and the right of return is cut off if she becomes pure from the last menstruation for ten [days] even if she did not perform ghusl, and for less than that, no, until she performs ghusl or a prayer time passes, or she performs tayammum and prays, even if she performed ghusl and forgot. Because his right of possession has appeared for the present, unlike the first, due to his admission of the remaining of the iddah, and his possession does not appear with it. So the conclusion is that there is no difference in the ruling between the two issues, that is the invalidity of the return, even though the description differs. And it is restricted to her saying 'my iddah has passed' because if she said 'I gave birth', meaning my iddah ended by childbirth, it is not accepted except with evidence. Similarly, if she said 'I miscarried a fetus with distinct creation', and the husband has the right to request her oath that she miscarried in this manner by agreement, and there is no difference in this between a free woman and a slave, as in Fath al-Qadeer. And in Sharh al-Niqayah: if she said 'my iddah has ended' then said 'it has not ended', he has the right of return because she informed of her lie regarding a right over her. End. His statement: 'And it is cut off if she becomes pure from the last menstruation for ten even if she did not perform ghusl, and for less, no until she performs ghusl or a prayer time passes' means the right of return is cut off if it is judged that she has exited from the third menstruation if free or the second if slave, upon completion of ten days absolutely, and what is meant by purity here is not the stopping, because by the passing of ten days she has exited from menstruation even if it did not stop. And he indicated by 'passing of time' that it must exit so that the prayer becomes a debt upon her. If the purity is at the end of the time, it is that short period in which she is able to perform ghusl and the takbirat al-ihram, not less than that. If it is at its beginning, this is not established until all of it exits because prayer only becomes a debt by that. And upon this, if she becomes pure in a neglected time. He said: 'And its apparent meaning is that the cutter of return is the stopping, etc.' He said in al-Nahr: And his statement, i.e., the author's, indicates that this is for one who is addressed with ghusl and prayer. As for a kitabiyya, by mere stopping for less than ten, her return is cut off due to her not being addressed. And the insane and the mentally deficient should be similar. And one may say that the condition of ghusl after stopping for the completion of the habit before ten is refuted by the evidence, namely His statement 'three quru'' [Quran 2:228], for it is free from conditioning it. If it is answered that certainty of stopping is non-existent due to it not being the maximum menstruation and the possibility of return of blood, it is refuted that this extra ghusl does not avail to cut this possibility neither in reality nor legally, because if she performed ghusl then blood returned and did not exceed ten, he has the right of return after we said the return was cut off, so the situation was dependent on non-return after ghusl as it was before it. And if he took her back after this ghusl by which we said the return is cut off, then it returned and did not exceed ten, his return is valid. Similarly for tayammum. So the answer to the issue is not in reality except restricted thus: if it stopped for less than ten and did not return, or returned and did not exceed, the cutting off of return becomes apparent from the time of stopping due to the expiry of the iddah then, even if she had married before ghusl, its validity becomes apparent. And if the blood returned and did not exceed, the mentioned rulings are reversed, as in Fath al-Qadeer. He said in al-Bahr: 'And this, I mean the validity of return and marriage, regarding when blood returns within ten.' Thus it is given in Fath al-Qadeer as a research, and although it contradicts the apparent of the texts, the meaning supports it. End. And you have known that the research is only about conditioning ghusl. Like after sunrise, the return is not cut off until the entry of the time of Asr. And he generalized the ghusl, so it includes if she performed ghusl with the remaining water of a donkey even with the presence of pure water, then the return is cut off due to the possibility of its purity, even if she does not pray with it due to the possibility of impurity, and therefore the husband does not approach her, nor does she marry another, as a precaution, as in al-Tatarkhaniyya. And the condition in the lesser case of one of the two things is because when the possibility of return of blood remains due to remaining time, it is necessary that the stopping be strengthened by the reality of ghusl or that something from the rulings of purity be required. So the kitabiyya is excluded because no additional indication is expected in her case, so the stopping is sufficient. Thus mentioned the commentators. And its apparent meaning is that the cutter of return is the stopping, but since it is not certain, it is conditioned with what verifies it. So it indicates that if she performed ghusl then blood returned and did not exceed ten, he has the right of return, and it becomes clear that the return was not cut off by the ghusl. And if she married after stopping for the lesser before ghusl and before the passing of time, the validity of the marriage becomes apparent. Thus it is given in Fath al-Qadeer as a research, and although it contradicts the apparent of the texts, the meaning supports it and the principles do not reject it. His statement: 'or she performs tayammum and prays' means the return is not cut off when water is absent until she performs tayammum and prays with it, whether a fard or other. And mere tayammum is not sufficient according to them because it is a necessary purification not legislated except in the inability to find water, so it must have a confirmatory act. So it does not contradict their statement in the chapter of imamah that it is an absolute purification, to the extent that they permitted a person with wudu to follow a person with tayammum as imam, because it is found only. And we do not concede contradiction to the apparent of the texts, because if it returned, it becomes clear that it was not cut off. And Allah is the Granter of success. End. And it is not hidden from you that the research on conditioning ghusl leads to the validity of marriage after the stopping for the lesser before ghusl, and similarly leads to the validity of return and invalidity of marriage if she performed ghusl then it returned and did not exceed. But in the speech of al-Fath, what is the meaning of refuting the author in transmission? Then indeed the statement of the text 'and for less, no until she performs ghusl' indicates that if she does not perform ghusl, the return is not cut off even if the blood does not return, and similarly indicates the invalidity of her marriage before ghusl. And the research of the author of al-Fath is contrary to this, as is not hidden. And his statement: 'He said in al-Bahr: This, I mean the validity of return and marriage, etc.' Apparently there is a lacuna, and the original is 'and its invalidity of marriage' reflect. Remain that the apparent of the text here is that ghusl in the case of stopping for less than ten cuts off the return even if it is before the habit. And the apparent of the beginning of the phrase of al-Fath earlier specifies it to the habit. And the author mentioned in the chapter of menstruation the text: 'In al-Khulasa: If a woman's blood stops before her known habit in menstruation or lochia, she performs ghusl until she fears missing prayer, and prays, and her husband avoids approaching her as a precaution until her habit passes, but she fasts Ramadan as a precaution. And if this menstruation is from the iddah, the return is cut off as a precaution, and she does not marry another husband as a precaution. Then if a man marries her, if the blood does not return, it is permissible; if it returns and it is within ten and does not exceed ten, the second marriage is void. Similarly, the one undertaking istibra' avoids her as a precaution.' He said in Fath al-Qadeer: 'And the meaning of the restriction is that if it increases, it does not become void, and his intent is if the return is after the passing of the habit; as for before it, it becomes void even if it increases because the increase necessitates return to the habit, and the assumption is that it returned in it.' So it appears that the marriage before the expiry of the menstruation. End. The author's speech there. His statement: 'because the third, etc.'

Commentary
Passage type: Ruling with proof and commentary. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the ruling on when a wife claims her iddah has expired and the husband denies it. The core ruling is that the wife's statement is accepted (القول لها) because she is the one who knows her own cycles, and the right of revocable return (الرجعة) is cut off if she becomes pure from the last menstruation either after ten days (regardless of ghusl) or for less than ten (which requires either ghusl or the passing of a prayer time). The proof cited is the Quranic verse 'wa al-mutallaqatu yatarabbasna bi-anfusihinna thalathata quru'' (three menstrual periods), which does not condition ghusl. The commentary then addresses the possibility that blood returns within ten days after ghusl, leading to the ruling that the return of blood negates the cut-off, so the husband may still revoke. The author follows the order of the matn: first the ruling on ten days (automatic cut-off even without ghusl), then the ruling on less than ten (requires ghusl or prayer time passage). Within this, there is an intra-Hanafi difference: the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) require tayammum to be followed by prayer for it to cut off talaq, while Abu Hanifa differs. The passage also treats the case of a kitabiyya (Scriptuary wife) for whom ghusl is not conditioned because she is not obligated to pray. The commentary stresses that the true cutter of the right of return is the actual completion of the iddah, not the ghusl or prayer time in themselves; these are only confirming indications. The author from Fath al-Qadeer notes that if the wife marries after the stoppage of blood for less than ten before performing ghusl, the marriage is valid if the blood does not return; if it returns, it invalidates the second marriage. This is a precautionary ruling due to the possibility of ongoing iddah. The passage concludes with a note that the debate is solely about the condition of ghusl, and the position of the matn may differ from the research of some later scholars. Overall, the student should understand that the iddah ends with the completion of three cycles, and the right of return depends on that fact. The ghusl and prayer time are only external indicators to ascertain the completion.

Key Terms
الرجعة (al-raj'ah): The right of a husband to take back his wife during her iddah after a revocable divorce without a new marriage contract.
العدة (al-iddah): The waiting period that a woman must observe after divorce or death before remarrying. For a divorced woman, it is three menstrual cycles (or three months if not menstruating).
الطهر (al-tuhr): Purity from menstruation, i.e., the cessation of menstrual blood. In this context, it is used to mean the completion of the third menstrual period and the beginning of purity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A free woman is in her 'iddah from a revocable divorce. Her third menstrual period stops after 8 days, but she does not perform ghusl or wait for a prayer time. According to the Hanafi relied-upon position, is her right of return cut off?
A) Yes, because the blood has stopped and her 'iddah is complete.
B) No, because she must perform ghusl or wait for a prayer time since it is less than ten days.
C) Yes, because ghusl is not a condition for the 'iddah to end.
D) No, because she must wait ten days regardless.

Key Takeaway
The right of return is cut off only when the 'iddah is established with certainty; for less than ten days, ghusl or prayer time is required to confirm that the blood has truly stopped.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate
8 min
Termination of Raj'ah through Ghusl and Tayammum
Explains conditions for termination of raj'ah with ghusl and tayammum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi conditions for termination of raj'ah and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding tayammum.

Core Idea
Raj'ah does not end with mere cessation of bleeding; ghusl or tayammum with prayer is required.

In this lesson, we examine a key ruling from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq on when a husband's right to take back his wife after revocable divorce is terminated. The author clarifies that the end of menstruation alone is insufficient; the wife must perform ghusl or, if water is absent, tayammum followed by prayer. This ruling carries important practical consequences and involves a nuanced dispute among Hanafi authorities regarding the sufficiency of tayammum alone.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
What is the legal act being ruled upon? It is the termination of raj'ah (the husband's right to take back his wife). The ruling: raj'ah is not terminated until the woman performs ghusl or, in absence of water, tayammum and prayer.
Note that the act is not the divorce itself, but the end of the revocation period.
2
Locate the condition
What condition is placed on the termination? Mere cessation of bleeding is insufficient; full purification through ghusl or its substitute is required. The passage uses the example of bathing with donkey's leftover water to illustrate that even potentially impure water suffices for ghusl in this context.
This highlights the precautionary nature of the ruling.
3
Examine the exception of the kitabiyya
Why is a non-Muslim woman (kitabiyya) exempted from the requirement of ghusl? Because no additional sign (like prayer) is expected from her, so cessation alone suffices.
The passage mentions that the explainers noted this exception.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
What is the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) regarding tayammum? Abu Hanifa holds that mere tayammum may suffice; the Sahibayn require prayer as a confirmatory act. Their reasoning: tayammum is a necessary purification (ṭahāra ḍarūriyya) that needs a confirmatory act to ensure certainty.
The text mentions that tayammum's absolute nature in the chapter of imamate does not carry over here.
5
Evaluate the practical consequence (thamarah)
What difference does this dispute make? If a woman performs only tayammum without prayer, she cannot remarry according to the mufta bihi position (Sahibayn). The precautionary approach requires prayer to ensure termination.
The tarjih authority (al-Marghinani, Fath al-Qadir) supports the Sahibayn.

العجز عن الماء فلا بد لها من مؤكد فلا ينافيه قولهما في باب الإمامة أنها طهارة مطلقة حتى جوز اقتداء المتوضيء بالمتيمم لأن موجود فقط، ولا نسلم المخالفة لظاهر المتون لأنه لو عاودها تبين عدم انقطاعه والله تعالى الموفق ا هـ. ولا يخفى عليك أن البحث في اشتراط الغسل يؤدي إلى صحة النكاح بعد الانقطاع للأقل قبل الغسل، ذلك وكذا يؤدي إلى صحة الرجعة وعدم صحة النكاح لو اغتسلت ثم عاودها ولم يجاوز بل في كلام الفتح فما معنى الرد على المؤلف في النقل؟ ثم إن قول المتن ولأقل لا حتى تغتسل» يفيد أنها لو لم تغتسل لا تنقطع الرجعة وإن لم يعاودها الدم، وكذا يفيد عدم صحة تزوجها قبل الغسل. وبحث صاحب الفتح بخلاف هذا كما لا يخفى. وقوله قال في البحر وهذا أعني صحة الرجعة والنكاح الخ» الظاهر أن فيه سقطاً والأصل وعدم صحته النكاح تأمل. بقي أن ظاهر كلام المتن هنا أن الاغتسال فيما لو انقطع لأقل من العشرة يقطع الرجعة ولو كان لدون العادة، وظاهر صدر عبارة الفتح السابقة تخصيصه بالعادة، وذكر المؤلف في باب الحيض ما نصه وفي الخلاصة : إذا انقطع دم المرأة دون عادتها المعروفة في حيض أو نفاس اغتسلت حتى تخاف فوت الصلاة وصلت واجتنب زوجها قربانها احتياطاً حتى تأتي على عادتها لكن تصوم رمضان احتياطاً، ولو كانت هذه الحيضة هي من العدة انقطعت الرجعة احتياطاً ولا تتزوج بزوج آخر احتياطاً، فإن تزوجها رجل إن لم يعاودها الدم جاز، وإن عاودها إن كان في العشرة ولم يزد على العشرة فسد نكاح الثاني، وكذا صاحب الاستبراء يجتنيها احتياطاً ا قال في فتح القدير : ومفهوم التقييد أنه إذا زاد لا يفسد ومراده إذا كان العود بعد انقضاء العادة، أما قبلها فيفسد وإن زاد لأن الزيادة توجب الرد إلى العادة والفرض أنه عاودها فيها فظهر أن النكاح قبل انقضاء الحيضة ا هـ . كلام المؤلف هناك. قوله: (لأن الثالثة ا هـ.
Book of Divorce, Chapter of Revocation: Like after sunrise, revocation is not terminated until the entry of the time of Asr. He generalized ghusl (ritual bath) to include the case where she bathed with the leftover water of a donkey even if pure water is available, for revocation is terminated due to its probability of purity, even if she does not pray with it due to probability of impurity. Therefore, her husband does not approach her, and she does not marry another, as a precaution, as in al-Tatar Khaniyya. And in the case of less than the minimum period, one of two things is required because since the return of blood is possible due to the remaining time, the cessation must be strengthened by actual ghusl or by something from the rulings of the ritually pure. Thus the kitabiyya (Scriptuary woman) is excluded because no additional sign is expected in her case, so she is content with cessation. Thus the explainers mentioned. And its apparent meaning is that the cutter of revocation is cessation, but since it is not certain, it is conditioned with something that makes it certain. So it conveys that if she bathed and then the blood returned but did not exceed ten days, he has the right of revocation, and it becomes clear that revocation did not terminate with ghusl. And if she married after cessation for the minimum period before ghusl and time passed, the marriage is valid. Thus it is conveyed in Fath al-Qadir as a research, and although it contradicts the apparent of the texts, the meaning supports it and the principles do not reject it. His saying: (or performs tayammum and prays), i.e., revocation is not terminated when water is absent until she performs tayammum and prays with it, obligatory or otherwise. Mere tayammum is not sufficient according to them (the Sahibayn) because it is a necessary purification not legislated except in inability to use water, so it must have a confirmatory. So their saying in the chapter of Imamate that it is absolute purification to the extent that a person with wudu may follow a person with tayammum does not contradict this, because existence only. And we do not admit contradiction to the apparent of the texts because if it returned to her, it would become clear that it was not cut. And Allah is the giver of success. And it is not hidden from you that the research on the condition of ghusl leads to the validity of marriage after cessation for the minimum before ghusl. That also leads to the validity of revocation and the invalidity of marriage if she bathed and then it returned but did not exceed. Rather, in the speech of Fath, what is the meaning of objection to the author in transmission? Then his saying: the matn says 'and for the minimum, not until she performs ghusl' indicates that if she did not perform ghusl, revocation is not terminated even if the blood did not return, and also indicates the invalidity of her marriage before ghusl. The research of the author of Fath is contrary to this, as is not hidden. And his saying: he said in al-Bahr: 'This, meaning the validity of revocation and marriage etc.' The apparent is that there is a lacuna, and the original is 'and the invalidity of marriage.' Consider. Remainder: The apparent of the matn here is that ghusl, when cessation occurs for less than ten days, cuts revocation even if it is less than the habit. And the apparent of the earlier part of Fath's statement specifies it to the habit. And the author mentioned in the chapter of hayd the text: In al-Khulasa: if a woman's blood stops before her known habit in hayd or nifas, she performs ghusl until she fears missing the prayer, then prays, and her husband avoids approaching her as a precaution until her habit is completed, but she fasts Ramadan as a precaution. And if this hayd is part of the 'idda, revocation is cut as a precaution, and she does not marry another as a precaution. If a man marries her, if the blood does not return, it is permissible; if it returns within ten days and does not exceed ten, the second marriage is invalid. Similarly, the owner of istibra' must avoid her as a precaution. He said in Fath al-Qadir: the implication of the restriction is that if it exceeds, it is not invalid; and his intention is if the return is after the completion of the habit. As for before it, it is invalid even if it exceeds, because the excess necessitates return to the habit and the assumption is that it returned within it. So it becomes clear that the marriage is invalid before the completion of the hayd. End of author's speech there. His saying: (because the third) etc.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq discusses the termination of the husband's right to take back his wife (raj'ah) after a revocable divorce. The passage begins by clarifying that the mere cessation of bleeding during the 'idda does not automatically end raj'ah. Instead, two conditions are required for termination: either the woman performs ghusl or she performs tayammum and prays. The text uses the example of a woman who bathes with wastewater from a donkey—although the water is potentially impure, the ghusl is considered valid for the purpose of cutting raj'ah due to the possibility of purity, even though she may not pray with it. The husband must avoid her and she cannot remarry until certainty is achieved, as a precaution. The commentary then addresses the case where bleeding resumes after ghusl but within ten days: the original raj'ah is not considered terminated, and the husband retains the right to take her back. A key point is the dispute between the author and Fath al-Qadir regarding whether ghusl is sufficient or whether additional time must pass. The text also examines tayammum: according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn), mere tayammum is not enough; prayer must be performed to confirm the termination. This is because tayammum is a dispensation that requires a confirmatory act. The passage then discusses the implications if the bleeding returns after ghusl and the role of precaution in determining the validity of subsequent marriages. Finally, it touches on the case of a non-Muslim woman (kitabiyya) for whom no additional sign is expected, so cessation alone suffices.

Key Terms
رَجْعَة (raj'ah): The right of a husband to take back his wife during her 'idda after a revocable divorce.
اغْتِسَال (ghusl): Ritual bath performed after menstruation or postpartum bleeding to become ritually pure.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position as explained in this passage, what is required for the termination of raj'ah after menstruation stops?
A) The mere cessation of bleeding
B) Ghusl or tayammum with prayer
C) Only tayammum
D) The passage of the full 'idda period

Key Takeaway
Raj'ah is terminated only by confirmed purification: ghusl or, in absence of water, tayammum with prayer; mere cessation of bleeding is insufficient.

Husband Denying Intercourse After Divorce of Pregnant Wife
Ruling on husband denying intercourse after divorcing pregnant wife

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a husband denies intercourse after divorcing his pregnant wife and explain why his denial is rejected based on the legal presumption of pregnancy.

Core Idea
Pregnancy legally proves consummation; the husband's denial is legally rejected (mukadhdhab shar'an).

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a scenario where a divorcing husband's claim contradicts a legal presumption. Understanding how the Hanafi school handles such conflicts reveals the weight given to established evidence (amara) over personal claims. The ruling is clear: the husband's right of raj'ah remains intact.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key factual claim and legal issue
The husband claims he never had intercourse, but his wife is pregnant or has recently given birth. The legal issue is whether his denial affects his right to take her back (raj'ah) during the waiting period.
The case assumes the divorce is revocable (talaq raj'i).
2
Recognize the legal presumption (amara)
Pregnancy (or birth) is a proven fact that legally establishes consummation because lineage (nasab) is affirmed through it. This is a strong indicator that overrides a mere claim to the contrary.
The text explicitly says: 'ثبت حكماً لثبوت النسب' (established legally due to lineage).
3
Apply the principle of 'mukadhdhab shar'an'
A person whose statement contradicts established legal evidence is deemed legally a liar. His claim is not heeded in matters of family law. Here, the husband's denial is rejected because it conflicts with the legal proof of pregnancy.
The term 'مكذب شرعاً' means 'legally rendered a liar'.
4
Distinguish the iqrar exception
The commentator contrasts this with a case from al-Kafi: if someone confesses a slave to another (iqrar), that confession binds him even if he later becomes 'mukadhdhab shar'an', because a third party's right is attached. In raj'ah, no third party's right is involved, so the general rule applies.
This distinction shows that iqrar can create binding obligations even against legal presumptions.
5
State the final ruling
The husband still has the right of raj'ah. His denial is legally irrelevant, and the waiting period (iddah) is established based on the pregnancy.
The operative ruling (mufta bihi) is that the husband's denial is rejected.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ولو طلق ذا حمل أو ولد وقال لم أطأها راجع يعني لو طلق امرأته وهي حامل أو بعد ما ولدت في عصمته وقال لم أجامعها فله الرجعة لأنها مبنية على الدخول وقد ثبت حكماً لثبوت النسب لأنه يثبت بظهور الحمل بأن ولدت لأقل من ستة أشهر فلم يلتفت إلى قوله لم أطأها لأنه صار مكذباً شرعاً ومن صار مكذباً شرعاً بطل زعمه ما لم يتعلق بإقراره حق الغير فلا يرد ما أورده في الكافي بأن . من أقر بعبد لآخر ثم اشتراه ثم استحق من يده ثم وصل إليه فإنه يؤمر بالتسليم إلى المقر له وإن صار مكذباً شرعاً لكونه تعلق بإقراره حق الغير بخلاف مسألة الرجعة. ثم اعلم أن حل قربان الزوج لها غير متوقف عليها الخ مخالف لما مر تصحيحه في الطهارة وعبارة المؤلف هناك فالحاصل أن التيمم لا يوجب حل وطئها وانقطاع الرجعة وحلها للأزواج إلا بالصلاة على الصحيح من المذهب ونقل تصحيحه عن المبسوط وأنه عند الكل ثم قال : لكن قال الأسبيجابي : وأجمعوا أنه يقربها زوجها وإن لم تصل ولا تتزوج زوجاً آخر ما لم تصل وفي انقطاع الرجعة الخلاف .
His saying: (And if he divorced a pregnant one or one who gave birth and said he did not have intercourse with her, he may take her back.) Meaning: if he divorced his wife while she was pregnant, or after she gave birth while in his marriage, and he said he did not have intercourse with her, he has the right of revocation because it (raj'ah) is built on consummation (dukhul), and it (consummation) is legally established due to the establishment of lineage, because it (lineage) is established by the appearance of pregnancy, such that she gave birth in less than six months. So his saying 'I did not have intercourse with her' is not heeded because he is legally rendered a liar. And whoever is legally rendered a liar, his claim is nullified unless a third party's right is attached to his confession. So it does not respond to what was raised in al-Kafi: if someone confesses a slave to another, then buys him, then the slave is claimed from his hand, then he reaches him, then he is ordered to hand him over to the confessee, even if he is legally rendered a liar, because a third party's right is attached to his confession, contrary to the issue of raj'ah. Then know that the permissibility of the husband's approach to her is not dependent on it, contrary to what was corrected in Tahara. The author's phrase there: so the conclusion is that tayammum does not necessitate the permissibility of intercourse, the termination of raj'ah, and her permissibility for husbands except with prayer, according to the correct position of the school. And its correction was transmitted from al-Mabsut and that it is according to all. Then he said: but al-Asbijabi said: and they all agreed that her husband approaches her even if she does not pray, but she does not marry another until she prays. And in the termination of raj'ah, there is disagreement.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This segment of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a case where a husband divorces his wife who is either pregnant or has recently given birth, and he claims that he did not have intercourse with her. The ruling: he still has the right of raj'ah because the pregnancy itself legally establishes that consummation occurred (since pregnancy cannot happen without intercourse). Therefore, his denial is not accepted as it contradicts legal evidence. The commentary contrasts this with a case in al-Kafi where a confession to a slave's ownership binds the confessor even if he becomes legally a liar, because a third party's right is involved. In the case of raj'ah, no third party's right is attached to the husband's statement. The passage also repeats the earlier ruling that for a woman using tayammum, her husband may approach her even without prayer, but she cannot marry another until she prays. The termination of raj'ah in this context is disputed.

Key Terms
مُكَذَّبٌ شَرْعاً (mukadhdhab shar'an): A person whose statement is legally rejected because it contradicts established legal evidence.
إِقْرَار (iqrar): A legal confession or admission, which can bind the confessor even if they are otherwise deemed unreliable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man divorces his pregnant wife and claims he never had intercourse with her. According to this passage, does he have the right of raj'ah?
A) No, because he denied intercourse
B) Yes, because pregnancy legally proves consummation
C) No, because divorce without consummation does not allow raj'ah
D) Yes, because his claim is accepted

Key Takeaway
Pregnancy is a legal proof of consummation that overrides a husband's denial; thus, his right of raj'ah remains intact.

Divorcing a Pregnant Wife and Denying Intercourse: Right of Raj'a
Ruling that a husband who divorces his pregnant wife and denies intercourse still has the right to raj'a, with a discussion of the principle of 'legally contradicted'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a husband who divorces his pregnant wife and denies intercourse still has the right of raj'a, and apply the principle of being legally contradicted (mukadhdhab shar'an) to other fiqh scenarios.

Core Idea
Pregnancy establishes paternity legally; a husband's denial of intercourse is overridden by the shari'a's indication.

In this passage, the commentator discusses a nuanced scenario: a husband divorces his pregnant wife or a wife who has recently given birth, and then claims he never had intercourse with her. At first glance, this might seem to nullify his right to take her back (raj'a), but the Hanafi school rules otherwise. The key lies in the shari'a's treatment of pregnancy as legal proof of prior intercourse, even if the husband denies it. This lesson will walk you through the reasoning and the principle of being 'legally contradicted' (mukadhdhab shar'an).

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the husband's right of raj'a after divorcing a pregnant wife when he denies intercourse. The ruling is that he still has the right.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is that the shari'a establishes paternity through pregnancy (if the child is born within six months of divorce), which indicates previous intercourse. This makes the husband's denial legally contradicted.
The dalil is not a specific verse but the legal principle derived from various proofs.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The shari'a's indication of intercourse through pregnancy is stronger than a human statement. The husband's denial is rejected because it conflicts with this established legal fact.
This is an application of the rule: 'The indication of the shari'a is stronger than the explicit statement of a person.'
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Sadr al-Shari'a objected, arguing pregnancy is only proven after birth, so no contradiction exists before delivery. The commentator rebuts this by citing evidence from other chapters (option of defect, lineage) that pregnancy is legally recognized before delivery.
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute; the dominant view (mufta bihi) is that raj'a is valid immediately.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The majority of Hanafi scholars (mashayikh) and later authorities uphold the right of raj'a before birth, relying on stronger proofs. The objection is considered weak or based on weaker narrations.
The practical effect: the husband can revoke the divorce at any time during the iddah, even if he denies intercourse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

بأن ولدت لأقل من ستة أشهر فلم يلتفت إلى قوله لم أطأها لأنه صار مكذباً شرعاً ومن صار مكذباً شرعاً بطل زعمه ما لم يتعلق بإقراره حق الغير فلا يرد ما أورده في الكافي بأن من أقر بعبد لآخر ثم اشتراه ثم استحق من يده ثم وصل إليه فإنه يؤمر بالتسليم إلى المقر له وإن صار مكذباً شرعاً لكونه تعلق بإقراره حق الغير بخلاف مسألة الرجعة. ثم اعلم أن حل قربان الزوج لها غير متوقف عليها الخ مخالف لما مر تصحيحه في الرجعة فيكون المراد أنه راجع قبل وضع الحمل فولدت لأقل من ستة أشهر يحكم بصحة الرجعة السابقة ولا يراد أنه يحل له الرجعة قبل وضع الحمل لأنه لما أنكر الوطء والشرع لا يحكم بوجود الحمل وقت الطلاق بل إنما يحكم به إذا ولدت لأقل . ستة أشهر من وقت الطلاق فلم يوجد تكذيب الشرع قبل وضع الحمل، فالصواب أن يقال : من قوله : (فاندفع ما اعترض به صدر الشريعة (الخ) رده المقدسي في شرحه فإنه قال بعد ما نقل كلام الصدر : وهذا تحقيق بالقبول حقيق وقول من رده بأن الحمل يثبت قبل الوضع ويثبت النسب به قبله مردود، أما ما استدل به في باب خيار العيب فرواية ضعيفة عن محمد أنه يرد بشهادة المرأة بالعيب. وعن أبي يوسف روايتان أظهرهما أنه إنما يقبل قولهما للخصومة لا للرد، وأما ما في باب ثبوت النسب من قولهم الحمل الظاهر فإنما يثبت النسب بالفراش والولادة بقول المرأة والخلاف هنا معروف أن أبا حنيفة رحمه الله يقول : إذا جحد الزوج ولادة المعتدة لا يثبت إلا بشهادة رجلين أو رجل وامرأتين إلا أن يكون الحمل ظاهراً فيثبت معه بشهادة المرأة وهي القابلة فليس في هذا أن
His statement: (And if he divorces one with pregnancy or a child and says 'I did not have intercourse with her', he has the right of raj'a) means if he divorces his wife while she is pregnant or after she has given birth while in his custody, and he says 'I did not have intercourse with her', then he has the right of raj'a because it is built on consummation, and it has been established legally due to the establishment of lineage, because it is established by the appearance of pregnancy when she gives birth at less than six months, so his statement 'I did not have intercourse' is not heeded because he has become legally contradicted. And whoever becomes legally contradicted, his claim is nullified, as long as it does not involve the right of another due to his acknowledgement. Therefore, what is mentioned in al-Kafi does not contradict: if a person acknowledges a slave to another, then buys it, then it is claimed from his hand, then it comes to him, he is ordered to hand it over to the acknowledged one, even though he became legally contradicted, because the right of another is attached to his acknowledgement, unlike the issue of raj'a. Then know that the permissibility of the husband approaching her is not dependent on it... contrary to what was corrected earlier in the chapter of purification. The statement of the author there: the conclusion is that tayammum does not permit intercourse with her nor end raj'a nor make her permissible for other husbands except by prayer according to the correct view of the madhhab, and its correction is transmitted from al-Mabsut and that it is agreed upon. Then he said: But al-Asbijabi said: They agreed that her husband may approach her even if she does not pray, but she may not marry another husband until she prays. And concerning the termination of raj'a, there is disagreement. It does not become... from the branches of the aforementioned principle: if the seller and buyer differ over the price of real estate, and the buyer says 'I bought it for a thousand' and the seller says 'I sold it for two thousand' and the seller provides evidence, then the pre-emptor takes it for two thousand because the judge contradicted the buyer in his acknowledgment. Among its branches also: if the buyer acknowledges the seller's ownership, then the sold item is claimed from his hand by evidence, then he has recourse against the seller for the price because he became contradicted in his acknowledgment when the judge ruled for the claimant. Both branches are in al-Khulasa. Also from it, what is in al-Talkhis: if a specific suretyship is claimed against him and he denies, then the plaintiff provides evidence and the judge rules against the surety, then he has recourse against the debtor if it was at his request, according to us, because he became contradicted in his denial when the judge ruled accordingly. And in al-Khulasa, the aforementioned principle in the chapter of qada from its third section is restricted to when the ruling is based on evidence. But if the judge rules by istishab al-hal (presumption of continuity), then he is not contradicted. For example, if he buys a slave and acknowledges that the seller freed him before the sale, and the seller denies, and the judge rules for the price against the buyer, the buyer's acknowledgment of the emancipation is not invalidated, so the slave is freed upon him. Similarly, if the debtor claims payment or discharge from the creditor, and the creditor denies and takes an oath, and the judge rules for the debt against the debtor, the debtor does not become contradicted, so if evidence of payment or discharge appears, it is accepted. So it was an indication of intercourse, and the indication of the shari'a is stronger than the explicit statement of the slave because liars are possible from the slave but not from the Lawgiver. From what we have established, it is known that pregnancy is established before delivery, and lineage is established through it before delivery, as they clarified in the chapter of option of defect: the pregnancy of a slave girl for sale is established by its appearance before delivery by the testimony of one woman, so the buyer may return her due to the defect of pregnancy before delivery. And in the chapter of establishing lineage, it is established by visible pregnancy. Thus the objection raised by Sadr al-Shari'a against the mashayikh—that their statement 'he has the right of raj'a' is lax because the existence of pregnancy at the time of divorce is only known if she gives birth at less than six months from the time of divorce. When she gives birth, the waiting period has ended, so he does not have the right of raj'a. Hence the meaning is that he declared raj'a before delivery, then she gave birth at less than six months, so the previous raj'a is ruled valid. It does not mean he has the right of raj'a before delivery because he denied intercourse and the shari'a does not rule on the existence of pregnancy at the time of divorce; rather, it only rules if she gives birth at less than six months from the time of divorce, so there is no contradiction from the shari'a before delivery. So it is correct to say: From his statement: (Thus the objection of Sadr al-Shari'a is repelled) al-Maqdisi replied in his commentary: after quoting the words of Sadr, he said: This is a plausible investigation, but the view of those who refute it—that pregnancy is established before delivery and lineage through it before delivery—is rejected. As for what he adduced from the chapter of option of defect, it is a weak narration from Muhammad that she may return based on a woman's testimony (of pregnancy). And from Abu Yusuf, there are two narrations, the stronger being that their (women's) testimony is only accepted in a legal dispute, not for return. As for what is in the chapter of lineage establishment concerning visible pregnancy, lineage is only established by the marriage bed and childbirth by the woman's statement, and the difference here is known: Abu Hanifa said: if the husband denies the childbirth of a woman in her waiting period, it is not established except by the testimony of two men or one man and two women, unless the pregnancy is visible, then it is established with the testimony of one woman, the midwife. So in this, there is no...
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a matn from Kanz al-Daqa'iq concerning a man who divorces his wife while she is pregnant or after childbirth, and he claims he never had intercourse with her (i.e., he is denying the paternity). The ruling is that he still has the right of raj'a because the shari'a establishes paternity through the pregnancy (if the child is born less than six months after the divorce, it confirms intercourse before divorce). Thus, the husband's denial is legally contradicted (mukadhdhab shar'an). The principle is: whoever is contradicted by the shari'a has his claim nullified, unless the claim involves a third-party right based on his own acknowledgment. The text then provides examples from other areas of fiqh to illustrate when a person becomes legally contradicted or not, such as the buyer-seller dispute, surety cases, and acknowledgment of ownership. The passage then addresses an objection by Sadr al-Shari'a, who argued that the husband's right to raj'a before delivery is problematic because pregnancy is only established after delivery (if less than six months), so there is no contradiction before delivery. The rebuttal, quoting al-Maqdisi, insists that pregnancy is established before delivery (for legal purposes) based on evidence from the chapters of option of defect and lineage. The objection is rebutted by questioning the strength of the proofs: the report from Muhammad about returning a slave girl due to pregnancy is weak, and Abu Yusuf's narrations restrict the testimony of women to dispute resolution, not return. Furthermore, lineage is established through the marriage bed and childbirth, not merely by visible pregnancy. The debate highlights the complexity of establishing pregnancy and lineage in Hanafi law.

Key Terms
مكذب شرعاً (mukadhdhab shar‘an): A person whose statement is rejected because the shari'a explicitly contradicts it, such as a husband denying intercourse when pregnancy is established.
الرجعة (al-raj‘a): The husband's right to resume marital relations without a new contract during the waiting period of a revocable divorce.
الحمل الظاهر (al-ḥaml al-ẓāhir): A visible pregnancy that is apparent to others, as opposed to an earlier stage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the wife confirms intercourse
B) Because the pregnancy legally establishes paternity, so his denial is rejected
C) Because the judge always accepts the husband's raj'a regardless
D) Because the waiting period is not affected by denial

Key Takeaway
A husband's denial of intercourse is legally overridden when pregnancy establishes paternity, preserving his right of raj'a.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate
10 min
Raj'a Valid Despite Denial of Intercourse if Birth Within Six Months
Explains the ruling on raj'a when husband denies intercourse but pregnancy appears

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on raj'a when the husband denies intercourse and explain the legal reasoning based on the principle of being 'mukadhdhib' and the strength of dalala (legal indication).

Core Idea
The Law's indication (dalala) overrides a husband's denial of intercourse for the purpose of raj'a.

In this lesson, we examine a nuanced case in Hanafi family law: a husband divorces his pregnant wife while denying that he had intercourse with her. Can he validly revoke the divorce (raj'a)? The answer hinges on the interaction between the husband's explicit denial, the apparent pregnancy, and the legal presumption of continuity (istishab). This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies the ruling and responds to a critical objection raised by Sadr al-Shari'a.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue and Hanafi ruling
The central question: if a husband denies intercourse with his wife, divorces her, and she later gives birth, is his revocation (raj'a) valid? The Hanafi ruling is yes, provided she gives birth within six months of the divorce.
Six months is the minimum duration of pregnancy recognized by Hanafi law.
2
Understand the principle of being 'mukadhdhib' by court ruling
The passage first establishes a general principle: when a judge rules based on evidence (bayyina), the party whose statement contradicts the ruling is deemed a liar (mukadhdhib) in that legal context. Various examples from sales and suretyship illustrate this.
This principle applies only when the judge rules by evidence, not by istishab.
3
Distinguish between ruling by evidence and by istishab
If the judge rules by istishab al-hal (presumption of continuity), the contradicting party is not considered a liar. The passage gives the example of a buyer acknowledging emancipation: the judge's price ruling by istishab does not make the buyer mukadhdhib in his acknowledgment.
Istishab is a weaker proof; it does not contradict a party's claim.
4
Apply the principle to the husband's denial and pregnancy
The Law's indication (dalala) of intercourse through pregnancy is treated as a form of evidence. Since the Lawgiver does not lie, this indication is stronger than the husband's explicit denial. Thus, when the wife gives birth within six months, the legal system treats the husband as if his denial is overridden, and raj'a is valid retroactively.
The dalala here is the physical fact of pregnancy, which the Law accepts as proof of intercourse for certain rulings.
5
Evaluate the critical refinement by al-Maqdisi
Sadr al-Shari'a objected that pregnancy is not legally established until birth, so raj'a before birth is premature. Al-Maqdisi responds by distinguishing between the establishment of pregnancy for different purposes: pregnancy is legally considered for some effects (like lineage and return of a slave-girl) even before birth, based on apparent pregnancy and the testimony of one woman. For raj'a, the ruling is retrospective: if birth occurs within six months, the previous revocation is confirmed valid.
Al-Maqdisi also notes that even Abu Yusuf has differing narrations on the strength of a woman's testimony for pregnancy, but the relied-upon view supports the ruling.

تساهل لأن وجود الحمل وقت الطلاق إنما يعرف إذا ولدت لأقل من ستة أشهر من وقت الطلاق فإذا ولدت انقضت العدة فلا يملك الرجعة فيكون المراد أنه راجع قبل وضع الحمل فولدت لأقل من ستة أشهر يحكم بصحة الرجعة الظاهر فإنما يثبت النسب بالفراش والولادة بقول المرأة والخلاف هنا معروف أن أبا حنيفة رحمه الله يقول : إذا جحد الزوج ولادة المعتدة لا يثبت إلا بشهادة رجلين أو رجل وامرأتين إلا أن يكون الحمل ظاهراً فيثبت معه بشهادة المرأة وهي القابلة فليس في هذا أن الحمل يثبت وإنما ظهوره يؤيد شهادة المرأة، وأما ثبوته فمتوقف على الولادة كما نص عليه في المبسوط فيما لو قال إن حبلت فطالق فقال لو وطئها مرة فالأفضل أن لا يقربها ثم قال : إن أتت بولد بعد قوله المذكور لأكثر من سنتين يقع الطلاق وتنقضي العدة بالولد فلم يثبته إلا بالولادة على الوجه المخصوص وظهوره لا يسمى ثبوتاً ولا يترتب عليه ما يتوقف على الثبوت ا هـ .
It does not become from the branches of the mentioned principle: the case when the seller and buyer dispute the price of a property, the buyer says 'I bought it for a thousand' and the seller says 'I sold it for two thousand' and the seller produces evidence, then the pre-emptor takes it for two thousand because the judge considered the buyer a liar in his acknowledgment. And from its branches also: if the buyer acknowledges ownership to the seller, then the sold item is claimed from his hand by evidence, then he has recourse to the seller for the price because he became a liar in his acknowledgment when the judge ruled for the claimant. Both branches are in al-Khulasa. And from it is what is in al-Talkhis: if someone claims a specific surety against him and he denies it, then the claimant provides proof and the judge rules against the surety, then he has recourse against the debtor if it was by his order according to us, because he became a liar in his denial when the judge ruled against him. And it is restricted in al-Khulasa: the mentioned principle in the book of Qada from its third chapter that the ruling must be by evidence. As for when the judge rules by istishab al-hal (presumption of continuity), he is not considered a liar, as in the case of someone who buys a slave and acknowledges that the seller emancipated him before the sale, and the seller denies it, and the judge rules for the price against the buyer, the buyer's acknowledgment of emancipation is not invalidated, so the slave becomes free for him. Similarly, if the debtor claims payment or release against the creditor, and the creditor denies and swears, and the judge rules for the debt against the debtor, the debtor does not become a liar, so if evidence of payment or release is found, it is accepted. So the indication (dalala) of intercourse and the indication of the law are stronger than the explicit statement of the slave due to the possibility of lying from the slave but not from the Lawgiver. It is known from what we have established that pregnancy is established before delivery, and lineage is established by it before delivery, as they explicitly stated in the chapter of option for defect: that the pregnancy of a sold slave-girl is established by its appearance before delivery through the testimony of one woman, so the buyer has the right to return her due to the defect of pregnancy before delivery. And in the chapter of establishing lineage, it is established by apparent pregnancy. So the objection of Sadr al-Shari'a against the scholars that their statement 'he has the right of raj'a' is laxity is repelled, because the existence of pregnancy at the time of divorce is only known if she gives birth in less than six months from the time of divorce. If she gives birth, the waiting period ends, so he does not have the right of raj'a. Thus the meaning is that he revoked before delivery, and she gave birth in less than six months, so the previous revocation is ruled valid. It does not mean that he is allowed to revoke before delivery, because since he denied intercourse, and the law does not rule on the existence of pregnancy at the time of divorce, but only rules if she gives birth in less than six months from the time of divorce, so no legal contradiction exists before delivery. So the correct view is to say: from his statement: 'So the objection of Sadr al-Shari'a is repelled' etc., al-Maqdisi replied to him in his commentary, saying after quoting the words of al-Sadr: This is an acceptable investigation, and the statement of one who refutes it by claiming that pregnancy is established before delivery and lineage is established by it before delivery is rejected. As for what he used as evidence from the chapter of option for defect, it is a weak narration from Muhammad that he returns her based on the woman's testimony of the defect. And from Abu Yusuf there are two narrations, the more apparent being that their statement is accepted for the dispute, not for return. As for what is in the chapter of establishing lineage from their statement 'apparent pregnancy', lineage is only established by the marriage bed and birth with the woman's statement. The dispute here is known: Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, says: if the husband denies the birth of the waiting woman, it is only established by the testimony of two men or one man and two women, unless the pregnancy is apparent, then it is established with the testimony of a woman, i.e., the midwife. There is no proof here that pregnancy is established; rather, its appearance supports the woman's testimony. As for its establishment, it is contingent on birth, as stated in al-Mabsut regarding the case where he says 'if you become pregnant, you are divorced' - he said: if he had intercourse with her once, it is better not to approach her. Then he said: if she gives birth after the said statement after more than two years, divorce occurs and the waiting period ends with the child, so he only establishes it by birth in the specific manner, and its appearance is not called establishment, nor are consequences that depend on establishment attached to it. End quote.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement addressing the objection of Sadr al-Shari'a regarding the validity of raja when a husband denies intercourse with his pregnant wife. The author first discusses the broader principle of being 'mukadhdhib' (considered a liar) by a court ruling, illustrated with examples from sales, suretyship, and acknowledgments. The key point is that when a judge rules based on evidence (bayyina), the party who contradicts the ruling is deemed a liar in that context. However, if the judge rules by istishab al-hal (presumption of continuity), no such contradiction applies. The author then applies this to the case at hand: the law's indication (dalala) of intercourse through pregnancy is stronger than the husband's explicit denial, because the Lawgiver does not lie. Thus, pregnancy is established before birth for purposes of lineage and raja. Sadr al-Shari'a objected that raja before birth is meaningless because the waiting period only ends at birth, but the scholars respond that raja is valid if she gives birth within six months, retroactively confirming its validity. Al-Maqdisi refutes the objection further, arguing that pregnancy is not established before birth; rather, it is only through birth that it becomes certain. He cites weak narrations from Muhammad and differing views from Abu Yusuf to support that apparent pregnancy does not constitute legal establishment. The practical ruling remains that a man who denies intercourse but divorces his pregnant wife can validly revoke the divorce if she gives birth within six months.

Key Terms
مكذب (mukadhdhib): A person whose statement is contradicted by a legal ruling, typically when a judge rules against his claim or acknowledgment.
دلالة (dalala): Legal indication or proof derived from a text or situation, in this case the apparent pregnancy indicating intercourse.
استصحاب الحال (istishab al-hal): Presumption of continuity of a previous state until something changes it; used here as a method of judicial ruling that does not make a person mukadhdhib.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Immediately after the divorce before birth.
B) Only if he admits to intercourse before the divorce.
C) If she gives birth within six months of the divorce.
D) If she gives birth after six months.

Key Takeaway
The Law's indication (dalala) of pregnancy overrides a husband's explicit denial for the purpose of raj'a, provided the birth occurs within six months of the divorce.

intermediate

Khalwa Without Intercourse: No Raj'a
States the ruling that a husband who denies intercourse despite khalwa has no raj'a

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a husband denies intercourse after khalwa and then divorces, and explain why his admission negates the right of raj'a.

Core Idea
Admission of no intercourse after khalwa nullifies the right of raj'a because the husband is believed regarding his own rights.

In this passage, the commentator explains the legal consequence when a husband is alone with his wife (khalwa) but later denies intercourse before divorcing. The key point is that while khalwa normally suggests intercourse, the husband's own admission overrides that presumption for the purpose of raj'a. Understanding this ruling requires distinguishing between the proof value of khalwa and the binding nature of a person's admission against his own interest.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the ruling
The husband is alone with his wife (khalwa), then says he did not have intercourse, then divorces her. The ruling: he does not have the right of raj'a.
2
Understand the rationale
Raj'a is a right that strengthens with actual intercourse. Since he admits no intercourse, he is believed regarding his own rights, and his admission is binding on himself.
His admission does not affect others' rights, like the waiting period which is precautionary.
3
Distinguish between legal effects
The waiting period is still obligatory as a precaution, but that does not mean the judge has ruled that intercourse occurred. The confirmation of dower is based on delivery, not receipt.
Khalwa alone does not legally establish intercourse for all purposes.
4
Contrast the case where husband claims intercourse
If the husband says he had intercourse and the wife denies, then he does have raj'a because the apparent circumstance (khalwa) supports his claim.
If there was no khalwa, the apparent circumstance supports the wife, so no raj'a.
5
Extract the general principle
The apparent circumstance (al-zahir) is a proof for repelling an entitlement. The husband's statement is sufficient to preserve his ownership and repel the wife's claim to herself.
This shows how Hanafi fiqh balances presumptions and admissions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وإن خلا بها وقال لم أجامعها ثم طلقها لا أي لا يملك الرجعة لأن الملك يتأكد بالوطء وقد أقر بعدمه فيصدق في حق نفسه والرجعة حقه ولم يصر مكذباً شرعاً لأن تأكيد المهر المسمى يبتنى على تسليم المبدل لا على القبض والعدة تجب احتياطاً لاحتمال الوطء فلم يكن القضاء بها قضاء بالدخول قيد بإنكاره الجماع لأنه لو قال جامعتها وأنكرت المرأة فله الرجعة لأن الظاهر شاهد له فإن الخلوة دلالة الدخول فإن لم يخل بها فلا رجعة له عليها لأن الظاهر شاهد لها كذا في الولوالجية. وفي المبسوط فإن قيل الظاهر حجة لدفع الاستحقاق والزوج إنما يريد استحقاق الرجعة بقوله قلنا : ليس كذلك بل الزوج إنما يستبقي ملكه بما يقول ويدفع استحقاقها نفسها والظاهر يكفي لذلك
His statement: 'And if he was alone with her and said, I did not have intercourse with her, then he divorced her, no' i.e., he does not possess the right of raj'a because the right of possession is strengthened by intercourse, and he has acknowledged its absence, so he is believed regarding himself, and raj'a is his right, and he does not become legally a liar because the confirmation of the specified dower is based on the delivery of the counter-value, not on receipt, and the waiting period is obligatory as a precaution due to the possibility of intercourse, so the ruling of the waiting period is not a ruling of entry. It is restricted by his denial of intercourse because if he said, 'I had intercourse with her' and the woman denies it, then he has the right of raj'a because the apparent circumstance testifies for him; for khalwa is an indication of entry. But if he did not have khalwa, then he has no raj'a over her because the apparent circumstance testifies for her. This is in al-Walwalijiyya. And in al-Mabsut: if it is said that the apparent circumstance is a proof for repelling entitlement, and the husband only wants to establish the right of raj'a by his statement, we say: it is not so; rather, the husband only preserves his ownership by what he says and repels her entitlement to herself, and the apparent circumstance is sufficient for that.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a statement from the base text (Kanz al-Daqa'iq) regarding a husband who was alone with his wife (khalwa) but claims he did not have intercourse, then divorces her. The ruling is that he does not have the right of raj'a (no). The reason is that the husband's right to raj'a is based on the strength of the marital bond, which is confirmed by actual intercourse. Since he admits no intercourse occurred, he is believed regarding himself, and thus he cannot claim raj'a. The waiting period ('idda) is still required as a precaution, but that does not imply that the judge has ruled that intercourse took place. The author notes that this applies only when the husband denies intercourse. If he claims he did have intercourse and the wife denies it, then he does have raj'a because the apparent circumstance (khalwa) supports his claim. If there was no khalwa, the apparent circumstance supports the wife, so no raj'a. The ruling is based on the principle that the apparent circumstance (zahir) is a proof to repel an entitlement (the wife's claim to be free from the marriage), and the husband's word is sufficient to preserve his ownership.

Key Terms
الظاهر (al-zahir): The apparent circumstance or external evidence that is considered a legal proof.
الرجعة (al-raj'a): The husband's right to revoke a revocable divorce (talaq raj'i) during the waiting period.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a man is alone with his wife (khalwa) but then says he did not have intercourse, and divorces her, does he have the right of raj'a?
A) Yes, because khalwa is equivalent to intercourse.
B) No, because he admitted no intercourse and is believed regarding himself.
C) Yes, because the waiting period is obligatory.
D) No, because the wife's denial is accepted.

Key Takeaway
A husband's admission of no intercourse after khalwa and before divorce prevents him from claiming the right of raj'a, because his admission is binding on himself and overrides the presumption of khalwa.

Conditional Divorce and Multiple Births as Raj'a
Expands rulings on conditional divorce and raj'a

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whether a subsequent birth during the waiting period constitutes a raj'a in conditional divorce scenarios.

Core Idea
A birth within the waiting period after a conditional divorce is a raj'a, not a new divorce.

In Hanafi law, the timing of births relative to a conditional divorce can determine whether a revocation (raj'a) occurs. This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains three related cases: when a birth validates a previous raj'a, when a second birth from a separate pregnancy constitutes a new raj'a, and when multiple births each act as revocations. The overarching principle is to avoid imputing adultery by presuming the waiting period continues and the child is legitimate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three scenarios
Read the passage and identify each distinct case: (1) raj'a followed by birth within two years, (2) conditional divorce with two separate pregnancies, and (3) repeated conditional divorce with three separate pregnancies.
Note that each scenario involves conditional divorce linked to childbirth.
2
Determine the legal outcome for each scenario
For scenario 1, the raj'a is valid. For scenario 2, the second birth is a raj'a. For scenario 3, the second and third births are each a raj'a.
The outcome hinges on whether the birth occurs during the waiting period.
3
Understand the underlying rationale
The rulings are based on the presumption of legitimacy and the principle that it is lighter to consider the husband truthful (even if it means assuming an earlier conception) than to impute adultery to the wife.
This rationale explains why births beyond two years can still be attributed to the husband if she does not admit the end of the iddah.
4
Apply the ruling to new cases
Given a new scenario, check the conditions: time since divorce, whether the pregnancy is separate, and whether she admits the end of iddah. Then classify the birth as either a valid raj'a or not.
If the births are less than six months apart, they are not separate pregnancies, so no new raj'a.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وإن راجعها ثم ولدت بعدها لأقل من عامين صحت تلك الرجعة) يعني راجعها والمسألة بحالها. والمراد بالصحة ظهور صحة الرجعة السابقة لأن العدة لما وجبت ثبت نسب الولد منه وظهر أن العلوق كان سابقاً على الطلاق فنزل واطئاً قبل الطلاق دون ما بعده لأن على الاعتبار الثاني يزول الملك بنفس الطلاق لعدم الوطء قبله فيحرم الوطء والمسلم لا يفعل الحرام، وهو وإن كان لا يكذب لكن لما لزم أحد الاعتبارين من الزنا أو كذبه فجعله كاذباً أخف من حمله على الزنا قوله : (إن ولدت فأنت طالق فولدت ثم ولدت من بطن آخر فهي رجعة) يعني ثم ولدت بعد ستة أشهر وإن كان أكثر من سنتين إذا لم تقر بانقضاء عدتها لأنه وقع الطلاق عليها بالولد الأول ووجبت العدة فيكون الولد الثاني من علوق حادث منه في العدة لأنها لم تقر بانقضاء العدة فيصير مراجعاً حملاً لأمرها على الصلاح كما إذا طلقها رجعياً فجاءت بولد لأكثر من سنتين . قيد بكونه من بطن آخر لأنه لو كان بينهما أقل من ستة أشهر لا يكون رجعة لأن الثاني ليس بحادث بعد الولد الأول كما إذا طلقها رجعياً فجاءت بولد لأقل من سنتين قوله : (كلما ولدت فأنت طالق فولدت ثلاثة في بطون فالولد الثاني والثالث رجعة) لوقوع الطلاق
His statement: 'And if he revoked her and then she gave birth after it in less than two years, that revocation is valid' — meaning he revoked her and the matter is as it is. And by validity is meant the appearance of the validity of the previous revocation, because when the waiting period became obligatory, the lineage of the child is established from him, and it becomes apparent that the conception was before the divorce, so he is considered as having intercourse before the divorce, not after, because on the second consideration, ownership ceases with the divorce itself due to the absence of intercourse before it, so intercourse becomes unlawful, and a Muslim does not do what is unlawful. And although he is not a liar, yet since one of the two considerations — either adultery or his being a liar — is necessitated, making him a liar is lighter than imputing adultery to him. His statement: 'If you give birth, you are divorced, and she gave birth, then she gave birth from another womb, then it is a raj'a' — meaning she gave birth after six months, even if more than two years, if she did not admit the expiry of her waiting period, because the divorce occurred upon her with the first child, and the waiting period became obligatory. So the second child is from a new conception from him during the waiting period, because she did not admit the expiry of the waiting period, so he becomes a revoker by acting on her state as being upright, just as if he divorces her revocably and she gives birth after more than two years. It is restricted by 'from another womb' because if there were less than six months between them, it is not a raj'a, because the second is not a new event after the first child, just as if he divorces her revocably and she gives birth in less than two years. His statement: 'Whenever you give birth, you are divorced, and she gave birth three times in separate pregnancies, then the second and third births are a raj'a' — because divorce occurs [with the first birth].
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage explains three related rulings from the base text. First: if a man revokes his wife (performs raj'a) and then she gives birth within less than two years from that revocation, the revocation is valid. This is because the birth establishes that the child is from him, indicating that conception occurred before the divorce (or at least during the waiting period). The law prefers to consider the husband truthful and the child legitimate rather than impute adultery. Second: in a conditional divorce where the husband says 'if you give birth, you are divorced', if she gives birth and then gives birth again from another pregnancy, the second birth is considered a raj'a (revocation of the divorce) because the waiting period from the first divorce is still ongoing (unless she admits it ended). The condition 'from another womb' means a separate pregnancy; if the births are less than six months apart (impossible), it is not a new pregnancy. Third: if the husband says 'whenever you give birth, you are divorced' and she gives birth three separate times, the second and third births are each a raj'a, because the first birth triggers the divorce, and the subsequent births occur during the waiting period, thus they are revocations.

Key Terms
علوق (uluaq): Conception or pregnancy; the act of becoming pregnant.
بطن (batn): Pregnancy or womb; used to refer to a separate pregnancy (i.e., a different gestation period).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband says to his wife 'if you give birth, you are divorced', and she gives birth once, then becomes pregnant again and gives birth a second time while still in the waiting period, what is the legal status of the second birth according to the Hanafi school?
A) It is a new divorce.
B) It is a revocation (raj'a) of the first divorce.
C) It is ignored.
D) It confirms the first divorce.

Key Takeaway
A birth during the waiting period after a conditional divorce is legally treated as a raj'a, not a new divorce, based on the principle of avoiding adultery.

Raj'ah when Husband Denies Intercourse after Khulwah
Explains ruling on revocation when husband denies intercourse after seclusion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the husband's right of revocation when he denies intercourse after seclusion, and explain the role of apparent evidence (zahir).

Core Idea
Denial of intercourse after seclusion bars revocation unless the husband later claims it; seclusion serves as apparent evidence.

In this lesson, we examine a scenario where a husband secludes with his wife but later denies intercourse, then divorces her. The Hanafi school determines his right of revocation based on the interplay of his admission and the legal presumption from seclusion. Understanding this ruling clarifies how the school balances truthfulness, ownership, and precaution.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the initial ruling
The husband had seclusion (khulwah) but stated he did not have intercourse. He then divorces her. The initial ruling is that he does not have the right of revocation (لا يملك الرجعة).
The husband's admission of no intercourse is binding on himself regarding his right.
2
Understand why he is believed
The husband is believed regarding his own right because revocation is his personal right, and his confession of no intercourse is against his own interest. He is not legally considered a liar because the iddah was imposed as a precaution, not as a proof of intercourse.
The iddah does not prove actual entry; it is precautionary.
3
Contrast with the case where he claims intercourse and she denies
If the husband says he had intercourse and the wife denies, the husband does have the right of revocation. The apparent evidence (zahir) from the seclusion supports his claim.
Khulwah is an indication of intercourse, so the zahir favors the husband.
4
Apply the principle of istihsan
The ruling is based on istihsan (juristic preference) to avoid imputing adultery to the wife. By taking the husband's later claim as truthful when seclusion existed, the school preserves the marriage and avoids accusing the wife of zina.
Imputing falsehood to the husband is lighter than imputing adultery to the wife.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطلاق باب الرجعة

وإن خلا بها وقال لم أجامعها ثم طلقها لا وإن راجعها ثم ولدت بعدها لأقل من عامين صحت تلك الرجعة إن ولدت فأنت طالق فولدت ثم ولدت من بطن آخر فهي رجعة كلما ولدت فأنت طالق فولدت ثلاثة في بطون فالولد الثاني والثالث رجعة

ثم قال: قوله : (وإن خلا بها ثم قال لم أجامعها ثم طلقها (لا أي لا يملك الرجعة لأن الملك يتأكد بالوطء وقد أقر بعدمه فيصدق في حق نفسه والرجعة حقه ولم يصر مكذباً شرعاً لأن تأكيد المهر المسمى يبتنى على تسليم المبدل لا على القبض والعدة تجب احتياطاً لاحتمال الوطء فلم يكن القضاء بها قضاء بالدخول قيد بإنكاره الجماع لأنه لو قال جامعتها وأنكرت المرأة فله الرجعة لأن الظاهر شاهد له فإن الخلوة دلالة الدخول فإن لم يخل بها فلا رجعة له عليها لأن الظاهر شاهد لها كذا في الولوالجية. وفي المبسوط فإن قيل الظاهر حجة لدفع الاستحقاق والزوج إنما يريد استحقاق الرجعة بقوله قلنا : ليس كذلك بل الزوج إنما يستبقي ملكه بما يقول ويدفع استحقاقها نفسها والظاهر يكفي لذلك قوله : (وإن راجعها ثم ولدت بعدها لأقل من عامين صحت تلك الرجعة) يعني راجعها والمسألة بحالها. والمراد بالصحة ظهور صحة الرجعة السابقة لأن العدة لما وجبت ثبت نسب الولد منه وظهر أن العلوق كان سابقاً على الطلاق فنزل واطئاً قبل الطلاق دون ما بعده لأن على الاعتبار الثاني يزول الملك بنفس الطلاق لعدم الوطء قبله فيحرم الوطء والمسلم لا يفعل الحرام، وهو وإن كان لا يكذب لكن لما لزم أحد الاعتبارين من الزنا أو كذبه فجعله كاذباً أخف من حمله على الزنا قوله : (إن ولدت فأنت طالق فولدت ثم ولدت من بطن آخر فهي رجعة) يعني ثم ولدت بعد ستة أشهر وإن كان أكثر من سنتين إذا لم تقر بانقضاء عدتها لأنه وقع الطلاق عليها بالولد الأول ووجبت العدة فيكون الولد الثاني من علوق حادث منه ، في العدة لأنها لم تقر بانقضاء العدة فيصير مراجعاً حملاً لأمرها على الصلاح كما إذا طلقها رجعياً فجاءت بولد لأكثر من سنتين . قيد بكونه من بطن آخر لأنه لو كان بينهما أقل من ستة أشهر لا يكون رجعة لأن الثاني ليس بحادث بعد الولد الأول كما إذا طلقها رجعياً فجاءت بولد لأقل من سنتين قوله : (كلما ولدت فأنت طالق فولدت ثلاثة في بطون فالولد الثاني والثالث رجعة) لوقوع الطلاق بالأول وثبتت الرجعة بالثاني والثالث ويقع بكل طلقة أخرى فتحرم حرمة غليظة ويثبت نسب الأولاد من الزوج وعليها العدة بالأقراء قيد
