Book of Divorce, Chapter of Revocation. And if he had seclusion with her and said 'I did not have intercourse with her' then divorced her: no [he does not have the right of revocation]. And if he revoked her and then she gave birth after that within less than two years, that revocation is valid. 'If you give birth, you are divorced' – she gave birth, then she gave birth from another womb – that is a revocation. 'Whenever you give birth, you are divorced' – she gave birth to three [children] in separate wombs – the second and third birth are revocations. Then he said: His saying: 'And if he had seclusion with her then said I did not have intercourse with her then divorced her' – no, meaning he does not own the right of revocation because ownership is confirmed by intercourse and he has admitted to its absence, so he is believed regarding his own right and revocation is his right, and he is not considered a liar legally, because the confirmation of the specified dower is based on delivery of the substitute not on receipt, and the waiting period (iddah) is required as a precaution due to the possibility of intercourse, so the ruling of iddah is not a ruling of actual entry. It is restricted by his denial of intercourse because if he said 'I had intercourse with her' and the woman denied it, then he has the right of revocation because apparent evidence (zahir) is a witness for him, for seclusion is an indication of entry. If he did not have seclusion with her, then he has no right of revocation against her because apparent evidence is a witness for her. Thus in al-Wilwaljiyyah. And in al-Mabsut: If it is said that apparent evidence is an argument to repel entitlement and the husband only wants to claim the right of revocation by his statement, we say: It is not so. Rather, the husband only preserves his ownership by what he says and repels her entitlement to herself, and apparent evidence suffices for that. His saying: 'And if he revoked her then she gave birth within less than two years, that revocation is valid' – meaning he revoked her and the case remains as is. And what is meant by validity is the appearance of the validity of the previous revocation because when the iddah became obligatory, the child's lineage from him was established and it became clear that conception occurred before the divorce, so he is considered as having intercourse before the divorce, not after, because on the second consideration the ownership would have been removed by the divorce itself due to no prior intercourse, making intercourse unlawful, and a Muslim does not commit the unlawful. And although he is not a liar, when one of two considerations (either adultery or his lie) is inevitable, making him a liar is lighter than imputing adultery to him. His saying: 'If you give birth, you are divorced' – she gave birth, then she gave birth from another womb – that is a revocation. Meaning, then she gave birth after six months even if it is more than two years, if she has not admitted to the end of her iddah, because the divorce occurred upon her by the first birth and the iddah became obligatory, so the second child is from a new conception from him during the iddah because she did not admit to the end of the iddah, so he becomes a revoker (muraji') by carrying her matter upon righteousness, just as if he divorced her revocably and she gave birth after more than two years. It is restricted by being from another womb because if the interval between them is less than six months, it is not a revocation because the second is not newly occurring after the first child, just as if he divorced her revocably and she gave birth in less than two years. His saying: 'Whenever you give birth, you are divorced' – she gave birth to three [children] in separate wombs – the second and third births are revocations due to the falling of the divorce by the first, and revocation is established by the second and third, and each additional divorce falls, so a severe prohibition (hurmat ghalizah) occurs, and the lineage of the children is established from the husband, and upon her is iddah by periods (aqra'). It is restricted by 'in separate wombs' meaning between each one a period of pregnancy or more, because if the interval between the two births is less than that, it is not a revocation, and two divorces fall by the first and second, and nothing falls by the third due to the end of the iddah. And if the first two are in one womb and the third in another, one divorce falls by the first only, and the iddah ends by the second, and nothing falls by the third. And if the first is in a womb and the second and third in one womb, two divorces fall by the first and second, and the iddah ends by the third, so nothing falls by it. This is from Fath al-Qadir. And in al-Muhit: If she gives birth to two children in one womb, a divorce falls by the first and not by the second because it coincides with the end of the iddah. And what is meant by the second and third birth being a revocation is that they show the validity of the previous revocation, as we have mentioned that it is interpreted as being by a new intercourse.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq on Kanz al-Daqa'iq discussing various scenarios of revocation (raj'ah) when the husband initially denies intercourse. The author presents rulings for cases where the husband had seclusion (khulwah) but denied intercourse, then later a child is born. The key principle: if the husband admits to seclusion but denies intercourse, he is believed regarding his own right of revocation because it is his right, but he cannot claim revocation because the right is tied to intercourse. However, if he had seclusion, the apparent evidence (zahir) indicates intercourse, so if he later claims intercourse, he is believed. The commentary then addresses cases of revocation followed by birth within two years: the revocation is valid, and the child’s lineage is established, showing that conception occurred before divorce. This avoids imputing adultery to the wife. For the case of conditional divorce ('If you give birth, you are divorced'), if she gives birth multiple times, each birth after six months from the previous is considered a new revocation, unless the iddah has ended. The commentary details various combinations of births in separate wombs and the count of divorces that fall. The ruling from Fath al-Qadir and al-Muhit are cited. Throughout, the reasoning relies on istihsan (juristic preference) and qiyas (analogy) to preserve the husband's right while protecting the wife's honor.

Key Terms
خلوة (khulwah): Lawful seclusion between spouses that presumes intercourse for legal purposes
ظاهر (zahir): Apparent evidence; a legal presumption that holds weight in the absence of contrary proof

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband secludes with his wife, denies intercourse, divorces her, and later claims intercourse, what is the Hanafi ruling on his right of revocation?
A) He cannot revoke because he initially denied.
B) He can revoke because khulwah is apparent evidence for his claim.
C) He can revoke only if the wife admits.
D) He cannot revoke because the iddah has not started.

Key Takeaway
The husband's denial of intercourse after seclusion does not permanently bar revocation; a later claim, supported by the apparent evidence of khulwah, allows him to revoke.

Validity of Revocation when Child is Born within Two Years
Explains validity of revocation when birth occurs within two years

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a revocation made before the birth is validated when the child is born within two years, and identify the legal principle that avoids imputing adultery.

Core Idea
A revocation is validated by the birth of a child within two years, proving conception occurred before divorce.

This lesson addresses a scenario where a husband revokes his revocably divorced wife, and later she gives birth within two years of the divorce. The Hanafi school rules that this retroactively validates the revocation. Understanding this ruling reveals how the school balances evidentiary considerations with the preservation of lineage and marital stability.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the scenario
The husband divorced his wife revocably, then revoked her (raj'ah). After the revocation, she gave birth within two years from the divorce. The question is whether the revocation is valid.
The two-year period is from the date of divorce, not from the revocation.
2
Understand why the revocation is considered valid
The birth within two years shows that conception occurred before the divorce. Therefore, the wife was pregnant at the time of divorce, and the revocation took place during the iddah (which, for a pregnant woman, ends only at delivery). Thus, the revocation was valid.
The iddah of a pregnant woman ends with delivery, so if she delivers within two years, the iddah likely began before the divorce.
3
Analyze the legal reasoning
The Hanafi school uses istihsan: it is better to consider the husband truthful and the child legitimate than to impute adultery to the wife. The alternative would be to say the husband committed the unlawful act (intercourse after divorce) or that the wife committed zina.
The principle: 'جعله كاذباً أخف من حمله على الزنا' (making him a liar is lighter than imputing adultery to her).
4
Apply the two-year rule
The maximum duration of pregnancy in Hanafi fiqh is two years (some say 2 years). Birth within this period from the divorce establishes that the child is from the husband and that the wife was pregnant at divorce.
If birth occurs after two years, the child's lineage is not established, and the revocation may be invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطلاق باب الرجعة

وإن خلا بها وقال لم أجامعها ثم طلقها لا وإن راجعها ثم ولدت بعدها لأقل من عامين صحت تلك الرجعة إن ولدت فأنت طالق فولدت ثم ولدت من بطن آخر فهي رجعة كلما ولدت فأنت طالق فولدت ثلاثة في بطون فالولد الثاني والثالث رجعة

ثم قال: قوله : (وإن خلا بها ثم قال لم أجامعها ثم طلقها (لا أي لا يملك الرجعة لأن الملك يتأكد بالوطء وقد أقر بعدمه فيصدق في حق نفسه والرجعة حقه ولم يصر مكذباً شرعاً لأن تأكيد المهر المسمى يبتنى على تسليم المبدل لا على القبض والعدة تجب احتياطاً لاحتمال الوطء فلم يكن القضاء بها قضاء بالدخول قيد بإنكاره الجماع لأنه لو قال جامعتها وأنكرت المرأة فله الرجعة لأن الظاهر شاهد له فإن الخلوة دلالة الدخول فإن لم يخل بها فلا رجعة له عليها لأن الظاهر شاهد لها كذا في الولوالجية. وفي المبسوط فإن قيل الظاهر حجة لدفع الاستحقاق والزوج إنما يريد استحقاق الرجعة بقوله قلنا : ليس كذلك بل الزوج إنما يستبقي ملكه بما يقول ويدفع استحقاقها نفسها والظاهر يكفي لذلك قوله : (وإن راجعها ثم ولدت بعدها لأقل من عامين صحت تلك الرجعة) يعني راجعها والمسألة بحالها. والمراد بالصحة ظهور صحة الرجعة السابقة لأن العدة لما وجبت ثبت نسب الولد منه وظهر أن العلوق كان سابقاً على الطلاق فنزل واطئاً قبل الطلاق دون ما بعده لأن على الاعتبار الثاني يزول الملك بنفس الطلاق لعدم الوطء قبله فيحرم الوطء والمسلم لا يفعل الحرام، وهو وإن كان لا يكذب لكن لما لزم أحد الاعتبارين من الزنا أو كذبه فجعله كاذباً أخف من حمله على الزنا قوله : (إن ولدت فأنت طالق فولدت ثم ولدت من بطن آخر فهي رجعة) يعني ثم ولدت بعد ستة أشهر وإن كان أكثر من سنتين إذا لم تقر بانقضاء عدتها لأنه وقع الطلاق عليها بالولد الأول ووجبت العدة فيكون الولد الثاني من علوق حادث منه ، في العدة لأنها لم تقر بانقضاء العدة فيصير مراجعاً حملاً لأمرها على الصلاح كما إذا طلقها رجعياً فجاءت بولد لأكثر من سنتين . قيد بكونه من بطن آخر لأنه لو كان بينهما أقل من ستة أشهر لا يكون رجعة لأن الثاني ليس بحادث بعد الولد الأول كما إذا طلقها رجعياً فجاءت بولد لأقل من سنتين قوله : (كلما ولدت فأنت طالق فولدت ثلاثة في بطون فالولد الثاني والثالث رجعة) لوقوع الطلاق بالأول وثبتت الرجعة بالثاني والثالث ويقع بكل طلقة أخرى فتحرم حرمة غليظة ويثبت نسب الأولاد من الزوج وعليها العدة بالأقراء قيد بكونهم في بطون أي بين كل واحد مدة الحمل فأكثر إذ لو كان بين الولادتين أقل منها لا يكون رجعة ويقع طلقتان بالأول والثاني ولا يقع بالثالث شيء لانقضاء العدة به، ولو كان الأولان في بطن والثالث في بطن تقع تطليقة واحدة بالأولى لا غير وتنقضي العدة بالثاني ولا يقع بالثالث شيء، ولو كان الأول في بطن والثاني والثالث في بطن يقع ثنتان بالأول والثاني وتنقضي العدة بالثالث فلا يقع به شيء؛ في فتح القدير. وفي المحيط ولو ولدت ولدين في بطن وقع بالأول ولا يقع بالثاني لمصادفته انقضاء العدة، والمراد من كون الولد الثاني والثالث رجعة أنه ظهر صحة الرجعة السابقة بهما كما قدمناه أنه يحمل على أنه بوطء حادث
Book of Divorce, Chapter of Revocation. And if he had seclusion with her and said 'I did not have intercourse with her' then divorced her: no [he does not have the right of revocation]. And if he revoked her and then she gave birth after that within less than two years, that revocation is valid. 'If you give birth, you are divorced' – she gave birth, then she gave birth from another womb – that is a revocation. 'Whenever you give birth, you are divorced' – she gave birth to three [children] in separate wombs – the second and third birth are revocations. Then he said: His saying: 'And if he had seclusion with her then said I did not have intercourse with her then divorced her' – no, meaning he does not own the right of revocation because ownership is confirmed by intercourse and he has admitted to its absence, so he is believed regarding his own right and revocation is his right, and he is not considered a liar legally, because the confirmation of the specified dower is based on delivery of the substitute not on receipt, and the waiting period (iddah) is required as a precaution due to the possibility of intercourse, so the ruling of iddah is not a ruling of actual entry. It is restricted by his denial of intercourse because if he said 'I had intercourse with her' and the woman denied it, then he has the right of revocation because apparent evidence (zahir) is a witness for him, for seclusion is an indication of entry. If he did not have seclusion with her, then he has no right of revocation against her because apparent evidence is a witness for her. Thus in al-Wilwaljiyyah. And in al-Mabsut: If it is said that apparent evidence is an argument to repel entitlement and the husband only wants to claim the right of revocation by his statement, we say: It is not so. Rather, the husband only preserves his ownership by what he says and repels her entitlement to herself, and apparent evidence suffices for that. His saying: 'And if he revoked her then she gave birth within less than two years, that revocation is valid' – meaning he revoked her and the case remains as is. And what is meant by validity is the appearance of the validity of the previous revocation because when the iddah became obligatory, the child's lineage from him was established and it became clear that conception occurred before the divorce, so he is considered as having intercourse before the divorce, not after, because on the second consideration the ownership would have been removed by the divorce itself due to no prior intercourse, making intercourse unlawful, and a Muslim does not commit the unlawful. And although he is not a liar, when one of two considerations (either adultery or his lie) is inevitable, making him a liar is lighter than imputing adultery to him. His saying: 'If you give birth, you are divorced' – she gave birth, then she gave birth from another womb – that is a revocation. Meaning, then she gave birth after six months even if it is more than two years, if she has not admitted to the end of her iddah, because the divorce occurred upon her by the first birth and the iddah became obligatory, so the second child is from a new conception from him during the iddah because she did not admit to the end of the iddah, so he becomes a revoker (muraji') by carrying her matter upon righteousness, just as if he divorced her revocably and she gave birth after more than two years. It is restricted by being from another womb because if the interval between them is less than six months, it is not a revocation because the second is not newly occurring after the first child, just as if he divorced her revocably and she gave birth in less than two years. His saying: 'Whenever you give birth, you are divorced' – she gave birth to three [children] in separate wombs – the second and third births are revocations due to the falling of the divorce by the first, and revocation is established by the second and third, and each additional divorce falls, so a severe prohibition (hurmat ghalizah) occurs, and the lineage of the children is established from the husband, and upon her is iddah by periods (aqra'). It is restricted by 'in separate wombs' meaning between each one a period of pregnancy or more, because if the interval between the two births is less than that, it is not a revocation, and two divorces fall by the first and second, and nothing falls by the third due to the end of the iddah. And if the first two are in one womb and the third in another, one divorce falls by the first only, and the iddah ends by the second, and nothing falls by the third. And if the first is in a womb and the second and third in one womb, two divorces fall by the first and second, and the iddah ends by the third, so nothing falls by it. This is from Fath al-Qadir. And in al-Muhit: If she gives birth to two children in one womb, a divorce falls by the first and not by the second because it coincides with the end of the iddah. And what is meant by the second and third birth being a revocation is that they show the validity of the previous revocation, as we have mentioned that it is interpreted as being by a new intercourse.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq on Kanz al-Daqa'iq discussing various scenarios of revocation (raj'ah) when the husband initially denies intercourse. The author presents rulings for cases where the husband had seclusion (khulwah) but denied intercourse, then later a child is born. The key principle: if the husband admits to seclusion but denies intercourse, he is believed regarding his own right of revocation because it is his right, but he cannot claim revocation because the right is tied to intercourse. However, if he had seclusion, the apparent evidence (zahir) indicates intercourse, so if he later claims intercourse, he is believed. The commentary then addresses cases of revocation followed by birth within two years: the revocation is valid, and the child’s lineage is established, showing that conception occurred before divorce. This avoids imputing adultery to the wife. For the case of conditional divorce ('If you give birth, you are divorced'), if she gives birth multiple times, each birth after six months from the previous is considered a new revocation, unless the iddah has ended. The commentary details various combinations of births in separate wombs and the count of divorces that fall. The ruling from Fath al-Qadir and al-Muhit are cited. Throughout, the reasoning relies on istihsan (juristic preference) and qiyas (analogy) to preserve the husband's right while protecting the wife's honor.

Key Terms
إستحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference; a method of legal reasoning that departs from qiyas to achieve a more equitable result
علوق (uluq): Conception; the beginning of pregnancy

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is a revocation validated when the wife gives birth within two years of the divorce?
A) Because the husband must have had intercourse after the divorce.
B) Because the birth proves she was pregnant at divorce, so the iddah was ongoing.
C) Because the two-year rule automatically extends the iddah.
D) Because the wife's testimony is accepted.

Key Takeaway
A revocation is validated by the birth of a child within two years, as it establishes that the wife was pregnant and the iddah was still running.

Talaq — Divorce
advanced
12 min
Multiple Births and Revocation in Conditional Divorce
Explains rulings on multiple births in conditional divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine which births constitute a revocation in a conditional divorce, considering intervals and separate wombs, and calculate the number of divorces that fall.

Core Idea
Each birth after six months from the previous one is a new revocation, unless the iddah has been completed by a prior birth.

This lesson explores a conditional divorce where the husband says 'Whenever you give birth, you are divorced.' The wife gives birth multiple times. The Hanafi school explains which births count as revocations and how many divorces occur. The rulings depend on whether the births are in separate wombs, the intervals, and the completion of the iddah. This detailed analysis sharpens your understanding of how iddah interacts with conditional divorce.

Guided Path
1
Understand the conditional divorce formula
The husband says 'كلما ولدت فأنت طالق' (Whenever you give birth, you are divorced). This is a conditional divorce that is triggered by each birth event.
Each time she gives birth, a divorce falls (if she is still in iddah).
2
Identify the first birth and its effect
The first birth triggers a divorce. At that point, the iddah begins (if not already). The first birth itself ends the iddah if she was pregnant at divorce? Actually, for a pregnant woman, iddah ends with delivery. So after the first birth, the iddah ends. But if the first birth occurred after a revocable divorce? The text assumes she was divorced revocably and then the conditional divorce is spoken. The first birth triggers one divorce.
The iddah after the first birth is by periods (aqra'), not by delivery, because she is no longer pregnant.
3
Determine when subsequent births count as revocations
The second birth (from a separate womb) occurring at least six months after the first is considered a new revocation. The third birth similarly, provided the iddah from the second divorce has not ended.
The minimum interval is six months (duration of pregnancy). If less, it is not a new conception.
4
Account for separate wombs and intervals
If births are in separate wombs (i.e., separate pregnancies), each birth after a full term is a new event. If two births occur in one womb (twins), only the first causes a divorce; the second does nothing because the iddah ended with the first.
The text details various combinations: first two in one womb, third in another; or first in one, next two in one; etc.

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq on Kanz al-Daqa'iq discussing various scenarios of revocation (raj'ah) when the husband initially denies intercourse. The author presents rulings for cases where the husband had seclusion (khulwah) but denied intercourse, then later a child is born. The key principle: if the husband admits to seclusion but denies intercourse, he is believed regarding his own right of revocation because it is his right, but he cannot claim revocation because the right is tied to intercourse. However, if he had seclusion, the apparent evidence (zahir) indicates intercourse, so if he later claims intercourse, he is believed. The commentary then addresses cases of revocation followed by birth within two years: the revocation is valid, and the child’s lineage is established, showing that conception occurred before divorce. This avoids imputing adultery to the wife. For the case of conditional divorce ('If you give birth, you are divorced'), if she gives birth multiple times, each birth after six months from the previous is considered a new revocation, unless the iddah has ended. The commentary details various combinations of births in separate wombs and the count of divorces that fall. The ruling from Fath al-Qadir and al-Muhit are cited. Throughout, the reasoning relies on istihsan (juristic preference) and qiyas (analogy) to preserve the husband's right while protecting the wife's honor.

Key Terms
بطن (batn): Womb; used metaphorically for a separate pregnancy or delivery
حرمة غليظة (hurmat ghalizah): Severe prohibition that results from three divorces, making the wife irrevocably divorced and requiring an intervening marriage (tahlil) for remarriage

Checkpoint
A husband says 'كلما ولدت فأنت طالق'. Wife gives birth to a child, then 7 months later gives birth to another child from a separate womb. How many divorces have fallen?
A) One divorce
B) Two divorces
C) Three divorces
D) No divorce because the condition is not fulfilled

Key Takeaway
In conditional divorce, each subsequent birth from a separate womb after at least six months is a new revocation and triggers another divorce.

Rulings for the Revocably Divorced Woman: Adornment and Entry Without Permission
Explains the rulings on adornment and entry for the revocably divorced woman with conditions and reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a revocably divorced woman's adornment for her husband, identify the three conditions for its permissibility, and explain the recommended procedure for the husband's entry.

Core Idea
A revocably divorced woman may adorn for her husband if revocation is hoped for; he should inform her before entering.

Divorce does not immediately sever the marriage bond. In a revocable divorce, the husband can return to his wife during her iddah without a new contract. To encourage this reconciliation, the Hanafi school permits the woman to adorn herself for her husband—under certain conditions. At the same time, the husband should act with decorum: he should not enter upon her without notice, to avoid accidental revocation or harm. This lesson unpacks the conditions and reasoning behind these two rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first ruling and its legal basis
State the ruling on adornment: the revocably divorced woman may adorn herself for her husband. Understand that the basis (illa) is encouraging revocation, which is recommended (mandub).
The text says al-tazayyun hamilun 'alayha (adornment encourages it).
2
Trace the three restrictions
The commentator lists three restrictions: (1) only for her husband, (2) only if the divorce is revocable (not ba'in or widow), (3) only if there is hope of revocation. Explain the rationale for each.
Absent husband → no adornment; ba'in divorce → no marriage left; no hope → pointless.
3
Analyse the husband's right and the entry etiquette
Note that the husband may demand adornment, and it is recommended that he inform her before entering, whether he intends revocation or not.
The rule is general; not restricted to intent to revoke.
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi difference
Abu Yusuf disliked kissing and touching without desire if the husband does not intend revocation. Understand the reasoning and the dominant view that the entry ruling remains general.
The commentator cites al-Wilwaljiyyah for the unrestricted view.
5
Apply the rulings to practical scenarios
Test your understanding with examples: a woman divorced with first talaq, a woman divorced with third talaq (ba'in), a woman whose husband is abroad, etc.
Distinguish between revocable and irrevocable cases.

His saying: 'And the revocably divorced woman adorns herself' – meaning for her husband when revocation is hoped for, because she is lawful for the husband since the marriage is still in effect between them. Then revocation is recommended, and adornment encourages it, so it is legislated. We restricted it to being for her husband because if he is absent, she does not adorn due to the absence of the reason. And we restricted it to the revocably divorced because the woman in iddah from an irrevocable divorce (ba'in) may not adorn at all due to the prohibition of looking at her and the lack of recommendation of revocation. Thus in Ghayat al-Bayan. And the widow in iddah is excluded, for she mourns. And we restricted it to being hoped for because if she knows that he will not revoke her due to intense hatred, she does not do it, as mentioned in Sharh Miskin. And they explicitly stated that the husband may beat his wife for leaving off adornment if he requests it from her, because it is his right, and this includes the revocably divorced woman.

His saying: 'And it is recommended that he not enter upon her until he informs her' – meaning he makes his entry known to her, either by striking his sandals, clearing his throat, calling out, or the like. He gave it generally, covering both cases: whether he intends revocation or not. In the first case, he is not safe from seeing her private parts with desire, which would constitute a revocation by action without witnesses, and that is disliked (makruh) from two aspects, as we have mentioned. In the second case, it may lead to prolonging her iddah, as he might become a revoker by looking unintentionally, then divorce her, causing harm. So by this it is known that there is no need to restrict the texts to the case where he does not intend revocation, as done in al-Hidayah and others; rather, they are general, as is not hidden. And his saying: 'It is disliked from two aspects' – meaning from the aspect of it being by action and from the aspect of it being without witnesses. There is a consideration regarding the first aspect in al-Shurnublaliyyah: the discussion is about the revocably divorced woman, and intercourse is not prohibited, so looking is similar to it, even more so because it precedes it. Yes, this appears when he does not intend revocation, and the author's speech is not about that. And the evidence for it is what he said earlier: 'And witnessing is recommended.' In al-Muhit, Abu Yusuf said: kissing and touching without desire are disliked if he does not intend revocation. He said: 'And he explicitly stated the general application in al-Wilwaljiyyah.' I say: What I saw in it is its text:
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on two rulings from Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding the revocably divorced woman (al-muttalaqa al-raj'iyya). First, she may adorn herself (tatazayyan) for her husband, provided there is hope of revocation (raj'ah marjuwwah). This is because the marriage is still intact and she is lawful to him. The commentator restricts this: it must be for her husband (not others), she must be revocably divorced (not ba'in), and there must be hope of revocation. If the husband is absent or she knows he will not revoke, she should not adorn. The husband even has the right to demand adornment. Second, it is recommended (mandub) that the husband not enter upon her without informing her (yūdhinuhā). The reason: if he intends revocation, looking with desire might constitute a revocation by action (bi al-fi'l) without witnesses, which is disliked from two aspects: the act itself and the lack of witnessing. If he does not intend revocation, entering might inadvertently prolong her iddah. The commentary notes that this ruling is general and need not be restricted. It also quotes Abu Yusuf's view that kissing and touching without desire are disliked if he does not intend revocation. The passage ends abruptly with a reference to al-Wilwaljiyyah.

Key Terms
مطلقة رجعية (muttalaqa raj'iyya): A woman who has received a revocable divorce (talaq raj'i) and is still in her waiting period, during which the husband may revoke the divorce without a new contract.
تزين (tazayyun): Adorning oneself with beautification such as makeup, jewelry, and attractive clothing.
إيذان (idhan): Informing another person about one's entry, such as by making a sound or calling out.

Key Takeaway
A revocably divorced woman may adorn for her husband only when there is hope of revocation, and the husband is recommended to inform her before entry.

Revocably Divorced Woman May Adorn Herself
Clarifying permissibility with conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on a revocably divorced woman adorning herself and identify the conditions that govern this permissibility.

Core Idea
A revocably divorced woman may adorn herself for her husband to encourage reconciliation.

This lesson explores a specific ruling for women undergoing a revocable divorce. While many assume a divorced woman must refrain from beautification, the Hanafi school provides a nuanced position: she may adorn herself for her husband, as the marriage bond is not yet severed and doing so may facilitate revocation. We will examine the conditions that limit this permissibility and the husband's right to demand it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is adorning (al-tazayyun) for a woman in revocable idda. The ruling is permissible (mubah) when done for the husband, based on the recommendation of revocation.
2
Determine the conditions
Two conditions are stated: (1) the adornment must be for the husband (not for others); (2) reconciliation must be hoped for (marjuwwah). If the husband is absent or reconciliation is not expected, she should not adorn.
3
Understand the rationale
The marriage contract remains intact during revocable idda. Revocation is recommended, and adorning encourages it, thus the act is legislated.
The prophetic encouragement of reconciliation (raj'ah) supports this reasoning.
4
Distinguish from other types of idda
An irrevocably divorced woman (ba'in) or a widow is not allowed to adorn herself at all, as the marriage is definitively over and looking at them is prohibited.
Widows must observe mourning (ihdad), which includes avoiding adornment.
5
Note the husband's right
The husband has the right to demand adornment, and if she refuses, he may discipline her (e.g., by striking, as per the text). This right applies to the revocably divorced woman as well, since she is still his wife in some respects.
This underscores that adorning is not merely optional but becomes obligatory upon the husband's request.

Source Text

His saying: (And the revocably divorced woman adorns herself) means for her husband if the revocation is hoped for, because she is lawful for the husband, and the marriage contract remains between them. Also, revocation is recommended, and adorning encourages it, so it is legislated. We restrict it to being for her husband because if he is absent, she does not adorn herself due to the absence of the cause. We restrict it to the revocably divorced because the woman in a waiting period from an irrevocable divorce is not allowed to adorn herself at all due to the prohibition of looking at her and the lack of legislation of revocation. Similarly, the widow is excluded, for she laments. We restrict it to being hoped for because if she knows that he will not take her back due to his strong aversion, she does not do that, as mentioned in Sharh Miskin. And they explicitly stated that the husband can strike his wife for leaving adornment if he demands it from her, because it is his right, and that includes the revocably divorced woman.

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement clarifying the permissibility for a revocably divorced woman to adorn herself during her waiting period. The commentator explains that the ruling is conditional: the adornment is for her husband when reconciliation is hoped for. The reason is that the marriage bond is not yet severed, and revocation is recommended; adorning encourages it. The restriction to the revocably divorced (not irrevocably divorced or widows) is because in the latter cases, the marriage is over, and adorning is not appropriate. Additionally, the text notes that if the husband demands adornment, she must comply, as it is his right, and he may even discipline her for neglecting it. This shows that the husband's rights remain intact during the revocable idda.

Key Terms
المطلقة الرجعية (al-mutallaqah al-raj'iyyah): A woman divorced with a revocable divorce (talaq raj'i) where the husband can take her back without a new marriage contract during her waiting period.
الرجعة (al-raj'ah): The act of the husband taking back his wife during the waiting period of a revocable divorce, restoring the marital relationship.
التزين (al-tazayyun): Adorning oneself with beautification such as wearing makeup, jewelry, or nice clothes.

Recommended to Announce Entry to Revocably Divorced Wife
Explaining the recommended practice for a husband to announce his presence to his revocably divorced wife

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on announcing entry to a revocably divorced wife and explain the rationale and scope of the recommendation.

Core Idea
A husband must announce his entry to a revocably divorced wife to avoid accidental revocation.

This lesson examines a specific etiquette in revocable divorce: the husband's duty to inform his wife before entering. The ruling, though a recommendation, carries legal significance because failing to announce could lead to unintended consequences like an unwanted revocation. We will explore the reasoning behind the rule and its absolute application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
State the act (announcing entry) and its hukm level (mandub/sunnah muakkadah). Note that it is a recommendation, not an obligation.
The Arabic term 'nudiba' indicates a meritorious act.
2
Understand the rationale (talil)
Examine the two reasons given: (1) to prevent accidental revocation by sight with desire, and (2) to avoid prolonging the idda through unintentional revocation followed by a second divorce.
Both reasons highlight harm prevention.
3
Note the absolute scope
The commentator stresses that the ruling applies regardless of whether the husband intends to revoke the divorce or not. This is derived from the author's unqualified statement.
Contrast with al-Hidayah's restriction.
4
Consider the intra-Hanafi variant
Abu Yusuf holds that kissing and touching without desire are disliked when revocation is not intended. This shows that even minor actions can be subject to differing views within the school.
The majority view is that such actions are permissible.

Prohibition of Traveling with Revocably Divorced Wife
Explaining the prohibition of traveling with a revocably divorced woman and its implications for revocation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on traveling with a revocably divorced wife and explain when such travel constitutes revocation by indication (raj'ah dalalah).

Core Idea
Travel with a revocably divorced wife is prohibited and, if silent, is an indication of revocation.

The Hanafi school derives the prohibition of traveling with a revocably divorced wife from the Quranic command not to expel wives from their homes during the idda. This lesson unpacks the ruling, its proof, and a key nuance: when the husband remains silent, the act of travel itself becomes an indication of revocation (raj'ah dalalah). We will explore the evidence, the conditions, and how this ruling applies in practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act under discussion is the husband taking his revocably divorced wife out of her home (traveling with her). The Hanafi ruling is that this is haram (prohibited).
The prohibition applies regardless of distance—any removal from her residence is included.
2
Locate the proof (dalil)
The primary proof is the Quranic verse: 'Do not expel them from their houses' (Surah al-Talaq 65:1). Travel is a form of expulsion, thus prohibited.
The dalil is qat'i al-thubut but zanni al-dalala—the verse is definitive in transmission but its application to travel requires reasoning.
3
Understand revocation by indication (raj'ah dalalah)
If the husband remains silent about his intention, the act of travel is considered an indication that he wishes to take her back. This is called raj'ah dalalah (revocation by implication).
However, if he explicitly denies intent to revoke, the travel remains prohibited but does not constitute revocation.
4
Distinguish between explicit declaration and silence
Explicit declaration (e.g., 'I do not intend to take you back') negates the indication. Silence allows the travel to function as an implied revocation.
This distinction is crucial for determining the marital status after such travel.
5
Apply the ruling to edge cases
The text also discusses kissing with desire (real revocation) versus travel only being an indication. Additionally, if the husband later takes her back during the idda, the prohibition of travel is lifted.
These edge cases show the depth of Hanafi reasoning in protecting the rights of the wife and the integrity of the idda.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ولا يسافر بها يعني يحرم عليه السفر بها لقوله تعالى ولا تخرجوهن من بيوتهن [الطلاق: ١] ولحرمته لم يكن رجعة لأن الرجعة مندوبة والمسافرة بها حرام ومراده إذا كان يصرح بعدم رجعتها، أما إذا سكت كانت رجعة دلالة كما أشار إليه في فتح القدير وشرح الجامع الصغير للقاضي وفتاويه والبدائع وغاية البيان معللين بأن السفر دلالة الرجعة فانتفى به ما ذكره الشارح من أن السفر ليس دلالة الرجعة وأورد أن التقبيل بشهوة يكون رجعة وإن نادى على نفسه بعدم الرجعة وجوابه الفرق بالحل والحرمة كما نقلنا كذا في فتح القدير. وأجاب الشمني بأن التقبيل رجعة حقيقة لا دلالة بخلاف السفر فإنه رجعة دلالة لأنه يستلزم شيئاً تثبت به الرجعة قيد بالسفر أي بإنشائه لأنه لو طلقها في السفر لها أن تمشي معه ؛ ذكره الأسبيجابي ومراده من المسافرة بها إخراجها من بيتها لا السفر الشرعي المقدر بثلاثة أيام لأنه يحرم إخراجها إلى ما دونه أيضاً للنهي المطلق لكن لا يكون رجعة دلالة واعلم أن في الهداية ما يدل على أن حرمة المسافرة بها مقيدة بما إذا لم يراجعها في عدتها لأنه تبين أن المبطل للعصمة عمل عمله من وقت الطلاق حتى احتسبت الأقراء الماضية من العدة فكانت المسافرة بأجنبية، أما إذا راجعها في عدتها تبين أنه لم يعمل عمله فزالت الحرمة
His saying: (And do not travel with her) means that it is prohibited for him to travel with her due to His saying: 'Do not expel them from their houses' [65:1]. And because of its prohibition, it is not considered a revocation, since revocation is recommended and traveling with her is prohibited. His meaning is when he explicitly states that he does not intend revocation. However, if he remains silent, it is a revocation by indication (raj'ah dalalah), as pointed out in Fath al-Qadir, Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir by al-Qadi, and his Fatawa, al-Bada'i', and Ghayat al-Bayan, reasoning that travel is an indication of revocation. This refutes what the commentator said that travel is not an indication of revocation. And it was objected that kissing with desire is a revocation even if he declares himself not to intend revocation. The response is the difference between halal and haram, as we transmitted. Similarly in Fath al-Qadir. Al-Shumanni answered that kissing is a revocation in reality, not by indication, unlike travel, which is a revocation by indication, because it entails something by which revocation is established. Travel is restricted to its initiation, because if he divorced her while traveling, she may walk with him; mentioned by al-Asbajabi. His intent by 'traveling with her' is taking her out of her home, not the legal travel defined as three days, because it is prohibited to take her out even for less than that due to the absolute prohibition, but that does not constitute a revocation by indication. And know that in al-Hidayah there is an indication that the prohibition of traveling with her is restricted to when he does not take her back during her idda, because it becomes clear that the nullifier of marital inviolability acted from the time of divorce, so the past periods counted towards the idda, and thus traveling with her would be with a foreign woman. However, if he takes her back during her idda, it becomes clear that it (the divorce) did not take effect, so the prohibition is removed.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof establishing the prohibition of a husband traveling with his revocably divorced wife. The primary proof is the Quranic verse prohibiting expulsion from their homes. Travel effectively removes her from the home, so it is prohibited. The commentator then discusses whether such travel constitutes a revocation (raj'ah). If the husband explicitly states he does not intend revocation, it is not considered revocation. But if he remains silent, travel is considered a revocation by indication (dalalah). This is supported by multiple Hanafi authorities. The passage also addresses an objection regarding kissing with desire: kissing is a real revocation regardless of declaration, but travel is only an indication. The commentator further clarifies that 'travel' here means any removal from the home, even short distances. Finally, a nuance from al-Hidayah: if the husband takes her back during the idda, the prohibition of travel is lifted.

Key Terms
السفر (al-safar): Travel; here it means any removal of the wife from her residence, not just long-distance travel.
الرجعة دلالة (al-raj'ah dalalah): Revocation by indication; an action that implies the husband's intent to take back his wife, even without explicit words.
المبطل للعصمة (al-mubtil li al-'ismah): That which nullifies marital inviolability; here it refers to the divorce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on a husband traveling with his revocably divorced wife if he remains silent about his intention?
A) Permissible
B) Prohibited and considered revocation by indication
C) Prohibited but not a revocation
D) Recommended

Key Takeaway
Traveling with a revocably divorced wife is prohibited in all cases, and if the husband remains silent, it also constitutes an implied revocation.

Intercourse During Revocable Divorce: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i
Dispute over intercourse during revocable divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position that intercourse during revocable divorce is permissible and requires no dower, compare it with the Shafi'i view, and identify the practical consequence of the difference.

Core Idea
Revocable divorce does not dissolve marriage; intercourse remains lawful and requires no dower.

The Hanafi school holds that a revocably divorced woman remains a wife, so intercourse is lawful. This lesson examines the ruling, the dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i, and the practical effect—dower or no dower.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling: intercourse is permissible and no dower is required because the marriage bond continues.
The source says 'does not prohibit intercourse' and 'no dower is required'.
2
Identify the Shafi'i objection
Al-Shafi'i prohibits intercourse and thus requires a dower (mahr al-mithl).
3
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical difference: Hanafi man owes nothing; Shafi'i man must pay a dower and may face ta'zir.
4
Examine the Shafi'i evidence
Al-Shafi'i cites five Quranic contexts where the divorced woman is treated as a wife, but he still prohibits intercourse.
Source mentions inheritance, ila', zihar, li'an, divorce, and idda of death.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : والطلاق الرجعي لا يحرم الوطء) لما قدمناه من الآيات والمعنى أول الباب فلا يلزم به عقر، والشافعي لما حرمه أوجب له العقر. وفي المعراج معزياً إلى الروضة للشافعية : لو وطئها فلا حد عليه وإن كان عالماً بالتحريم وفيه وجه ضعيف لا يجب التعزير إن كان جاهلاً أو يعتقد إباحته وإلا فيجب ولو وطئها ولم يراجعها يجب مهر المثل، ولو راجعها فالنص وجوب مهر المثل. وفي الروضة أيضاً قال الشافعي: إنها زوجته في خمس مواضع من كتاب الله في آية الميراث والإيلاء والظهار واللعان والطلاق وعدة الوفاة وكذا في عدم اشتراط الولي في الرجعة وعدم اشتراط لفظة النكاح والتزويج ورضاها عند الكل ا هـ . وأشار إلى أن الخلوة بها لا تحرم لكنها مكروهة كراهة تنزيهية إن لم يكن من قصده المراجعة وإلا فلا وكذا القسم لأنه لو ثبت لها القسم فخلا بها فربما أدى إلى المساس بشهوة فيصير مراجعاً يريدها فيطلقها فتطول العدة عليها حتى لو كان وهو المراجعة كان لها القسم؛ كذا في البدائع والله سبحانه وتعالى أعلم. من قصده ويكره أن يراها متجردة إن لم يرد الرجعة لأنه ربما يأتي بشيء يصير به مراجعاً ثم يطلقها فتطول العدة عليها، فإن كان من شأنه أن لا يراجعها فأحسن ذلك أن يعلمها بدخوله عليها بالتنحنح وخفق النعل كي تتأهب لدخوله كيلا يقع بصره على فرجها فيصير مراجعاً لها ثم يطلقها، وكذا إن كان من شأنه أن يراجعها فالأحسن أن يعلمها كي لا يصير مراجعاً بغير شهود، وكذا يكره التقبيل واللمس بغير شهوة ا هـ . فما نسبه إليها من التصريح بالإطلاق ليس موجوداً كما رأيت. وأما قوله «ويكره التقبيل واللمس بغير شهوة» فهو فيما إذا لم يرد مراجعتها أيضاً؛ صرح به في البدائع .
His saying: (And revocable divorce does not prohibit intercourse) due to what we have presented of verses and the meaning at the beginning of the chapter. Therefore, no dower is required due to it. Al-Shafi'i, because he prohibited it, required a dower for it. In al-Mi'raj, citing al-Rawdah of the Shafi'is: If he has intercourse with her, there is no prescribed punishment even if he knows the prohibition; there is a weak view that ta'zir is not required if he is ignorant or believes it permissible, otherwise it is required. And if he has intercourse and does not take her back, the proper dower is required; if he takes her back, the text mandates the proper dower. In al-Rawdah also, al-Shafi'i said: She is his wife in five places in the Book of Allah: in the verse of inheritance, ila', zihar, li'an, and divorce and the idda of death. And similarly in the non-requirement of a guardian in revocation and the non-requirement of the word 'nikah' or 'tazwij' and her consent according to all. He (the commentator) indicated that seclusion with her is not prohibited but is disliked with a tanzihi dislike if he does not intend revocation, otherwise not. Similarly, division (qasm) because if division is established for her and he secludes with her, it might lead to touching with desire, making him a revoker intending her, then divorcing her, prolonging her idda. Even if he is intending revocation, she has the right to division. This is in al-Bada'i'. And Allah knows best. If it is his intention, and it is disliked to see her naked if he does not intend revocation, because he might do something that makes him a revoker, then divorce her, prolonging her idda. If it is his habit not to take her back, the best is that he informs her of his entry by clearing his throat or tapping his sandals so that she prepares for his entry, lest his eye fall on her private parts and he becomes a revoker, then divorces her. Similarly, if it is his habit to take her back, the best is to inform her so that he does not become a revoker without witnesses. Similarly, kissing and touching without desire are disliked. That is the end. So what he attributed to her of explicit absoluteness is not present as I saw. As for his saying 'kissing and touching without desire are disliked', it is when he does not intend to take her back either; it is stated explicitly in al-Bada'i'.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage is a dispute between the Hanafi school and Imam al-Shafi'i regarding the ruling of intercourse during a revocable divorce. The Hanafi position, based on the absence of any prohibition in the Quran and Sunnah, is that intercourse is permissible and does not require a dower. Imam al-Shafi'i, considering the divorce as a form of separation, prohibited it and thus required a dower. The commentator then discusses Shafi'i views on punishment and dower. After establishing the main dispute, the passage turns to related etiquettes: seclusion (khalwah) with the revocably divorced woman is disliked if no intention to revoke; division of days (qasm) is also discussed. Finally, it addresses the ruling of seeing her naked, kissing, and touching without desire: these are disliked if the husband does not intend revocation, to avoid inadvertent revocation. The passage also corrects a misunderstanding from al-Walwalji's Fatawa, stating that the disliked aspect is absolute when he does not intend revocation.

Key Terms
الوطء (al-wat'): Sexual intercourse.
العقر (al-'aqr): A dower or compensation for intercourse; here it means a specific dower (mahr al-mithl) due to the act.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on intercourse with a revocably divorced woman?
A) Permissible and no dower required
B) Prohibited but no punishment
C) Permissible but requires a dower
D) Prohibited and requires punishment

Key Takeaway
Revocable divorce does not prohibit intercourse; the marriage continues until the idda expires.

Etiquettes of Interaction with a Revocably Divorced Wife
Rulings on seclusion, nudity, and touching with revocably divorced wife

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rulings on seclusion (khalwah), seeing her naked, and kissing/touching without desire when with a revocably divorced wife, and identify when these actions are disliked or permissible.

Core Idea
Physical intimacy is allowed, but actions that may inadvertently lead to revocation are disliked if no revocation is intended.

After establishing the permissibility of intercourse, the commentary addresses related etiquettes: seclusion, seeing the wife naked, and intimate touches. These actions may be disliked if the husband does not intend to revoke the divorce, to avoid accidental revocation and prolonging the idda.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general ruling on khalwah
Seclusion with a revocably divorced woman is permissible but disliked (makruh tanzihi) if the husband does not intend revocation; otherwise not disliked.
Source: 'الخلوة بها لا تحرم لكنها مكروهة كراهة تنزيهية'
2
Examine the ruling on seeing her naked
It is disliked to see her naked if he does not intend revocation, to avoid accidental revocation.
Justification: risk of sexual arousal leading to revocation then divorce, lengthening idda.
3
Analyze the ruling on kissing and touching
Kissing and touching without desire are disliked if he does not intend revocation.
Source: 'ويكره التقبيل واللمس بغير شهوة'
4
Consider the best practice
The husband should announce his entry by clearing throat or tapping sandals so the wife can prepare, preventing inadvertent eye contact on private parts.
This applies whether he intends revocation or not, for different reasons.

Travel with Revocably Divorced Woman: Prohibition and Proof
Ruling with proof from Quran on prohibition of travel during iddah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that traveling with a revocably divorced woman during her iddah is haram and explain the Quranic proof and conditions.

Core Idea
Traveling with a divorcée during iddah is haram because it violates the Quranic command not to expel her.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq establishes the prohibition of a husband traveling with his wife during her revocable iddah. It cites Quran 65:1 and clarifies that the prohibition applies even for short distances. The passage also distinguishes between travel as an implicit return and other acts like kissing, which constitute an actual return. Understanding this ruling is crucial for correctly applying iddah laws.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is traveling with a revocably divorced woman during her iddah. The ruling is haram. Confirm that the source text explicitly states 'يحرم عليه السفر بها'.
2
Locate the dalil
The primary proof is Quran 65:1: 'Do not turn them out of their houses.' The author uses this verse to establish the prohibition by analogy: travel is a form of expulsion.
The imperative form indicates a prohibition.
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
The verse commands not to expel women from their homes during iddah. Travel, even for a short distance, involves leaving the home, thus falls under the prohibition. The author clarifies that the prohibition applies absolutely, not just for long journeys.
4
Understand the nuance of implicit return
The passage discusses whether travel constitutes an implicit return (رجعة دلالة). If the husband remains silent, some authorities consider it an implicit return. However, the Hanafi position is that travel itself is not a return; only kissing with desire is a real return. This distinction affects the lifting of the prohibition if the husband later claims return.
5
Identify the condition for the prohibition
The prohibition applies only if the husband has not made a clear return (رجعة) to his wife during the iddah. If he explicitly returns, the prohibition is removed and travel becomes permissible. The hidayah indicates this condition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ولا يسافر بها يعني يحرم عليه السفر بها لقوله تعالى ولا تخرجوهن من بيوتهن [الطلاق: ١] ولحرمته لم يكن رجعة لأن الرجعة مندوبة والمسافرة بها حرام ومراده إذا كان يصرح بعدم رجعتها، أما إذا سكت كانت رجعة دلالة كما أشار إليه في فتح القدير وشرح الجامع الصغير للقاضي وفتاويه والبدائع وغاية البيان معللين بأن السفر دلالة الرجعة فانتفى به ما ذكره الشارح من أن السفر ليس دلالة الرجعة وأورد أن التقبيل بشهوة يكون رجعة وإن نادى على نفسه بعدم الرجعة وجوابه الفرق بالحل والحرمة كما نقلنا كذا في فتح القدير. وأجاب الشمني بأن التقبيل رجعة حقيقة لا دلالة بخلاف السفر فإنه رجعة دلالة لأنه يستلزم شيئاً تثبت به الرجعة قيد بالسفر أي بإنشائه لأنه لو طلقها في السفر لها أن تمشي معه ؛ ذكره الأسبيجابي ومراده من المسافرة بها إخراجها من بيتها لا السفر الشرعي المقدر بثلاثة أيام لأنه يحرم إخراجها إلى ما دونه أيضاً للنهي المطلق لكن لا يكون رجعة دلالة واعلم أن في الهداية ما يدل على أن حرمة المسافرة بها مقيدة بما إذا لم يراجعها في عدتها لأنه تبين أن المبطل للعصمة عمل عمله من وقت الطلاق حتى احتسبت الأقراء الماضية من العدة فكانت المسافرة بأجنبية، أما إذا راجعها في عدتها تبين أنه لم يعمل عمله فزالت الحرمة
His saying: (And he should not travel with her) meaning it is forbidden for him to travel with her due to His saying 'Do not turn them out of their houses' [At-Talaq:1]. And because of its prohibition, it is not a return (رجعة) because return is recommended and traveling with her is forbidden. His intention is when he explicitly states that he is not returning to her. But if he remains silent, it is an implicit return as indicated in Fath al-Qadir, Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir, Qadi's Fatawa, al-Bada'i, and Ghayat al-Bayan, reasoning that travel is an indication of return. This refutes what the commentator said that travel is not an indication of return. And it was objected that kissing with desire is a return even if he declares he does not intend return, and his response is the distinction between permissible and forbidden as we transmitted in Fath al-Qadir. And al-Shumni answered that kissing is a real return, not an indication, unlike travel which is an implicit return because it necessitates something by which return is established. He restricted it to travel, i.e., initiating it, because if he divorced her while traveling, she may walk with him; mentioned by al-Asbijabi. His intention by traveling with her is to take her out of her house, not the legally defined travel of three days, because taking her out to a lesser distance is also forbidden due to the absolute prohibition, but it does not constitute an implicit return. Know that in al-Hidayah there is evidence that the prohibition of traveling with her is conditional upon not having returned to her during her iddah, because it becomes clear that the invalidator of the marriage bond took effect from the time of divorce such that the past menstrual periods counted towards the iddah, so traveling with her would be with a foreign woman. But if he returned to her during her iddah, it becomes clear that his action did not take effect, so the prohibition is removed.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof on the prohibition of traveling with a revocably divorced woman during her iddah. The author begins by stating the ruling: it is forbidden for the husband to travel with her, citing Quran 65:1 which commands not to expel them. Since traveling is forbidden, it cannot be considered an actual return (رجعة) because return is a recommended act while travel is prohibited. However, if the husband remains silent about his intention while traveling, it can be an implicit return (رجعة دلالة) according to many authorities, because travel indicates his intent to keep her. There is a distinction: kissing with desire is a real return even if he says he does not intend return, whereas travel is only an implicit return. The Hanafi position is that the prohibition of travel applies even for short distances, but only if he has not explicitly returned to her; if he returns, the prohibition is lifted. This analysis is based on the notion that the divorce invalidates the marriage bond from the time of utterance, so traveling without return makes her a stranger.

Key Terms
رجعة (raj'ah): The revocable return to a divorced wife during her iddah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because travel is haram and return is mandub.
B) Because she has become a stranger.
C) Because travel is a form of divorce.
D) Because return requires witnesses.

Key Takeaway
Traveling with a revocably divorced woman during her iddah is haram unless the husband explicitly returns to her.

Intercourse During Revocable Divorce: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
inter-madhhab dispute over intercourse in revocable divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on intercourse during revocable divorce, explain why no dower is required, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument regarding the ongoing marital bond.

Core Idea
In revocable divorce, intercourse remains halal—no dower, no prohibition.

Our author has established that revocable divorce does not sever the marriage. Now he addresses a specific consequence: may the husband have intercourse with his divorced wife? The Hanafi school answers unequivocally yes, and this answer carries implications for dower, seclusion, and the division of nights among co-wives. The Shafi'i school disagrees, creating a well-known ikhtilaf.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi ruling
Identify the ruling: revocable divorce does not forbid intercourse. This is based on Quranic verses presented earlier in the book. The act is permissible and no dower is incurred.
The ruling is stated clearly in the opening sentence.
2
Identify the opposing school
Imam al-Shafi'i and his school consider intercourse forbidden during revocable divorce. Their reasoning: the woman remains a wife in several Quranic contexts (inheritance, ila', zihar, li'an, talaq, iddah of death), so the marriage contract still governs, making intercourse potentially illicit and requiring a dower.
The text lists the five (actually six) contexts where the Shafi'i school sees the woman as a wife.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
The text does not provide a direct rebuttal but implies that since the act is not forbidden, no dower is due. The Hanafi school emphasizes the general permissibility of marital relations and the verses allowing return without a new contract.
The Hanafi position prioritizes the possibility of reconciliation.
4
Examine practical implications
The passage discusses related issues: seclusion is makruh tanzihi unless intending return; division of nights must be observed; if seclusion leads to intercourse, it acts as a return and may prolong iddah if followed by another divorce.
These details show the real-world application of the core ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : والطلاق الرجعي لا يحرم الوطء) لما قدمناه من الآيات والمعنى أول الباب فلا يلزم به عقر، والشافعي لما حرمه أوجب له العقر. وفي المعراج معزياً إلى الروضة للشافعية : لو وطئها فلا حد عليه وإن كان عالماً بالتحريم وفيه وجه ضعيف لا يجب التعزير إن كان جاهلاً أو يعتقد إباحته وإلا فيجب ولو وطئها ولم يراجعها يجب مهر المثل، ولو راجعها فالنص وجوب مهر المثل. وفي الروضة أيضاً قال الشافعي: إنها زوجته في خمس مواضع من كتاب الله في آية الميراث والإيلاء والظهار واللعان والطلاق وعدة الوفاة وكذا في عدم اشتراط الولي في الرجعة وعدم اشتراط لفظة النكاح والتزويج ورضاها عند الطلاق ا هـ . وأشار إلى أن الخلوة بها لا تحرم لكنها مكروهة كراهة تنزيهية إن لم يكن من قصده المراجعة وإلا فلا وكذا القسم لأنه لو ثبت لها القسم فخلا بها فربما أدى إلى المساس بشهوة فيصير مراجعاً يريدها فيطلقها فتطول العدة عليها حتى لو كان وهو المراجعة كان لها القسم؛ كذا في البدائع والله سبحانه وتعالى أعلم.
His saying: (And revocable divorce does not make intercourse forbidden) due to what we presented of verses and the meaning at the beginning of the chapter, so no dower is incurred by it. Al-Shafi'i, because he forbade it, made a dower obligatory for it. In al-Mi'raj, attributed to Rawdah of the Shafi'is: If he has intercourse with her, no hadd punishment is upon him even if he knew of the prohibition, and there is a weak view that ta'zir is not obligatory if he is ignorant or believes it permissible, otherwise it is obligatory. And if he had intercourse without returning to her, a dower of the like is obligatory; if he returned to her, the text states obligation of a dower of the like. In al-Rawdah also, al-Shafi'i said: She is his wife in five places in the Book of Allah: in the verse of inheritance, ila', zihar, li'an, talaq, and iddah of death, and likewise in not requiring a guardian in return, and not requiring the word nikah or tazwij, and her consent at divorce. And he indicated that seclusion with her is not forbidden but is disliked in a tanzihi manner if he does not intend to return, otherwise it is not disliked. And likewise the division of nights, because if division is established for her and he is alone with her, it might lead to passionate touching, making him a returner, then he divorces her, prolonging her iddah. Even if he intends return, she has the right to division. This is in al-Bada'i. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding intercourse during revocable divorce. The author first states the Hanafi position: revocable divorce does not forbid intercourse, based on Quranic verses at the beginning of the book, and if intercourse occurs, no dower is due. He then presents the Shafi'i position: al-Shafi'i considers intercourse forbidden and thus obligates a dower. The text recounts that in Shafi'i school, if a man has intercourse knowing it is forbidden, no hadd is applied but ta'zir may be required unless ignorant; and regardless, a dower of the like is owed. The Shafi'i argument is that the woman is still a wife in several Quranic contexts. The author then notes that seclusion with her is not forbidden but is disliked (makruh tanzihi) unless he intends to return, in which case it is not disliked. Division of nights is also discussed: if he has a co-wife, he must be fair, but if seclusion leads to intercourse, he would become a returner, potentially harming the woman by prolonging iddah.

Key Terms
عقر (aqr): Dower (mahr) paid for intercourse in a dubious or forbidden relationship.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In revocable divorce, intercourse remains halal and no dower is required, though the Shafi'i school disagrees.

Disliked Acts During Iddah of Revocable Divorce
Lists disliked actions for a husband during his divorcée's iddah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the disliked acts for a husband during his divorcée's iddah in a revocable divorce and explain the underlying reasons.

Core Idea
Seeing, kissing, or touching one's divorcée during iddah is disliked to protect her from prolonged iddah due to accidental return.

The iddah period after a revocable divorce is a sensitive time. While the husband retains the right to return, certain actions are discouraged to prevent harm and confusion. This lesson clarifies these disliked acts and the wisdom behind them, based on the commentary of Imam al-Nasafi and al-Bada'i.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What specific action is being discussed? What is its legal classification (makruh tanzihi)? The passage focuses on three actions: seeing her undressed, kissing, and touching without desire.
The ruling is makruh tanzihi, not haram.
2
Understand the underlying reason
Why is this act disliked? The text explains that it may lead to an accidental return (raj'ah) followed by another divorce, prolonging the iddah and causing harm to the wife.
The harm is to the wife, not a ritual impurity.
3
Note the recommended alternative
What should the husband do instead? If he does not intend to return, he should announce his entry (by clearing throat or tapping shoes) so she can cover herself and avoid an unintentional return.
Even if he intends to return, announcing is recommended to ensure witnesses for the return.
4
Recognize the correction of a misunderstanding
The author refutes a claim that seeing undressed is absolutely permissible. He reiterates that it is disliked even without the intent to return, citing al-Bada'i.
This is an important clarification to prevent misconception.
5
Apply to real scenarios
Consider a husband living in the same house. He must avoid actions that could be misinterpreted as a return, and take precautions to protect his divorcée's interests.
Modern application: husbands should be mindful of privacy and communication.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويكره أن يراها متجردة إن لم يرد الرجعة لأنه ربما يأتي بشيء يصير به مراجعاً ثم يطلقها فتطول العدة عليها، فإن كان من شأنه أن لا يراجعها فأحسن ذلك أن يعلمها بدخوله عليها بالتنحنح وخفق النعل كي تتأهب لدخوله كيلا يقع بصره على فرجها فيصير مراجعاً لها ثم يطلقها، وكذا إن كان من شأنه أن يراجعها فالأحسن أن يعلمها كي لا يصير مراجعاً بغير شهود، وكذا يكره التقبيل واللمس بغير شهوة ا هـ. فما نسبه إليها من التصريح بالإطلاق ليس موجوداً كما رأيت. وأما قوله «ويكره التقبيل واللمس بغير شهوة» فهو فيما إذا لم يرد مراجعتها أيضاً؛ صرح به في البدائع.
And it is disliked (makruh) for him to see her undressed if he does not intend to return, because perhaps he will do something that makes him a returner, then divorce her, prolonging her iddah. If his habit is not to return, then it is best that he inform her of his entry by clearing his throat or tapping his shoes so she can prepare for his entrance, lest his sight fall on her private part and he becomes a returner to her then divorces her. Likewise if his habit is to return, it is best to inform her so that he does not become a returner without witnesses. Also it is disliked to kiss and touch without desire. So what was attributed to her of explicit absolute permission is not found, as you see. And his saying 'It is disliked to kiss and touch without desire' applies even when he does not intend to return to her; this is explicitly stated in al-Bada'i.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage is a statement of disliked actions regarding the husband's conduct during his divorcée's iddah in a revocable divorce. The author explains that it is disliked (makruh tanzihi) for the husband to see his divorced wife undressed if he has no intention to return, because it might lead to arousal and thus an accidental return, which could harm her by prolonging the iddah if he later divorces again. To avoid this, it is best for him to announce his entry (clearing throat, tapping shoes) so she can cover herself. Even if he intends to return, informing her is recommended to avoid an unwitnessed return. Additionally, kissing and touching without sexual desire are also disliked. The author notes that the claim of absolute permissibility is incorrect; the dislike applies even without intent to return.

Key Terms
كراهة تنزيهية (karahah tanzihiyyah): A disliked act that is not sinful but avoidance is better; lesser than makruh tahrimi.
العدة (al-iddah): The waiting period after divorce or death during which a woman cannot remarry.
الطلاق الرجعي (al-talaq al-raj'i): A revocable divorce where the husband can return to the wife without a new contract during the iddah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) It might lead to accidental return and prolong her iddah.
C) Because she is now a stranger.
D) Because it invalidates the divorce.

Key Takeaway
During revocable divorce iddah, a husband should avoid acts that could lead to unintentional return or harm the wife, even if not sinful.
