Chapter on what makes the divorced woman permissible. And in Manaqib al-Bazzazi: If the marriage contract was without a guardian but by the woman's own words, or was with the word 'gift', or was in the presence of two immoral witnesses, then he divorced her three times, then he wanted to make her permissible for himself without [her marrying] a husband, then he should refer the matter to a Shafi'i judge who will rule that the marriage is invalid and [then] he may marry her with a new contract. And it is not objected that ruling the marriage invalid entails the unlawfulness of previous intercourse and that children are born from unlawful intercourse, because we say: The ruling applies to the present and future, not the past. End quote. And in his Fatawa: If she fears that the muhallil will not divorce her, she may say to him [the muhallil] until he says: 'If I marry you and have intercourse with you, you are divorced.' End quote. And it is general, encompassing whether the first husband acknowledges the triple divorce or denies it after the triple divorce actually occurred. Hence they said: If he divorced her three times and he denies it, she may marry another and secretly make herself permissible for him if he [the first husband] is away on a journey; when he returns, she asks him to renew the marriage due to a doubt that entered her heart, not because of the husband's denial of the marriage. It is mentioned in al-Qunyah a difference, so he [the author] annotated to the original that if she is able to flee from him, it is not permissible for her to observe the waiting period and marry another because she is in the status of being under the first husband's marriage until a court rules separation. Then he annotated to Shams al-A'imma al-Ozjandi, saying: They said, this is in court; but she may do so diyaanatan (religiously). Similarly, if she heard him divorce her three times, then he denied and swore he did not do it, and the judge returned her to him, it is not permissible for her to stay with him, and it is also not permissible for her to marry another. He—i.e., al-Badi‘—said: In summary, according to the answer of Shams al-Islam al-Ozjandi, Najm al-Din al-Nasafi, al-Sayyid Abi Shuja‘, Abu Hamid, and al-Sarakhsi, it is permissible for her to marry another husband in matters between her and Allah the Exalted; according to the answer of the others, it is not permissible. End quote. And in al-Fatawa al-Sirajiyya: If a trustworthy person informs her that her husband divorced her while he is absent, it is permissible for her to observe the waiting period and marry, and he did not restrict it to diyaanatan. And Allah knows best. The author (may Allah have mercy on him) said: It is narrated in al-Qunyah before that from Sharh al-Sarakhsi, its wording: He divorced his wife three times and was absent from her, so she may marry another husband after the waiting period diyaanatan. Another [scholar] narrated that it is not permissible in the correct madhhab. End quote. I [the commentator] say: He [the author of al-Qunyah] annotated to Shams al-A'imma al-Ozjandi, and he is the one agreeing with what preceded, and the one who said that the correct madhhab is [the opinion of] ‘Ala’ al-Turjumani; then after him he annotated to ‘Umar al-Nasafi, who said: He swore three times and thought he was not sinful, and she knew the sin and thought that if she told him he would deny the oath; so when he is absent due to some reason, she may make herself permissible diyaanatan, not qada’an. ‘Umar al-Nasafi said: I asked about this al-Sayyid Aba Shuja‘, and he wrote that it is permissible. Then I asked him after a while and he said it is not permissible. The apparent meaning is that he answered regarding a woman who is not trustworthy. End quote. Similarly in Sharh al-Manzuma. And in al-Bazzaziyya: If witnesses testify that her husband divorced her three times—if he is absent, it is permissible for her to marry another; if he is present, it is not, because the husband, if he denies, a court ruling of separation is needed, and the ruling of separation is only allowed in the presence of the husband. End quote. And in it: She heard that her husband divorced her three times and she cannot prevent him except by killing him; if she knows he will approach her, she may kill him with poison but she may not kill herself. And al-Ozjandi mentioned that she should refer the matter to the judge; if she has no evidence, he [the judge] asks him to swear; if he swears, the sin is on him; if she kills him, there is nothing on her. And the woman who is bain (irrevocably divorced) is like the one divorced thrice. End quote. And in al-Tatarakhaniyya: Sheikh Abu al-Qasim was asked about a woman who heard from her husband that he divorced her three times and she cannot prevent him herself; is it permissible for her to kill him at the time he wants to approach her and she cannot prevent him except by killing? He said: She may kill him. Such was the fatwa of Imam Sheikh al-Islam ‘Ata' ibn Hamza Abi Shuja‘. Judge Imam al-Asbijabi used to say: She may not kill him. And in al-Multaqat: The fatwa is upon it [the latter view]. And in the fatawa of Sheikh Imam Muhammad ibn al-Walid al-Samarqandi in Manaqib Abi Hanifa from ‘Abdullah ibn al-Mubarak from Abu Hanifa that she may kill him. And in al-Muhit in the issue of the poem: She should ransom herself with her wealth and flee from him; if she cannot, she kills him when she knows he will approach her, but she should kill him with poison, and she should not kill herself. I [the commentator] said: In al-Muntaqa: If she kills him with a weapon, retaliation is necessary. And in al-Tatimma: He was asked about a woman who became forbidden to her husband and cannot free herself; if he is absent from her, she may use magic to bring him back to her; is it permissible to plot to kill her with poison or other means to escape from her? He said: It is not permissible; he should distance himself from her by any means possible. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq is a series of fatwas and rulings regarding the thrice-divorced woman (mubtallat) when the husband denies the divorce or is absent. It opens with a scenario where the original marriage was defective (without a guardian or with a suspicious contract), then the husband divorces her thrice. To make her permissible again without a second husband, the matter is taken to a Shafi'i judge who rules the marriage invalid ab initio, then the husband remarries her. This is a concession to reality: the judge's ruling only affects the future, not past intercourse. Next, the woman who fears the muhallil (the second husband) may not divorce her can have him pronounce a conditional divorce. When the husband denies the triple divorce, different opinions arise: according to Shams al-A'imma al-Ozjandi, Najm al-Din al-Nasafi, and others, she may marry another secretly in her private dealings with Allah (diyaanatan), not in court. Other scholars forbid even that. The author inclines to the permissibility diyaanatan, citing the fatwa of al-Sarakhsi and the ruling that if she is unable to escape, she may even kill the husband who forces intercourse, though there is a difference: Abu Hanifa permits it, while Judge al-Asbijabi forbids it, and the fatwa is on forbidding. The passage ends with a note that a woman should not commit suicide but may use poison when absolutely necessary. The overall lesson highlights the tension between legal form (qada') and religious reality (diyaanah) in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
ديانة (diyaanatan): In matters between the individual and Allah; religiously permissible even if not enforceable in court.
محلل (muhallil): The second husband who marries a thrice-divorced woman temporarily (in practice) to make her permissible for the first husband.
قضاء (qada'an): By court ruling; legally enforceable.
بائن (bain): Irrevocable divorce; the wife becomes completely separate and halal for others after iddah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling if a thrice-divorced woman's husband denies the divorce and she cannot obtain a court ruling?
A) She may not remarry under any circumstances.
B) She may remarry secretly but only according to court order.
C) She may remarry secretly (diyaanatan) according to many Hanafi scholars.
D) She may kill the husband immediately to free herself.

Key Takeaway
A thrice-divorced woman may remarry diyaanatan if certain of the divorce, but the court cannot enforce it; killing the husband is only permissible in extreme necessity and the later fatwa discourages it.

Talaq — Divorce

Second Husband's Destruction of Divorces: The Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on destruction of divorces

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on how many divorces the first husband retains after a second marriage and divorce, and explain the tarjih.

Core Idea
The second husband destroys only one divorce, so the first husband retains two (free) or one (slave) — Muhammad's view is mufta bihi.

When a woman divorced once remarries and is divorced again, her first husband regains the right to divorce her—but how many divorces does he have left? This lesson examines a classic intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf between Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Imam Muhammad on the effect of the second husband's marriage. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to weigh proofs (dalala) and apply the principle of tahlil correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the base ruling
The passage describes a wife divorced once, remarried, consummated, divorced again, then remarried to the first husband. The key question is how many divorces the first husband retains.
Consummation is required by consensus; without it, no destruction occurs.
2
Map the intra-Hanafi disagreement
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold the first husband retains three (free) or two (slave). Muhammad holds two (free) or one (slave). Note the difference in the number of divorces 'destroyed' by the second husband.
The disagreement is over the extent of destruction, not its occurrence.
3
Analyze the proofs cited
Identify the hadith ('Allah curses the muhallil...') and the two methods of istidlal used: dalala (indication) and qiyas (analogy). Understand how each side interprets the hadith.
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf see tahlil as applicable to all cases; Muhammad limits it to the severe case (triple divorce).
4
Evaluate the muhaqqiq's response and tarjih
The muhaqqiq (in Fath al-Qadir) rejects the analogy because tahlil is only for removing hardship (the triple divorce). Thus, Muhammad's view is preferred.
This tarjih is now the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practically, if a free woman is divorced once, then marries another, is divorced, and returns to the first husband, he can divorce her two more times (Muhammad) or three more times (Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf).
The difference affects the number of remaining divorces and the possibility of final separation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطلاق فصل فيما تحل به المطلقة للأول ويهدم الزوج الثاني ما دون الثلاث حتى لو طلقها واحدة وانقضت عدتها وتزوجت بآخر وطلقها وانقضت عدتها منه ثم تزوجها الأول يملك عليها ثلاثاً إن كانت حرة وثنتين إن كانت أمة، ولا يتحقق في الأمة إلا هدم طلقة واحدة، وعند محمد يملك عليها ثنتين في الحرة وواحدة في الأمة، ومراده إن دخل بها ولو لم يدخل بها لا اتفاقاً كما في القنية. وقد أخذ أبو حنيفة وأبو يوسف فيها بقول شبان الصحابة رضي كابن عباس وابن عمر، وأخذ محمد بقول الأكابر كعمر وعلي رضي الله عنهما. وحاصل ما استدلوا به من قوله صلى الله عليه وسلم الله المحلل والمحلل له (١) بطريق الدلالة أنه لما كان محللاً في الغليظة ففي الخفيفة أولى، أو بالقياس بجامع كونه زوجاً، ورده المحقق في فتح القدير والتحرير بأن التحليل إنما جعل «لعن يهدم الله عنهم في حرمتها بالثلاث فلا حرمة قبلها فظهر أن القول ما قاله محمد وباقي الأئمة الثلاث.
Book of Divorce, Chapter on what makes the divorced woman permissible to the first husband. The second husband destroys what is less than three (divorces). So if he divorced her once, her idda expired, and she married another, he divorced her, her idda from him expired, then the first married her, he has power over her three divorces if she is free, and two if she is a slave; and in a slave, only one divorce is destroyed. According to Muhammad, he has power over two in a free woman and one in a slave. His meaning is if he consummated the marriage; if he did not consummate, there is no destruction by consensus, as in al-Qunyah. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf took the opinion of the young companions like Ibn Abbas and Ibn Umar (may Allah be pleased with them), while Muhammad took the opinion of the elders like Umar and Ali (may Allah be pleased with them). The substance of their evidence from his statement (peace be upon him) 'Allah curses the one who makes permissible and the one for whom it is made permissible' by way of indication: since he is a 'make permissible' in the severe case, then in the light case it is more appropriate; or by analogy with the commonality of being a husband. The muhaqqiq in Fath al-Qadir and al-Tahrir rejected this, saying that making permissible was only made to remove the hardship of three divorces, so there is no hardship before it. So it appears that the correct view is that of Muhammad and the rest of the three Imams.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the effect of the second husband on the number of divorces the first husband can use after remarriage. The base ruling states that the second husband 'destroys' divorces below three, meaning that after the second marriage and divorce, the first husband regains the right to the full number of divorces allowable (three for a free woman, two for a slave), but the second husband's marriage reduces that number. The dispute is how many of those divorces are 'destroyed' by the second husband's single divorce. According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (the Sahibayn), if the first husband had divorced her once, the second husband's one divorce destroys that one, and the first husband can then use three divorces (for a free woman) as if starting anew. According to Imam Muhammad, the first husband can only use two divorces (for a free woman) because the second husband's one divorce only destroys one, leaving the first husband with the original remaining two. The proof cited is the hadith cursing the muhallil (one who marries to make a woman permissible) and the one for whom she is made permissible. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf use dalala (indication) that if the second husband is a muhallil in the severe case (triple divorce), he is even more so in lesser cases. Muhammad argues that the concept of tahlil only applies where there is hardship (the triple divorce), so without that hardship, the second husband does not fully erase the past. The later muhaqqiq (in Fath al-Qadir) supports Muhammad, and this is the relied-upon position. The passage also clarifies that this only applies if the second husband consummated the marriage; without consummation, no destruction occurs by consensus.

Key Terms
يهدم (yahdim): The second husband's marriage destroys the effect of previous divorces, resetting the count for the first husband.
المحلل (al-muhallil): A man who marries a woman divorced thrice with the aim of making her permissible to her first husband after divorcing her.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon (mufta bihi) view in the Hanafi school, if a free woman is divorced once by her first husband, then marries a second man who divorces her after consummation, how many divorces does the first husband retain?
A) Three divorces
B) Two divorces
C) One divorce
D) None, she becomes permanently prohibited

Key Takeaway
The second husband's consummated marriage destroys only one divorce, so the first husband retains two (free) or one (slave) — the view of Imam Muhammad is the operative ruling.

When a First Husband May Accept a Woman’s Report on Her 'Idda and Subsequent Marriage
Ruling on when a first husband may trust a woman's report about her second marriage and 'idda

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a first husband may rely on a woman’s report about her 'idda and second marriage, and explain the Hanafi rationale for accepting her word.

Core Idea
A woman’s report about her 'idda and marriage is accepted if the time is plausible and the husband has a strong impression of truthfulness.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq expands on a concise matn from Kanz al-Daqa'iq. It addresses a practical scenario: a thrice-divorced woman informs her first husband that she has completed her 'idda, married another man, that marriage ended, and she is now permissible. The Hanafi school allows the first husband to accept her report under certain conditions, treating it as a matter of transaction or religious permissibility. The sharh clarifies the conditions, the role of subjective impression, and the time frame for plausibility, while also addressing objections and varying opinions within the school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the first husband marrying his ex-wife based on her report. The ruling is that it is permissible (mubah) if conditions are met. The text states: 'he may believe her if he has a strong impression that she is truthful.'
The ruling is not wajib or haram.
2
Locate the rationale (dalil)
The rationale is that this is 'a transaction or a religious matter' (معاملة أو أمر ديني) where the statement of one person is accepted. Matters of permissibility and transactions are built on probable information, not conclusive evidence.
This is a general Hanafi principle: single testimony suffices in non-litigious religious matters.
3
Apply the condition of time plausibility
The woman's report must be physically possible. For the 'idda of a triple divorce, the minimum possible period is two months (Abu Hanifa) or 39 days (the Sahibayn). If less time has passed, her report is rejected as impossible.
The text explicitly restricts with 'and the period allows it' (والمدة تحتمله).
4
Note the subjective requirement of strong impression
The husband must have a strong impression (غلب على ظنه) of her truthfulness. This is a personal determination, not a legal testimony. Even if she is not 'adl (just) in court, he may believe her if his heart inclines.
Al-Sarakhsi requires questioning her thoroughly before marriage, but the mufta bihi is the lenient view.
5
Explore the ikhtilaf and resolutions
The text presents objections and intra-Hanafi differences: e.g., if the woman later contradicts herself about consummation, her later denial is accepted only if she was unaware of the legal conditions. Al-Sarakhsi's stricter view is mentioned but the general Hanafi position allows reliance on subjective impression.
These nuances show the school's systematic approach to balancing trust and caution.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ولو أخبرت مطلقة الثلاث بمضي عدته وعدة الزوج الثاني والمدة تحتمله له أن يصدقها إن غلب على ظنه صدقها) يعني للزوج الأول أن يتزوجها لأنه معاملة أو أمر ديني لتعلق الحل به وقول الواحد فيهما مقبول وهو غير مستنكر إذا كانت المدة تحتمله، وقد اقتصر المصنف في إخبارها على ما ذكر وذكره في الهداية مبسوطاً فقال : قالت قد انقضت عدتي وتزوجت ودخل بي الزوج وطلقني وانقضت عدتي وفي النهاية إنما ذكر إخبارها هكذا مبسوطاً لأنها لو قالت حللت لك فتزوجها ثم قالت لم يكن الثاني دخل بي إن كانت عالمة بشرائط الحل لم تصدق وإلا تصدق وفيما ذكرته مبسوطاً لا تصدق في كل حال. وعن السرخسي لا يحل له أن يتزوجها حتى يستفسرها لاختلاف بين الناس في حلها بمجرد العقد. وفي التفاريق: لو تزوجها ولم يسألها ثم قالت ما تزوجت أو ما دخل بي صدقت إذ لا يعلم ذلك إلا من جهتها واستشكل بأن إقدامها على النكاح دليل على اعتراف منها بصحته فكانت متناقضة فينبغي أن لا يقبل منها كما لو قالت بعد التزوج بها كنت مجوسية أو مرتدة أو معتدة أو منكوحة الغير أو كان العقد بغير شهود؛ ذكره في الجامع الكبير وغيره بخلاف قولها لم تنقض عدتي ولو قال الزوج لها ذلك وكذبته تقع الفرقة كأنه طلقها ولذا يجب عليه نصف المهر المسمى أو كماله ا هـ . من قائله ثم رأيت في الخلاصة ما يوافق الإشكال المذكور. وقال في الفتاوى في باب الباء : لو قالت بعدما تزوجها الأول ما تزوجت بآخر وقال الزوج الأول تزوجت بآخر ودخل بك لا تصدق المرأة ا ولو قال الزوج النكاح وقع فاسداً لأني جامعت أمها إن صدقته المرأة لا تحل للزوج الأول، وإن كذبته تحل؛ كذا أجاب القاضي الإمام. ولو قالت دخل بي الثاني والثاني منكر فالمعتبر قولها، وكذا على العكس. وفي النهاية : ولم يمر بي لو قال المحلل بعد الدخول كنت حلفت بطلاقها إن تزوجتها هل تحل للأول . قلت : يبتني الأمر على غالب ظنها إن كان صادقاً عندها فلا تحل له، وإن كان كاذباً تحل. وعن الفضلي : لو قالت تزوجني فإني تزوجت غيرك وانقضت عدتي فتزوجها ثم قالت ما تزوجت صدقت إلا أن تكون أقرت بدخول الثاني كأنه ـ والله أعلم - يحمل قولها تزوجت على العقد وقولها ما تزوجت على معنى ما دخل بي لا على إنكار ما اعترفت به ولذا قال إلا أن تكون أقرت بدخول الثاني فإنه لم يقبل قولها فإنه حينئذ تكون صريحة؛ كذا في فتح القدير. وأشار بقبول قولها إلى أنه لا عبرة بقول الزوج الثاني حتى لو قال لم أدخل بها أو كان النكاح فاسداً وكذبته فالمعتبر قولها، ولو قال الزوج الأول لها ذلك يعتبر قوله في حق الفرقة كأنه طلقها لا في حقها حتى يجب لها نصف المسمى أو كماله إن دخل بها. وأشار بقوله إن غلب على ظنه صدقها إلى أن عداتها ليست شرطاً ولهذا قال في البدائع وكافي الحاكم وغيرهما : لا بأس أن يصدقها إذا كانت ثقة عنده أو وقع في قلبه صدقها، وبقبول قول المطلقة إلى أن منكوحة رجل قالت لآخر طلقني زوجي وانقضت عدتي جاز تصديقها إذا وقع في الظن صدقها عدلة كانت أم لا، ولو قالت نكاحي الأول فاسد ليس له أن يصدقها وإن كانت عدلة ؛ كذا في البزازية فيها : سمع رجل من امرأة أنها مطلقة الثلاث والزوج يقول لا بل مطلقة الثنتين لا يسع لمن سمع منها أن يحضر نكاحها ويمنعها ما استطاع . أراد أن يتزوّج امرأة فشهد عنده أو عند القاضي أن لها زوجاً فتزوجها لا يفرق انتهى. وفيها قالت طلقني ثلاثاً ثم أرادت تزويج نفسها منه ليس لها ذلك أصرت عليه أم كذبت نفسها ا هـ . وقيد بقوله «والمدة تحتمله لأن المدة لو لم تحتمله فإنه لا يصدقها واحتمالها أن يذكر لكل عدة ما يمكن وهو شهران عند أبي حنيفة، وتسعة وثلاثون يوماً عندهما؛ تمامه في الشرح
His saying: (And if a woman divorced thrice informs him of the passage of her idda and the idda of the second husband, and the period allows it, he may believe her if he has a strong impression that she is truthful) means that the first husband may marry her, because it is a transaction or a religious matter due to permissibility being attached to it, and the statement of one person in these matters is accepted, and it is not objectionable if the period allows it. The author limited her report to what is mentioned, and it is mentioned in al-Hidayah in detail: she said, 'My idda has expired, I married, the husband consummated with me, divorced me, and my idda expired.' In al-Nihayah, he only mentioned her report in detail because if she said 'I have become permissible for you' and he married her, then she said 'the second did not consummate with me,' if she was aware of the conditions of permissibility, she is not believed; otherwise she is believed. In what he mentioned in detail, she is not believed in any case. Al-Sarakhsi said: it is not permissible for him to marry her until he questions her thoroughly due to disagreement among people regarding her permissibility by mere contract. In al-Tafariq: if he married her without asking, then she said 'I did not marry' or 'he did not consummate,' she is believed because that is only known from her. It is objected that her proceeding with marriage is evidence of her acknowledgment of its validity, so she contradicts herself and should not be accepted, just as if she said after marrying him, 'I was a Zoroastrian, apostate, in idda, or married to another, or the contract was without witnesses'; mentioned in al-Jami' al-Kabir and others, unlike her saying 'my idda did not expire.' If the husband (first) said that to her and she denies it, separation occurs as if he divorced her, so half or full dower is due. Then I saw in al-Khulasah something agreeing with the objection. And in al-Fatawa in Bab al-Ba': if she said after the first married her, 'I did not marry another,' and the first husband said 'you married another and he consummated with you,' the woman is not believed. If the husband said 'the marriage was invalid because I had intercourse with her mother,' if the woman confirms him, she is not permissible to the first husband; if she denies him, she is permissible. Similarly answered the chief judge. If she said 'the second consummated with me' and the second denies, her statement is considered, and vice versa. In al-Nihayah: I have not come across if the muhallil after consummation said 'I had sworn to divorce her when I married her,' does she become permissible to the first? I say: it depends on her predominant opinion; if she thinks he is truthful, she is not permissible; if she thinks he is lying, she is permissible. From al-Fadli: if she said 'marry me, for I married another and my idda expired,' then he married her, then she said 'I did not marry,' she is believed unless she had admitted consummation by the second; as if her saying 'I married' means the contract, and her saying 'I did not marry' means 'he did not consummate with me,' not denial of what she acknowledged. Hence he said 'unless she admitted consummation by the second,' because then she is explicit; so in Fath al-Qadir. His indication that her statement is accepted implies that no attention is paid to the second husband's statement; even if he says 'I did not consummate' or 'the marriage was invalid' and she denies him, her statement is considered. If the first husband says that to her, his statement is considered regarding separation (as if he divorced her), but not regarding her rights; so half or full dower is due if consummated. His indication 'if he has a strong impression that she is truthful' implies that her 'iddas are not a condition. Hence in al-Bada'i', Kafi al-Hakim, etc.: there is no harm in believing her if she is trustworthy in his view or his heart inclines to her truthfulness. Accepting the report of a divorced woman extends to a woman married to a man who says to another 'my husband divorced me and my idda expired' – it is allowed to believe her if her truthfulness is probable, whether she is just or not. If she says 'my first marriage was invalid,' he may not believe her even if she is just; so in al-Bazzaziyyah. In it: a man heard from a woman that she is thrice-divorced while the husband says 'no, she is twice-divorced' – it is not permissible for the one who heard her to attend her marriage and he should prevent her as much as possible. He wanted to marry a woman, and someone testified before him or the judge that she has a husband, then he married her – no separation. And in it: if she said 'he divorced me thrice' then she wanted to marry herself to him, she has no right to do so, whether she persists or retracts. And he restricted with his saying 'and the period allows it' because if the period does not allow it, he does not believe her. The allowance is that for each idda, what is possible is mentioned: two months according to Abu Hanifa, and thirty-nine days according to them; its completion is in the commentary.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the acceptance of a woman's report regarding the completion of her idda and the events of her second marriage, relevant for a first husband who wishes to remarry her. The matn states that the first husband may believe her if he has a strong impression of her truthfulness and the period allows it. The sharh expands on this by comparing short and detailed reports, discussing when a woman is or is not believed if she later contradicts herself. It outlines that her report is accepted because this is a matter of muamalah (transactions) or a religious matter where single testimony is normally accepted. However, if her report is implausible (period too short), she is not believed. Various scenarios are considered: if she fully details the second marriage and divorce, her later denial may be rejected; if she marries without asking, she may later be believed about non-consummation. The second husband's denial is not considered; her word is taken. The passage also addresses the first husband's statement: if he claims she is not permissible, his word is taken regarding separation but not for financial rights. The condition of 'predominant opinion' is emphasized, meaning it is not a strict evidentiary requirement but a personal judgment. Additional rulings are given for cases where the woman contradicts herself about the second marriage, or where the second husband claims he did not consummate. The passage also states that if the woman is trustworthy in the eyes of the first husband, he may act on her report even if she is not considered 'adi (just) in a legal sense. Finally, the duration of idda is discussed: the longest possible idda for a free woman is two months (Abu Hanifa) or 39 days (the Sahibayn). If the period is too short, her report is not accepted.

Key Terms
غلب على ظنه (ghalaba 'ala zannihi): A strong subjective impression of truthfulness, not required to meet the legal standard of testimony (shahadah).
عدتها ('iddatuhu): The waiting period of a woman after divorce or death of husband. Here, the 'idda of the first divorce and the 'idda after the second husband's divorce.
المدة تحتمله (al-mudda tahammalahu): The period of time is sufficient for the claimed events to have occurred. If impossible, she is not believed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the minimum time that must have passed for a woman's claim that she completed her 'idda after a triple divorce to be accepted?
A) One month
B) Two months according to Abu Hanifa, or 39 days according to the Sahibayn
C) Three months
D) No minimum; her word is always accepted

Key Takeaway
A first husband may rely on a woman's report about her 'idda and second marriage if the time is sufficient and he has a genuine strong impression of her truthfulness; this is a personal evaluation, not a court testimony.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate
8 min
Accepting a Woman's Claim About Her 'Iddah
Commentary on the conditions for a divorced woman to remarry and the validity of her testimony about her 'iddah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling regarding acceptance of a woman's claim about her 'iddah and apply the plausibility condition to different scenarios.

Core Idea
A woman's claim about her 'iddah is accepted if plausible, even within a day, due to possible early miscarriage.

This passage from Bahru al-Raiq explores the delicate balance between a woman's testimony and the presumption of innocence in marriage. The Hanafi school adopts a pragmatic approach: accept her claim if it is plausible, regardless of her character. This lesson will guide you through the key scenarios and an intra-Hanafi dispute about the minimum duration.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core principle
The author states that the woman's claim is accepted 'if the time allows it.' Determine what 'allow' means in this context.
It means the time period must be biologically possible for 'iddah to expire.
2
Examine the objection
The text mentions a criticism that her action of remarrying contradicts her claim. Understand why the author rejects that objection.
Her proceeding to marriage does not negate the possibility that her 'iddah ended.
3
Map the intra-Hanafi dispute
What is the minimum possible period for 'iddah according to Abu Hanifa vs. the Sahibayn? How does this affect plausibility?
Two months vs. thirty-nine days.
4
Apply the ruling to specific cases
Go through the scenarios: husband's denial, second husband's claim, tahlil issues. Identify when her statement is accepted and when it is not.
Key: unless she explicitly contradicts herself (e.g., initially admits second's entry then denies).
5
Evaluate the role of the husband's belief
The text says 'if he predominantly believes her truthfulness.' What does this imply about the ruling?
The permissibility of marriage is subjective based on his conviction, not an objective condition.

لا يعلم هـ. ذلك إلا من جهتها واستشكل بأن إقدامها على النكاح دليل على اعتراف منها بصحته فكانت متناقضة فينبغي أن لا يقبل منها كما لو قالت بعد التزوج بها كنت مجوسية أو مرتدة أو معتدة أو منكوحة الغير أو كان العقد بغير شهود؛ ذكره في الجامع الكبير وغيره بخلاف قولها لم تنقض عدتي ولو قال الزوج لها ذلك وكذبته تقع الفرقة كأنه طلقها ولذا يجب عليه نصف المهر المسمى أو كماله ا هـ . من قائله ثم رأيت في الخلاصة ما يوافق الإشكال المذكور. وقال في الفتاوى في باب الباء : لو قالت بعدما تزوجها الأول ما تزوجت بآخر وقال الزوج الأول تزوجت بآخر ودخل بك لا تصدق المرأة ا ولو قال الزوج النكاح وقع فاسداً لأني جامعت أمها إن صدقته المرأة لا تحل للزوج الأول، وإن كذبته تحل؛ كذا أجاب القاضي الإمام. ولو قالت دخل بي الثاني والثاني منكر فالمعتبر قولها، وكذا على العكس. وفي النهاية : ولم يمر بي لو قال المحلل بعد الدخول كنت حلفت بطلاقها إن تزوجتها هل تحل للأول . قلت : يبتني الأمر على غالب ظنها إن كان صادقاً عندها فلا تحل له، وإن كان كاذباً تحل. وعن الفضلي : لو قالت تزوجني فإني تزوجت غيرك وانقضت عدتي فتزوجها ثم قالت ما تزوجت صدقت إلا أن تكون أقرت بدخول الثاني كأنه ـ والله أعلم - يحمل قولها تزوجت على العقد وقولها ما تزوجت على معنى ما دخل بي لا على إنكار ما اعترفت به ولذا قال إلا أن تكون أقرت بدخول الثاني فإنه لم يقبل قولها فإنه حينئذ تكون صريحة؛ كذا في فتح القدير. وأشار بقبول قولها إلى أنه لا عبرة بقول الزوج الثاني حتى لو قال لم أدخل بها أو كان النكاح فاسداً وكذبته فالمعتبر قولها، ولو قال الزوج الأول لها ذلك يعتبر قوله في حق الفرقة كأنه طلقها لا في حقها حتى يجب لها نصف المسمى أو كماله إن دخل بها. وأشار بقوله إن غلب على ظنه صدقها إلى أن عداتها ليست شرطاً ولهذا قال في البدائع وكافي الحاكم وغيرهما : لا بأس أن يصدقها إذا كانت ثقة عنده أو وقع في قلبه صدقها، وبقبول قول المطلقة إلى أن منكوحة رجل قالت لآخر طلقني زوجي وانقضت عدتي جاز تصديقها إذا وقع في الظن صدقها عدلة كانت أم لا، ولو قالت نكاحي الأول فاسد ليس له أن يصدقها وإن كانت عدلة ؛ كذا في البزازية فيها : سمع رجل من امرأة أنها مطلقة الثلاث والزوج يقول لا بل مطلقة الثنتين لا يسع لمن سمع منها أن يحضر نكاحها ويمنعها ما استطاع . أراد أن يتزوّج امرأة فشهد عنده أو عند القاضي أن لها زوجاً فتزوجها لا يفرق انتهى. وفيها قالت طلقني ثلاثاً ثم أرادت تزويج نفسها منه ليس لها ذلك أصرت عليه أم كذبت نفسها ا هـ . وقيد بقوله «والمدة تحتمله لأن المدة لو لم تحتمله فإنه لا يصدقها واحتمالها أن يذكر لكل عدة ما يمكن وهو شهران عند أبي حنيفة، وتسعة وثلاثون يوماً عندهما؛ تمامه في الشرح ولكن في القنية برقم شب : قالت المعتدة أسقطت سقطاً استبان خلقه أو بعض خلقه تصدق وتنقضي به العدة وإن أخبرت بعد الطلاق بساعة أو يوم ففي مناقضة بق : إذا قالت انقضت عدتي في يوم أو أقل تصدق أيضاً وإن لم تقل سقط لاحتماله. بو: فقولهم الامكان بشهرين عند الإمام محله ما إذا لم تقل أسقطت سقطاً استبان بعض خلقه وجزمهم بهذه المدة دليل على ضعف قول من قال بقبول قولها انقضت عدتي بعد يوم أو أقل لاحتمال سقوط سقط من غير تصريح منها بذلك والله سبحانه وتعالى أعلم بالصواب وإليه المرجع والمآب.
That is not known except from her side. It is objected that her proceeding with marriage is evidence of her acknowledgment of its validity, so she is contradictory and should not be accepted from her, just as if she said after marrying: I was a Magian, an apostate, in 'iddah, another's wife, or the contract was without witnesses; this is mentioned in al-Jami' al-Kabir and others, unlike her statement: my 'iddah has not expired. If the husband says that to her and he lies, separation occurs as if he divorced her; therefore, half of the named dower or the full amount is obligatory, etc. From its author, then I saw in al-Khulasa something agreeing with the mentioned objection. And it is said in al-Fatawa in the chapter on B: If she says after her first husband married her: I did not marry another, and the first husband says: you married another and he entered upon you, the woman is not believed. And if the husband says: the marriage is invalid because I had intercourse with her mother, if the woman believes him, she is not permissible for the first husband; if she disbelieves him, she is permissible; thus answered the chief judge. And if she says: the second entered upon me, and the second denies, her statement is considered, and similarly the opposite. And in al-Nihaya: It has not occurred to me: If the tahlil after entry says: I had sworn by her divorce that I would marry her, does she become permissible for the first? I said: It depends on her predominant opinion; if he is truthful in her view, she is not permissible for him; if he is lying, she is permissible. And from al-Fadli: If she says: Marry me, for I married someone else and my 'iddah expired, so he married her, then she says: I did not marry, she is believed unless she has confessed to the second's entry. It is as if, and Allah knows best, her saying 'I married' is interpreted as the contract and her saying 'I did not marry' as meaning 'he did not enter upon me', not as denial of what she admitted. Therefore he said: unless she confessed to the second's entry, then her statement is not accepted, because then she is explicit. Thus in Fath al-Qadir. And he indicated by accepting her statement that the second husband's statement is not considered; even if he said: I did not enter upon her or the marriage was invalid and she lies, her statement is considered. And if the first husband says that to her, his statement is considered regarding separation, as if he divorced her, not regarding her right, so half of the named dower or the full amount is obligatory if he entered upon her. And he indicated by his saying 'if he predominantly believes her truthfulness' that her 'iddah is not a condition, and therefore he said in al-Bada'i', Kafi al-Hakim and others: There is no harm in believing her if she is trustworthy in his view or he is inclined in his heart to believe her. And by accepting the statement of the divorced woman who is a wife of a man who says to another: My husband divorced me and my 'iddah expired, it is permissible to believe her if he is disposed to think she is truthful, whether she is just or not. And if she says: My first marriage is invalid, he may not believe her even if she is just. Thus in al-Bazzaziyya: A man heard from a woman that she is triple-divorced, and the husband says: No, she is double-divorced; it is not permissible for the one who heard from her to attend her marriage and to prevent her as much as he can. He wanted to marry a woman, and a witness testified before him or before the judge that she has a husband, so he married her; it is not annulled. End. And in it: She said: He divorced me thrice, then she wanted to marry herself to him; she has no right to do that, whether she persists or contradicts herself. End. And he restricted it with his saying 'and the time allows it' because if the time did not allow it, he would not believe her. Its allowance is that he mentions for each 'iddah what is possible: two months according to Abu Hanifa, and thirty-nine days according to them. Its completion is in the Sharh. But in al-Quniyya, number Sh: If a woman in 'iddah says: I miscarried a foetus whose limbs were distinguished or part of them, she is believed and the 'iddah ends therewith, even if she informs after divorce by an hour or a day. So in the objection: Remaining: If she says: My 'iddah expired in a day or less, she is also believed, even if she does not mention miscarriage, due to possibility. By: So their statement 'possibility with two months according to the Imam' applies when she does not say 'I miscarried a foetus whose limbs were distinguished'. Their determination of this period is evidence of the weakness of the saying that accepts her statement 'my 'iddah expired after a day or less' due to the possibility of a miscarriage without her explicit mention of that. And Allah, the Exalted, knows best what is correct, and to Him is the return and refuge.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage is a ruling-with-proof commentary on the acceptance of a woman's testimony regarding the completion of her 'iddah. The commentator discusses various scenarios where a woman's claim about her 'iddah is accepted or rejected. The overarching principle is that her statement is generally accepted if it is plausible, but not if it contradicts her previous actions or if the time frame is impossible. For example, if she claims her 'iddah expired in a day or less, it is still accepted due to the possibility of a miscarriage. However, if the husband objects and she contradicts herself, the ruling depends on the strength of her claim. The commentator cites multiple authorities including al-Jami' al-Kabir, al-Fatawa, al-Nihaya, and Fath al-Qadir to illustrate the nuanced jurisprudence. He also notes an intra-Hanafi difference: Abu Hanifa considers two months as the default possible period for 'iddah, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad consider thirty-nine days. This affects the plausibility assessment. Importantly, the woman's trustworthiness and the husband's inclination also play a role in the judge's acceptance.

Key Terms
عدة ('Iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death of husband before she can remarry.
محلل (Muhallil): A man who marries a triple-divorced woman to make her permissible for her first husband after divorcing her.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Two months
C) Three months
D) One month

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school accepts a woman's claim about her 'iddah if it is plausible, even for very short periods, due to the possibility of early miscarriage, and the man's personal conviction plays a role.

The Definition of Ila' and the Scope of Conditional Oaths
Commentary defining ila' and addressing objections regarding inclusion of conditional oaths.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of ila' and distinguish between the opinions of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding conditional oaths that do not involve hardship.

Core Idea
Ila' requires an oath of abstention for four months; conditional oaths only count if they involve hardship (mufta bihi: Abu Yusuf).

The definition of ila' may seem straightforward—an oath to avoid intercourse for four months—but the Hanafi school debates whether conditional oaths (e.g., vowing a prayer if intercourse occurs) qualify. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq unpacks that debate, showing how the scope of ila' affects whether a wife can seek judicial divorce after four months.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of ila'
The passage distinguishes real ila' (direct oath of abstention) and metaphorical ila' (conditional oath that binds something harmful upon approach). Identify each type from the Arabic classification.
Real ila' uses explicit language like 'I swear I will not approach you.'
2
Locate the dispute over non-hardship conditional oaths
The author addresses an objection from Ibn al-Humam: does a conditional oath without hardship (e.g., 'if I have intercourse, I will pray two rak'ahs') constitute ila'? Note that the definition given by the author of Kanz al-Daqa'iq would include it.
3
Contrast the positions of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad
Abu Yusuf holds that such an oath is not ila' because it lacks a deterrent (hardship). Muhammad holds that any conditional oath upon intercourse is ila' because it binds the person. Identify the reasoning each used.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
4
Apply the mufta bihi to a specific case
The relied-upon position is Abu Yusuf's. Given a man who says 'If I have intercourse, I will pray two rak'ahs', determine that he is not a muli and explain the practical consequence: the four-month rule does not apply.
The tarjih authority is the statement 'the relied-upon is Abu Yusuf's saying'.

Source Text

As for what is like an oath, which is the conditional binding of something harmful on approach, we will discuss it afterwards. Commentary
This passage is a definition ruling with proof and dispute. It begins by defining ila' as an oath to refrain from approaching one's wife for four months or more. The commentator distinguishes between two types: real ila' (oath explicitly stating abstention) and metaphorical ila' (conditional oaths that threaten hardship). The main dispute involves the inclusion of conditional oaths that do not cause harm (e.g., vowing to pray two rak'ahs if intercourse occurs). Abu Yusuf says this does not constitute ila', while Muhammad says it does. The author of Kanz al-Daqa'iq likely defined ila' in a way that covers both types to avoid dispute. The passage then addresses an objection from Ibn al-Humam regarding the scope of the definition and provides a second answer that the definition is for the agreed-upon type. The commentary also cites al-Nahr and Sharh al-Maqdisi to analyze the objections, ultimately supporting the inclusive definition.

Key Terms
إيلاء (Ila'): An oath to refrain from sexual intercourse with one's wife for four months or more.
مؤلى عليه (Mu'la 'alayhi): The wife from whom the husband has sworn to abstain.

D) No, because it is not an oath

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of ila' includes both direct oaths and conditional oaths that involve hardship; the mufta bihi is Abu Yusuf's opinion that only hardship-based conditions create ila'.

Ruling on Ila' When Wife is Menstruating
Explains the ruling on ila' when husband vows not to approach menstruating wife, with exceptions and related cases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling that ila' is not established when the wife is menstruating without a specified period, and identify the exception when a four-month period is mentioned.

Core Idea
If the prohibition already exists naturally, the oath adds nothing—unless a specific period is stated.

This lesson from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a nuanced scenario: a husband swears not to approach his wife while she is menstruating. The central question is whether this constitutes ila' (a vow of abstinence). The author explains that because the husband is already prohibited from intercourse during menstruation, the oath does not create a new prohibition, so ila' is not established. However, if the husband specifies a duration like four months, the prohibition becomes attributable to the oath, and ila' is established. The text also discusses related cases involving time/place restrictions, sharing ila' between wives, and the difference between explicit and implicit expressions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
The passage states that if a husband swears not to approach his wife while she is menstruating, without specifying a period, he is not a moli.
2
Understand the reasoning (ta'lil)
The reason is that the prohibition is already present due to menstruation, so the oath does not add a new prohibition. The prohibition is attributed to the natural state, not the oath.
This is a qiyas based on the existing prohibition.
3
Recognize the exception
If the husband specifies a period of four months, the specification indicates that the prohibition is from the oath, making him a moli. This is from al-Hawashi al-Sa'diyyah.
This is an expansion of the base text.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Ibn Abi Layla differs on time/place restrictions, but the Hanafi position is that such restrictions prevent ila' because the husband can approach in another time/place.

So if he approaches the foreign woman, he cannot approach her except with expiation necessitated, and it becomes as if he swore not to approach his wife and his slave-girl. And upon this, if he swore not to approach her while she was in ihram or fasting an obligatory fast, it is likewise, because the period of menstruation and similar does not last four months, so its condition is not found. Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq discusses the ruling of ila' when the wife is menstruating. The passage type is a ruling with proof, specifically an expansion of a ruling from the base text (Kanz al-Daqa'iq). The text first establishes that if a husband swears not to approach his wife while she is menstruating, he is not considered a moli (one who performs ila') because the husband is already prohibited from intercourse by the menstruation itself. Therefore, the prohibition is attributed to the natural state, not to the oath. This shows that even explicit expressions do not require intention to constitute ila', but here a diverting factor prevents it. The author then discusses various scenarios: sharing ila' between two wives (not valid in ila' but valid in zihar), the difference between saying 'You are forbidden to me' and 'By Allah, I will not approach you both', vows with time or place restrictions, and vows involving both wife and a foreign woman. The text then returns to the issue of menstruation, noting that if the husband specifies a period of four months despite the menstruation, he becomes a moli because the specification indicates the prohibition is due to the oath. The commentator al-Maqdisi is cited as interpreting the phrase 'while she was menstruating' as a circumstantial clause describing the wife, not the swearing, leading to the same conclusion. The discussion emphasizes that the intention of the husband and the presence of other prohibitions affect the validity of ila'.

Key Terms
الإيلاء (al-īlā'): A vow to abstain from sexual relations with one's wife for a period of four months or more.
المولي (al-mūlī): The husband who performs ila'.
الصريح (al-ṣarīḥ): Explicit expression that clearly indicates ila' without need for intention.

Key Terms from Source Passage

According to the Hanafi school, when does a husband's oath not to approach his menstruating wife constitute ila'?
A) Whenever the oath is explicit and sworn by Allah.
B) Only if he specifies a period of four months.
C) Only if he also intends divorce.
D) Never, because menstruation always prevents ila'.

Key Takeaway
The establishment of ila' depends on whether the prohibition is attributed to the oath or to an existing natural prohibition; specification of four months shifts the attribution to the oath.

Distinguishing Ila' Wording: Skin vs. Intercourse
Rules for ila' wording and the menstruation exception

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between expressions that constitute ila' (vow of abstention) and those that do not, based on Hanafi fiqh, and apply the ruling to new verbal scenarios.

Core Idea
Ila' requires a vow specifically about intercourse; general touch oaths are not ila' without intention.

In this passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, the author clarifies the precise wording needed for a valid ila'. The key distinction is whether the oath explicitly refers to intercourse (e.g., 'my penis touches your vagina') or merely to touching (e.g., 'my skin touches your skin'). Relying on later authoritative works (al-Jawharah, al-Walwaljiyyah), the Hanafi position is that only the former constitutes ila' without intention; the latter requires explicit intent. This ruling is based on customary usage (urf) and the principle that ila' is fundamentally about abstaining from intercourse.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Recognize that ila' is a husband's vow to abstain from sexual relations with his wife for more than four months. The ruling is that after four months, the wife may demand either resumption of relations or divorce.
Ila' itself is not wajib; its consequences are.
2
Locate the proof (dalil)
The dalil is istihsan based on 'urf (customary usage). The text distinguishes between expressions based on common understanding: 'skin touches skin' does not necessarily imply intercourse, while 'penis touches vagina' explicitly does.
Istihsan overrides qiyas here because of the contextual difference.
3
Distinguish the two expressions
Compare the two oaths: (1) 'By Allah, my skin does not touch your skin' – this does not constitute ila' without intention because touching can occur without intercourse. (2) 'By Allah, my penis does not touch your vagina' – this constitutes ila' because it clearly refers to intercourse.
The mufta bihi is that the second expression is ila' even without intention.
4
Consider the menstruation exception
If the husband swears not to approach his wife while she is menstruating, he is not a moli because the period of abstinence is less than four months and the prohibition is from menstruation, not the oath.
Al-Walwaljiyyah supports this exception.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The operative ruling for fatwa: Expressions like 'my skin touches your skin' require intention to form ila'; expressions like 'my penis touches your vagina' constitute ila' even without intention.
This is based on al-Jawharah and al-Walwaljiyyah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن كان يرجى وجوده مع بقاء النكاح كان مولياً مثل أن يقول إن قربتك فعبدي حر ؛ كذا في الجوهرة وقيد بالقربان لأنه لو قال والله لا يمس جلدي جلدك لا يكون مولياً لأنه يحنث في يمينه بالمس بدون الجماع في الفرج. ولو قال والله لا يمس فرجي فرجك يكون مولياً لأنه يراد بهذا الكلام الجماع في الفرج. ولو قال لامرأته إن قربتك أو دعوتك إلى فراشي فأنت طالق لا يكون مولياً لأنه يمكنه قربانها من غير وقوع من قال وبهذا علم أن الصريح وإن كان لا يحتاج إلى نية لا يقع به لوجود صارف» ظاهره أنه لما كانت حائضاً وحلف كان حيضها مانعاً من الوطء لا اليمين فإن أراد أن الأربعة أشهر التي يمنع نفسه فيها تكون خالية من الحيض ونحوه من الموانع فهذا لم يقل به أحد ولم يقيد بذلك في كلام أحد وإنما المراد ما بينا اهـ. فليتأمل. ثم رأيت في الولوالجية ما يشير إلى تأييد بحثه حيث قال : ولو حلف لا يقربها وهي حائض لم يكن مولياً لأنه منع نفسه عن قربانها في مدة الحيض وأنه أقل من أربعة أشهر اهـ. نعم قوله فإن أراد الخ غير وارد لأن الكلام فيما لم يقيد بمدة كما مر عن سعدي، وكذا المسألة المنقولة عن غاية البيان . هو كذلك في تصویر قوله : (لأنه لو قال والله لا يمس جلدي جلدك لا يكون مولياً) يعني بلا نية كما مر .
And if its occurrence is expected while the marriage continues, he is a moli, such as if he says 'If I approach you, my slave is free'; thus in al-Jawharah. And he restricted with 'approach' because if he said 'By Allah, my skin does not touch your skin', he is not a moli, because he would violate his oath by touching without intercourse in the vagina. And if he said 'By Allah, my penis does not touch your vagina', he is a moli, because this speech refers to intercourse in the vagina. And if he said to his wife 'If I approach you or invite you to my bed, you are divorced', he is not a moli, because he can approach her without occurrence. And from his saying: 'By this it is known that the explicit (expression) although it does not require intention, does not occur here due to the presence of a diverting factor.' Its apparent meaning is that when she was menstruating and he swore, her menstruation prevented intercourse, not the oath. If he meant that the four months in which he prevents himself are free from menstruation and similar prohibitions, then no one has said this, nor was it restricted in anyone's words. Rather, what is intended is what we have clarified. End quote. Then let it be considered. Then I saw in al-Walwaljiyyah something indicating support for his research, where he said: If he swore not to approach her while she was menstruating, he is not a moli, because he prevented himself from approaching her during the period of menstruation, and it is less than four months. End quote. Yes, his saying 'if he meant...' is not applicable, because the discussion concerns what is not specified with a period, as passed from Sa'di. And likewise the issue transmitted from Ghayat al-Bayan. It is so in the depiction of his saying: (Because if he said 'By Allah, my skin does not touch your skin', he is not a moli), meaning without intention, as passed.

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses the ruling on ila' when the vow includes a future condition. The passage type is a ruling with proof, specifically an expansion on various scenarios. The text starts by stating that if the condition is expected to occur while the marriage continues, the husband is a moli. An example is 'If I approach you, my slave is free'. Then it discusses the difference between expressions that refer to touching (not ila') versus those that specifically refer to intercourse (ila'). The ruling is based on the literal meaning and common usage. The text then revisits the menstruation issue, clarifying that the previous discussion about sarih expressions not requiring intention but being diverted does not apply if the husband specified a period. The authority of al-Walwaljiyyah is cited to support that ila' is not established when the wife is menstruating because the period of prohibition is less than four months. This lesson emphasizes the importance of wording and intention in determining ila'.

Key Terms
القربان (al-qurbān): Sexual intercourse in the context of ila'.
اليمين (al-yamīn): An oath that forms the basis of ila'.

D) 'By Allah, I will not touch your hand.'

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, ila' is only established when the vow explicitly refers to intercourse; general touch oaths require intention.

Definition of Ila' and Valid Oaths
Defines Ila' and lists valid oath phrases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between valid oaths (by Allah or His attributes) and invalid oaths for Ila', and state the Hanafi ruling on the role of intention.

Core Idea
An oath for Ila' must be by Allah or His attributes; swearing by His knowledge or wrath does not count.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq defines Ila' and clarifies which oaths establish it. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly determining the legal status of a husband's oath.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act being defined
Ila' is an oath to abstain from intercourse for four months or more. Recognize that the passage defines both the act and the conditions for a valid oath.
Ila' is a prelude to potential divorce after four months.
2
Note the conditions for a valid oath
The oath must be by Allah or His attributes (e.g., 'by Allah', 'by His greatness'). These are the only phrases that constitute a binding oath.
Implicit oaths like 'you are forbidden' require intention of Ila'.
3
Recognize what is excluded
Oaths by 'knowledge of Allah' or 'wrath of Allah' do not form a valid Ila'. They are not binding.
These are not considered attributes that establish an oath.
4
Consider the role of intention
For explicit oaths (by Allah/attributes), intention is not required; for implicit oaths (like 'you are forbidden'), intention to make Ila' is necessary.
Intention differentiates Ila' from a general prohibition.
5
Apply the rule to distinguish binding from non-binding oaths
Test each phrase against the criteria: is it by Allah or an attribute? If yes, it's valid Ila'; if not, it is not.
This is the core of the ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطلاق باب الإيلاء [؟] امرأة فلان وقد كان فلان آلى من امرأته فإن كان نوى الإيلاء كان مولياً وإلا فلا. ومنها ما لو قال أنت علي كالميتة كذا في الظهيرية وسيأتي أنت علي حرام وأراد بقوله والله ما ينعقد به اليمين كقوله فالله وعظمة الله وجلاله وكبريائه فخرج ما لا ينعقد به اليمين كقوله «وعلم الله لا أقربك» و «علي غضب الله وسخطه إن قربتك»
Book of Divorce, Chapter of Ila'. ... a man's wife and he had sworn Ila' from his wife. If he intended Ila', he is an muli, otherwise not. Among them is if he said 'You are like a dead thing for me' as in al-Zahiriya, and 'You are forbidden for me' will come. And he intended by his saying 'By Allah' what an oath is made with, like his saying 'By Allah' and 'By the Greatness of Allah' and 'By His Majesty' and 'By His Grandeur'. So excluded is what an oath is not made with, like his saying 'By the knowledge of Allah I will not approach you' and 'Upon me is the wrath of Allah and His displeasure if I approach you'.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement defining Ila' and specifying which oaths constitute it. The author states that if a man says 'my wife' and had intended Ila', he is considered an muli (one who has sworn Ila'). Otherwise not. Then it mentions that saying 'you are like a dead thing' is considered, as in al-Zahiriya, and the ruling for 'you are forbidden for me' will be discussed later. The key point: an oath that binds must be by Allah (swearing by Allah) or by His attributes like greatness, majesty, grandeur. Swearing by 'Knowledge of Allah' or 'wrath of Allah' does not form a binding oath. This distinction is crucial: only oaths by Allah or His attributes create the legal effect for Ila'.

Key Terms
الإيلاء (al-īlā’): An oath by Allah to abstain from sexual intercourse with one's wife for four months or more.
مولٍ (mulin): The husband who has sworn Ila'.
يمين (yamīn): An oath; specifically here, an oath by Allah or His attributes that is legally binding.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) By the knowledge of Allah
B) By the greatness of Allah
C) Upon me is the wrath of Allah
D) You are like a dead thing (without intention)

Key Takeaway
For Ila' to be valid, the husband must swear by Allah or His attributes; swearing by knowledge or wrath does not count.

The Core Ruling of Ila' with a Ghayah: The Four-Month Threshold and the Istihsan Exception
A detailed exposition of the rules of Ila' with time limits.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the four-month threshold to determine if an oath constitutes Ila' and explain how istihsan overrides qiyas for oaths implying permanence.

Core Idea
If the event in the oath cannot happen within four months, it is Ila'; if it could, qiyas says no but istihsan says yes when custom implies permanence.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq delves into the intricacies of Ila' when a time limit or condition (ghayah) is attached. The author first establishes a clear quantitative rule: whether the event can occur within four months. Then he introduces a critical methodological conflict—qiyas versus istihsan—when the phrase, though literally possible soon, is understood in custom as perpetual.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is swearing an oath to abstain from intercourse with a time limit. The ruling: it becomes Ila' if the limit is four months or more.
This is the general principle derived from the definition of Ila'.
2
Locate the dalil
The dalil is the legal reasoning based on the definition of Ila' (abstention for four months or more). No specific Quranic verse or hadith is cited; the proof is ijtihad.
The Hanafi school defines Ila' as abstaining for four months or more, so any oath with a limit of that duration fits.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The definitional rule: if the event cannot occur within four months, the oath amounts to abstention for at least that period, hence Ila'. If it can occur within four months, it is not Ila' by default.
Examples: fasting Muharram from Rajab (>4 months) and distant travel (≥4 months) are Ila'; less than four months is not.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
For events like 'until the sun rises from the west', by qiyas it is not Ila' because the event is expected any moment. However, the Hanafi school adopts istihsan due to custom.
The opposing view (pure qiyas) would rule it not Ila'; the Hanafi tarjih is istihsan.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The Hanafi position uses istihsan to align with common understanding, making the oath Ila' to protect the wife's rights. This is supported by the custom (urf) that such phrases indicate permanence.
Tarjih authority: Al-Marghinani in al-Hidayah prefers istihsan over qiyas.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن جعل للإيلاء غاية إن كان لا وجودها في مدة الإيلاء كان مولياً كما إذا قال والله لا أقربك حتى أصوم المحرم وهو في رجب أو لا أقربك إلا في مكان كذا وبينه مسيرة أربعة أشهر فصاعداً فإنه يكون مولياً، وإن كان أقل لم يكن مولياً. وكذا إذا قال حتى تفطمي طفلك وبينها وبين الفطام أربعة أشهر فصاعداً فإنه يكون مولياً وإن كان أقل لم يكن مولياً. وإن قال لا أقربك حتى تطلع الشمس من مغربها أو حتى تخرج الدابة أو الدجال كان القياس أن لا يكون مولياً لأنه يرجى وجود ذلك ساعة فساعة وفي الاستحسان يكون مولياً لأن هذا اللفظ في العرف والعادة إنما يكون للتأبيد. وكذا إذا قال حتى تقوم الساعة أو قال حتى يلج الجمل في سم الخياط فإنه يكون مولياً، فإن كان يرجى وجوده في المدة لا مع بقاء النكاح فإنه يكون مولياً أيضاً مثل أن يقول والله لا أقربك حتى تموتي أو أقتل أو حتى أطلقك ثلاثاً فإنه يكون مولياً إجماعاً. وكذا إذا كانت أمة فقال لا أقربك حتى أملكك أو أملك شقصاً منك يكون مولياً. وإن قال حتى أشتريك لا يكون مولياً لأنه قد يشتريها لغيره ولا يفسد النكاح. ولو قال حتى أشتريك لنفسي لا يكون مولياً أيضاً لأنه ربما يشتريها لنفسه شراء فاسداً. ولو قال اشتريتك لنفسي وأقبضك كان مولياً. وإن كان يرجى وجوده مع بقاء النكاح كان مولياً مثل أن يقول إن قربتك فعبدي حر
And if he sets a limit for the Ila', if that limit does not occur within the period of Ila', he is an muli. For example, if he says, 'By Allah, I will not approach you until I fast Muharram,' and he is in Rajab, or 'I will not approach you except in such and such place' and between them is a distance of four months or more, then he is an muli. And if it is less, he is not an muli. Likewise, if he says, 'Until you wean your child' and between her and weaning is four months or more, then he is an muli; if less, not. And if he says, 'I will not approach you until the sun rises from the west,' or 'until the beast comes out' or 'the Dajjal,' then by qiyas he is not an muli because its occurrence is expected moment by moment, but by istihsan he is an muli because in custom these words are for perpetuity. Likewise, if he says, 'Until the Hour is established,' or 'until the camel passes through the eye of a needle,' then he is an muli. And if it is expected to occur in the period but not with the continuation of the marriage, then he is also an muli, like if he says, 'By Allah, I will not approach you until you die or I am killed or until I divorce you three times,' then he is an muli by consensus. Likewise, if she is a slave and he says, 'I will not approach you until I own you or own a share of you,' he is an muli. And if he says, 'until I buy you,' he is not an muli because he may buy her for someone else and the marriage is not invalidated. And if he says, 'until I buy you for myself,' he is also not an muli because he might buy her for himself with an invalid purchase. And if he says, 'I have bought you for myself and taken possession,' he is an muli. And if it is expected to occur with the continuation of the marriage, he is an muli, like if he says, 'If I approach you, my slave is free.'
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage is a detailed ruling with proof about Ila' when a time limit (ghayah) is set. The author explains the rule: if the limit is such that the event will not occur within four months (the duration of Ila'), then it is Ila'; otherwise not. Several examples are given: fasting Muharram starting from Rajab (which is more than four months away), a journey of four months or more, weaning a child with four months or more until weaning — these are Ila'. If the limit is less than four months, it is not Ila'. Then a subtle point: for events like sunrise from west or appearance of Dajjal, qiyas (analogy) says it is not Ila' because these are expected any moment, but istihsan (juristic preference) overrides and says it is Ila' because in common usage these phrases indicate perpetuity. For impossible events like the Day of Judgment or camel through needle's eye, it is Ila' by istihsan. Also, if the event is expected but would end the marriage (e.g., death, or triple divorce), it is Ila' by consensus. For slave wives, the same principle applies: if the condition is that he will own her (by purchase), then if it is certain he will own her (e.g., he has already bought and taken possession) it is Ila', otherwise not because the purchase may be invalid or for another. Finally, if the condition is possible while the marriage continues (e.g., freeing a slave), it is also Ila'.

Key Terms
غاية (ghāyah): A time limit or condition set in the oath that ends the prohibition.
القياس (al-qiyās): Analogical reasoning that would consider the oath not Ila' due to possible occurrence within 4 months.
الاستحسان (al-istiḥsān): Juristic preference that overrides qiyas due to custom (urf).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a man swears, 'By Allah, I will not approach you until the sun rises from the west,' what is the Hanafi ruling?
A) Not Ila' by qiyas
B) Ila' by istihsan
C) Not Ila' by ijma
D) Ila' only if he intends more than 4 months

Key Takeaway
The key to Ila' with a ghayah is whether the event is expected within four months; if custom treats it as permanent, istihsan overrides qiyas.

Ila' with Ghayah: Special Cases (Impossible Events, Marriage-Ending Events, and Slave Wives)
Additional clarifications on Ila' with unusual conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify oaths that end the marriage or are impossible and determine Ila' status for slave wives.

Core Idea
Oaths that end marriage (death, divorce) are Ila' by consensus; impossible events are Ila' due to custom; slave wife rules depend on certainty of ownership.

The same passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq continues with exceptional scenarios that reinforce the principles of Ila'. These include events that would dissolve the marriage, completely impossible occurrences, and conditions involving slave wives. The rulings illustrate how the Hanafi school consistently applies custom (urf) and consensus (ijma) to protect the wife's rights.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position on marriage-ending events
For oaths like 'until you die', 'until I am killed', or 'until I divorce you three times', it is Ila' by consensus. The event ends the marriage, but the prohibition remains until then, making it Ila'.
These events are expected to occur eventually but without continuation of the marriage.
2
Identify the basis for impossible events
For impossible events (Day of Judgment, camel through needle), the Hanafi ruling is Ila' by istihsan because such phrases are customarily used to imply permanent abstention.
Qiyas would also likely say it's Ila' because the event never occurs, but the text emphasizes istihsan.
3
Note the ruling for slave wives
If the husband says 'until I own you' or 'until I own a share of you,' it is Ila' because ownership is possible. If he says 'until I buy you,' it is not Ila' because the purchase could be invalid or for someone else. But if he says 'I have bought you and taken possession,' it is Ila'.
The key is certainty: if ownership is virtually certain (already bought), it is Ila'; if uncertain, not.
4
Understand the condition for events that preserve the marriage
If the event can occur while the marriage continues (e.g., 'if I approach you, my slave is free'), it is also Ila'.
This shows that Ila' is not limited to negative conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Key Terms
غاية (ghāyah): A time limit or condition set in the oath that ends the prohibition.
إجماع (ijmāʿ): Consensus of scholars; used for events that end the marriage.
أمة (amah): A female slave; the ruling for Ila' with slave wives involves special conditions.

Checkpoint
A man swears to his slave wife, 'I will not approach you until I buy you and take possession.' He has already bought her and taken possession. What is the ruling?
A) Not Ila' because the purchase is complete.
B) Ila' because ownership is certain.
C) Not Ila' because the marriage continues.
D) Ila' only if he intends more than 4 months.

Key Takeaway
Ila' rulings adjust based on the type of event: marriage-ending events are Ila' by consensus, impossible events by istihsan, and slave wife cases depend on certainty of ownership.

Ila' When Condition Ends Marriage or Slavery
Ruling on Ila' with condition ending marriage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition for Ila' and apply the rule to examples where the condition may or may not end the marriage.

Core Idea
Ila' is established only when the condition guarantees the marriage will end within the period.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies a nuanced rule in Ila'. When a husband makes an oath not to approach his wife, and attaches a condition that, if fulfilled, would end the marriage (like death or triple divorce), he becomes an muli by consensus. But if the condition is uncertain — such as buying a slave wife without ownership — then Ila' does not take effect. The key is certainty that the marriage will terminate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
Recognize that the passage states by consensus (ijma) that an oath becomes Ila' when the condition ends the marriage (e.g., death or triple divorce).
This is a unanimous ruling.
2
Extract the condition
Note the condition for Ila': the oath must be attached to an event that, if it occurs, will certainly end the marriage within the period of Ila' (four months).
Uncertainty prevents Ila'.
3
Apply to the free wife example
The examples: 'until you die', 'until I am killed', 'until I divorce you three times' all end the marriage, so Ila' is established.
These events are certain to dissolve the marriage.
4
Apply to the slave wife example
For a slave wife, 'until I own you' or 'own a share' ends the marriage upon ownership, but 'until I buy you' is uncertain because purchase may be for another or invalid, so not Ila'.
Only certain ownership triggers Ila'.
5
Conclude the principle
Summarize: Ila' depends on the condition being a definite end to the marriage; ambiguous conditions do not suffice.
This is the core legal principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن كان يرجى وجوده في المدة لا مع بقاء النكاح فإنه يكون مولياً أيضاً مثل أن يقول والله لا أقربك حتى تموتي أو أقتل أو حتى أطلقك ثلاثاً فإنه يكون مولياً إجماعاً. وكذا إذا كانت أمة فقال لا أقربك حتى أملكك أو أملك شقصاً منك يكون مولياً. وإن قال حتى أشتريك لا يكون مولياً لأنه قد يشتريها لغيره ولا يفسد النكاح. ولو قال حتى أشتريك لنفسي لا يكون مولياً أيضاً لأنه ربما يشتريها لنفسه شراء فاسداً. ولو قال اشتريتك لنفسي وأقبضك كان مولياً.
And if it is expected to occur in the period but not with the continuation of the marriage, then he is also an muli, like if he says, 'By Allah, I will not approach you until you die or I am killed or until I divorce you three times,' then he is an muli by consensus. Likewise, if she is a slave and he says, 'I will not approach you until I own you or own a share of you,' he is an muli. And if he says, 'until I buy you,' he is not an muli because he may buy her for someone else and the marriage is not invalidated. And if he says, 'until I buy you for myself,' he is also not an muli because he might buy her for himself with an invalid purchase. And if he says, 'I have bought you for myself and taken possession,' he is an muli.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof stating that if the condition or event will end the marriage (like death or triple divorce), then the oath is Ila' by consensus (ijma). The example of a slave woman: if he swears not to approach until he owns her (or a share), it is Ila' because when he owns her, the marriage ends (she becomes free and marriage is dissolved). But if he says 'until I buy you,' it is not Ila' because he might buy her for another person, and the marriage would not be affected. Even if he says 'for myself,' it could be an invalid purchase, which does not dissolve the marriage. Only if he says 'I have bought you for myself and taken possession' (implying a valid purchase that actually occurs) does it become Ila'. This shows the principle: the condition must certainly lead to the end of the marriage within the period; uncertainty prevents Ila'.

Key Terms
إجماع (ijmā‘): Consensus of the Companions and scholars; here, the ruling is unanimous.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the statement 'until I buy you' not considered Ila' when said to a slave wife?
A) Because buying does not end the marriage
B) Because he might buy her for someone else
C) Because he might buy her with an invalid contract
D) Both B and C

Key Takeaway
Ila' is only valid when the condition upon which the oath is based will certainly end the marriage.
